--- DAILY NOTES (1A_MWF 10-1 Day 1.pdf) ---
THE COLLEGE PANDA

Chapter 10: Systems of Equations

CHAPTER EXERCISE:

1. [B] Substituting the second equation into the first,
3(1 - 3y) - 5y = -11
3 - 9y - 5y = -11
3 - 14y = -11
-14y = -14
y = 1

Finally, x = 1 - 3(1) = -2.

2. [D] From the first equation, y = 20 - 2x. Plugging this into the second equation,
6x - 5(20 - 2x) = 12
6x - 100 + 10x = 12
16x = 112
x = 7

We already know the answer is (D) at this point, but just in case, y = 20 - 2(7) = 6.

3. [B] Add the two equations to get 7x - 7y = 35. Dividing both sides by 7, x - y = 5. We can multiply both sides by -1 to get y - x = -5.

4. [C] The fastest way to do this problem is to subtract the second equation from the first, which yields x + y = 9.

5. [C] In the first equation, we can move 3x to the right hand side to get y = -5x + 8. Substituting this into the second equation,
-3x + 2(-5x + 8) = -10
-3x - 10x + 16 = -10
-13x = -26
x = 2

Then, y = -5(2) + 8 = -2. Finally, xy = (2)(-2) = -4.

275


CHAPTER 29 ANSWERS TO THE EXERCISES

6. [C] If the two lines intersect at the point (2, 8), then (2, 8) is a solution to the system. Plugging the point into the equation of the second line, we can solve for b,
y = -bx
8 = -b(2)
-4 = b

Plugging the point into the equation of the first line,
y = ax + b
8 = a(2) - 4
12 = 2a
6 = a

7. [A] The two graphs do not intersect at all, so there are no solutions.

8. [B] From the first equation, we can isolate y to get y = -5x - 2. Substituting this into the second equation,
2(2x - 1) = 3 - 3(-5x - 2)
4x - 2 = 3 + 15x + 6
4x - 2 = 15x + 9
-11 = 11x
-1 = x

Finally, y = -5(-1) - 2 = 3.

9. [B] Divide the first equation by 2 to get x - 2y = 4. We can't get the coefficients to match (-2 vs. 2 for the y's). Therefore, the system has one solution. In fact, we can even solve this system by adding the two equations to get 2x = 8, x = 4, which makes y = 0.

10. [C] To get the same coefficients, multiply the first equation by -2 to get -4x + 10y = -2a. Now we can see that -2a = -8, a = 4.

11. [A] Multiply the first equation by -3 to get -3ax - 6y = -15. The constant a cannot be -1. Otherwise, the second equation's coefficients would then be equal to the first equation's coefficients, resulting in a system with no solution.

12. [B] First, multiply the first equation by 3 to get rid of the fraction: 12x - y = -24. Next, substitute the second equation into the first,
12x - (4x + 16) = -24
12x - 4x - 16 = -24
8x = -8
x = -1

Finally, y = 4(-1) + 16 = 12.

276


THE COLLEGE PANDA

13. [10] We can isolate x in the second equation to get x = y - 18. Substituting this into the first equation,
y = 0.5(y - 18) + 14
y = 0.5y - 9 + 14
0.5y = 5
y = 10

14. [C] To match the coefficients, multiply the first equation by 18 to get 6x - 3y = 72. We can then see that a = 3 if the system is to have no solution.

15. [D] Divide the first equation by 3 to get x - 2y = 5. Divide the second equation by -2 to get x - 2y = 5. They're the same, so there are an infinite number of solutions.

16. [C] For a system to have infinitely many solutions, the equations must essentially be the same. Looking at the constants, we can make them match by multiplying the second equation by 2. The equations then look like this:
mx - 6y = 10
4x - 2ny = 10

Now it's easy to see that m = 4 and 2n = 6, n = 3. Finally, m/n = 4/3.

17. [9] Plugging the first equation into the second,
√4x - (√x + 3) = 3
2√x - √x - 3 = 3
√x = 6
x = 36

Therefore, y = √36 + 3 = 9.

18. [B] Let s, m, and l be the weights of small, medium, and large jars, respectively. Based on the information, we can create the following two equations:
16s = 2m + l
4s + m = l

To get the weight of the large jar in terms of the weight of the small jar, we need to get rid of m, the weight of the medium jar. We could certainly use elimination, but here, we'll use substitution. Isolating m in the second equation, m = l - 4s. Substituting this into the first equation, we get
16s = 2(l - 4s) + l
16s = 2l - 8s + l
24s = 3l
8s = l

Eight small jars are needed to match the weight of one large jar.

277


CHAPTER 29 ANSWERS TO THE EXERCISES

19. [D] Since there were 30 questions, James must have had 30 answers, x + y = 30. The points he earned from correct answers total 5x. The points he lost from incorrect answers total 2y. Therefore, 5x - 2y = 59.

20. [5] Let a and b be the number points you get for hitting regions A and B, respectively. From the information, we can form the following two equations:
a + 2b = 18
2a + b = 21

To solve for b, multiply the first equation by 2 and subtract to get 3b = 15, b = 5.

21. [D] Let r and c be the number of rectangular tables and circular tables, respectively, at the restaurant. Based on the information, we can make the following two equations:
4r + 8c = 144
r + c = 30

To solve for r, multiply the second equation by 8 and subtract to get -4r = -96, r = 24.

22. [8] To find the point(s) where two graphs intersect, solve the system consisting of their equations. In this problem, that system is
y = x² - 7x + 7
y = 2x - 1

Substituting the first equation into the second, we get
x² - 7x + 7 = 2x - 1
x² - 9x + 8 = 0
(x - 1)(x - 8) = 0
x = 1 or 8

So the x-coordinates of the points of intersection are 1 and 8. Since the question already gave us the point (1, 1), p must be equal to 8.

23. [9 or 16] First, add 11 to both sides of the second equation to get y = x + 11. Then substitute this in for y in the first equation:
x² - 2x = x + 11 - 1
x² - 3x - 10 = 0
(x + 2)(x - 5) = 0
x = -2 or 5

When x = -2, y = -2 + 11 = 9. When x = 5, y = 5 + 11 = 16. The solutions to the system are then (-2, 9) and (5, 16). Therefore, the possible values of y are 9 and 16.

### PAGE 2

**The 3 Useless Clauses**

What is absolutely necessary for there to be a complete sentence:
* [x] Subject
* [x] Verb

Ex: Tom plays. (Sentence)  
    (s)  (v)

**Useless Clause 1: Relative Clause**  
*simply a word that can be used to replace a noun.*

This is a clause that begins with a relative pronoun and will often continue until a verb is reached.

There are 5 relative pronouns:
* [x] who
* [x] whom
* [x] whose
* [x] which
* [x] that

*Note: "that" does not use a comma, neither before nor after.*

Ex: Tom plays an instrument [that was given to him by his grandfather].  
                          ^ Rp  \_____________________________/  
                                      Relative Clause  
*(Where Rp points to "instrument")*

**Useless Clause 2: Comma Clauses**

There are 3 types of comma clauses:  
**Type 1: Introductory comma clause (ICC) - dependent clauses**  
Most Introductory comma clauses begin with a subordinating conjunction, a preposition, or an -ing adverb.

*Note: Tom is playing [ is / ~~playing~~ ]*  
*Any word ending in -ing is never a verb.*

---

### PAGE 3

Form: Introductory comma clause, Sentence

(sc)  
[Whereas others are studying for the SAT], Tom is playing.  
\____________________________________/  
      Introductory Comma Clause  

**Type 2: Ending Comma Clause (ECC) - defining or clarifying phrase**  
Most ending comma clauses will begin with an -ing adverb.

Form: Sentence, Ending Comma Clause

Ex: Tom plays football, [spending more than twenty hours per week practicing].  
                       \_____________________________________________/  
                                     Ending Comma Clause  
*(Where -ing adverb points to "spending")*

**Type 3: Middle Comma Clause (MCC) - appositive or non-essential clause**  
This is a clause that disrupts a full sentence. Usually, the first part of the sentence contains the subject and the second part of the sentence contains the verb.

Form: SEN, Middle Comma Clause, TENCE  
       |                          |  
       v (usually the subject     v (usually the verb is  
          of the sentence is         found here)  
          found here)  

*Tricky: a middle comma clause can actually be found in an introductory comma clause.*  
Ex: During the hail storm, the first ever recorded in the city, Manasa hit her head.

Ex: Tom, [usually very uncreative], wrote a beautiful song that was awarded first place in the talent competition.  
          \______________________/  
            Middle Comma Clause  

---

### PAGE 4

*Note: a sentence could contain zero, one, two, or all three comma clauses.*

Ex: [Beyond studying for his academics], Tom, [currently the valedictorian of his high school], enjoys playing frivously [with his many video games], [much to the dislike of his strict parents].
- "Beyond studying for his academics" is labeled as ICC.
- "Tom" is labeled as (S) (Subject).
- "currently the valedictorian of his high school" is labeled as MCC.
- "enjoys" is labeled as (V) (Verb).
- "enjoys playing frivously" is labeled as SENTENCE.
- "much to the dislike of his strict parents" is labeled as ECC.

**Useless Clause 3: prepositional phrase**  
This is a phrase that begins with a preposition and continues until a noun is reached, called the direct object or the object of the preposition.

Ex: Tom plays football [against the wishes] [of his strict parents].  
                       P           N     (P)         (N)  
                       \_______  _______/\________  ________/  
                               PP                 PP  

Consider the following sentence:  
[Gluing] beautiful pieces ~~of artwork~~ ~~that are drawn by classmates~~ is an experience ~~Tom~~ will not forget.  

Result: Gluing is the subject.

*Note: a subject of a sentence can NEVER be found within a relative clause, a comma clause, or a prepositional phrase.*

---

### PAGE 5

Because the above clauses are effectively useless, I want you to get into the habit of crossing them out. To confirm that you have done so correctly, confirm that the balance is still a sentence.

**MATH**  
**DAY #** 1

**TOPICS OF THE DAY**
* linear functions

---

### PAGE 9

**linear functions**  
A linear function is a relationship between x (independent variable) and y (dependent variable) such that for each additional constant (same) change in x, there exists a corresponding constant change in y.

Graph showing a downward sloping line with steps:
- From $(x_0, y_0)$, +2 right, -1 down.
- +2 right, -1 down.
- +4 right, -2 down.

*Note: the ratio of the constant change in y to the constant change in x is called the slope.*

Slope formula: $m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$

Since the slope of a line is a ratio, there are several instances where using the slope as a proportion is effective.

**Instance 1: given three collinear points**  
$m_{12} = m_{13}$

Ex: find a relationship b/t n and m.  
Graph showing a line passing through Pt 1 (0, 0), Pt 2 (n, 4), and Pt 3 (6, m).  
$m_{12} = m_{13}$  
$\frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1} = \frac{y_3 - y_1}{x_3 - x_1}$  
$\frac{4 - 0}{n - 0} = \frac{m - 0}{6 - 0}$  
$\frac{4}{n} = \frac{m}{6}$  
$mn = 24$ or $m = \frac{24}{n}$ or $n = \frac{24}{m}$  

*NOTE: parallel lines are lines that have the same slope.*  
*perpendicular lines are lines whose slopes product to -1 -> the two slopes are negative reciprocals of each other.*

---

### PAGE 10

Ex:  
Graph of perpendicular lines intersecting at (3, s):
Line 1 passes through (1, 4) and (3, s).  
Line 2 passes through (5, 3) and (3, s).  
find s:  
$m_1 = \frac{s - 4}{3 - 1} = \frac{s - 4}{2}$  
$m_2 = \frac{s - 3}{3 - 5} = \frac{s - 3}{-2}$  

$(\frac{s - 4}{2})(\frac{s - 3}{-2}) = -1$  
$\frac{(s - 4)(s - 3)}{-4} = -1$  
$(s - 4)(s - 3) = 4$  

**Instance 2: if a problem demonstrates linear relationships and discusses changes in the dependent and independent variable.**

Ex: The number of ounces of spaghetti sauce needed for a certain number of ounces of spaghetti can be modeled as:  
$ss = \frac{2}{3}(s) + 4$  
where ss is the number of ounces of spaghetti sauce and s is the number of ounces of spaghetti.

If I were to add 15 ounces of spaghetti, then how many additional ounces of sauce would I need?

DV: number of ounces of spaghetti sauce  
IV: number of ounces of spaghetti  
slope: 2/3  

$\frac{2}{3} = \frac{x}{15}$  
$3x = 30$  
$x = 10\text{ oz}$  

---

### PAGE 11

**linear function modelling:**

* Model a horizontal line: <-------> (m = 0)  
  $y = \#$

* Model a vertical line:  
  ^  
  |  
  v (m = undefined)  
  $x = \#$

* Model a diagonal line:  
  There are 3 forms:  
  1. slope-intercept form:  
     $y = mx + b$  
     Best for: graphing a line, analysis of a line.  
  2. point-slope form:  
     $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$  
     Best for modelling.  
  3. Standard form:  
     $Ax + By = C$ (A should normally be +, A, B, C should normally be integers)  
     Best for word problems and for standardized testing.  

*Note: though the point-slope form is best for modeling, the answer is often in slope-intercept form or standard form. However, it is easy to convert from point-slope form to either slope intercept or standard.*

---

### PAGE 12

Point-slope form:  
$5 \cdot (y - 4) = -\frac{2}{5} \cdot 5(x + 1)$  
S1) If the slope is a fraction, multiply all terms by the LCD.  
$5y - 20 = -2(x + 1)$  
S2) distribute the x-side  
$5y - 20 = -2x - 2$  

* To slope-intercept form:  
  S3) Isolate y  
  $5y = -2x + 18$  
  $y = -\frac{2}{5}x + \frac{18}{5}$  

* To standard form:  
  S3) get x and y together  
  $2x + 5y = 18$  
  S4) Confirm conditions for A, B, C.  
  $2x + 5y = 18$  

**full modelling:**  
S1) identify both the dependent variable (y) and the independent variable (x).  
*Note: if you see the phrase "as a function of," then the term before is the DV, while the term after is the IV.*

S2) identify either the slope and one coordinate point or two coordinate points.

S3) if necessary, find the slope:  
$m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$

S4) Using the slope and either coordinate point, model the line in point-slope form:  
$y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$

S5) Convert  

---

### PAGE 13

Ex: In 2012, a scientist recorded 12 foxes and 40 rabbits in a forest. Ten years later, he recorded 20 foxes and 52 rabbits. Assuming a linear relationship, model the number of foxes as a function of the number of rabbits.

DV: the number of foxes  
IV: the number of rabbits  

in 2012: (40, 12) (x1, y1)  
in 2022: (52, 20)  

$m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$  
$m = \frac{20 - 12}{52 - 40}$  
$m = \frac{8}{12}$  
$m = \frac{2}{3}$  

$y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$  
$3 \cdot (f - 12) = \frac{2}{3} \cdot 3(r - 40)$ (point-slope form)  
$3f - 36 = 2(r - 40)$  
$3f - 36 = 2r - 80$  

* Slope-intercept form:  
  $3f = 2r - 44$  
  $f = \frac{2}{3}r - \frac{44}{3}$  

* Standard form:  
  $(-2r + 3f = -44)(-1)$  
  $2r - 3f = 44$  

Ex: In 1954, a can of Coke cost $0.50. By 2012, the same can of Coke would cost $2.00. Assuming linear growth in a can of Coke, model, in all three forms, the cost of a can of Coke as a function of the number of years since 1950.

---

### PAGE 14

**slope interpretation:**  
First, ensure the slope is a fraction (a/b).

**Interpretation:**  
The value of the DV will [ (m=+) increase or (m=-) decrease ] by |a| for each additional b units of the IV.

Ex: In 2012, a scientist recorded 12 foxes and 40 rabbits in a forest. Ten years later, he recorded 20 foxes and 52 rabbits. Assuming a linear relationship, model the number of foxes as a function of the number of rabbits.

DV: the number of foxes  
IV: the number of rabbits  

in 2012: (40, 12) (x1, y1)  
in 2022: (52, 20)  

$m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$  
$m = \frac{20 - 12}{52 - 40}$  
$m = \frac{8}{12}$  
$m = \frac{2}{3}$  

$y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$  
$f - 12 = \frac{2}{3}(r - 40)$ (point-slope form)  

$3(f - 12) = 2(r - 40)$  
$3f - 36 = 2r - 80$  

* Slope-intercept form:  
  $3f = 2r - 44$  
  $f = \frac{2}{3}r - \frac{44}{3}$  

* Standard form:  
  $(-2r + 3f = -44)(-1)$  
  $2r - 3f = 44$  

Interpret the slope (2/3): the number of foxes will increase by 2 for each additional 3 rabbits in the forest.

---

### PAGE 15

The price of a can of Coke will increase by $3 for each additional 116 years after 1950.

--- DAILY NOTES (1A_MWF 10-1 Day 1.pdf) ---
**Page 1**

READING / GRAMMAR
DAY # 3

TOPICS OF THE DAY
* Punctuation

---

**Page 2**

Punctuation

Commas

Use 1: to separate the items of a list
... item 1, item 2, item 3, and item 4...
(comma before "and" is circled and labeled "wrong")

Oxford Comma: the oxford comma is not mandatory in writing. As such, you will not be tested on its use. However, you will be tested on its location - it is located before the "and".

Note: the items of a list can also be separated by the use of a semi-colon(;). You do so if the items of the list themselves contain commas.
(annotation: this is the only exception where a semi-colon can be joined with "and")

Ex: Ruhi plans to read Title 1, description 1; Title 2, description 2; and Title 3, description 3.
* item 1: Title 1, description 1
* item 2: Title 2, description 2
* item 3: Title 3, description 3

Use 2: to join two full sentences with the use of a coordinating conjunction.
∴ two expressed subjects
   two expressed verbs
   two full ideas

form: sentence 1, (cc) sentence 2.

Where your coordinating conjunctions (cc) are your FANBOYS
F - for     B - but
A - and     O - or      S - so
N - nor     Y - yet

Ex: Ashish plans to buy a new car and will have it shipped to him. [✓]
Ashish plans to buy a new car, and he will have it shipped to him. [✓]
(S1 under "Ashish", V1 under "plans")
(S2 under "he", V2 under "will have")

---

**Page 3**

Correctly Joining 2 Independent Clauses:
1. IC. IC
2. IC; IC
3. IC, cc IC
4. IC: IC

Incorrectly Join 2 Independent Clauses:
1. IC IC (no punctuation) (run-on sentence)
2. IC, IC (no coordinating conjunction) (comma splice)
3. IC; cc IC
4. IC, false cc IC

Most common false cc is "however"
[X] Tom is dating Emily, however he is attracted to Julie.
(labeled: cc = false pointing to however)
(labeled "Sentence" under "Tom is dating Emily" and "Sentence" under "he is attracted to Julie")

[✓] Correctly using "however"
1. IC. However, IC  ->  Tom is dating Emily. However, he is attracted to Julie.
2. IC; however, IC
3. IC. IC, however.  ->  Tom is dating Emily. He is attracted to Julie, however.
4. IC; IC, however.
5. IC. IC1/2, however, IC2/2  ->  Tom is dating Emily. He, however, is attracted to Julie.
6. IC; IC1/2, however, IC2/2

Careful: the SAT often tests which sentence the "however" is part of - it needs to be a part of the sentence performing the opposition.

Use 3: to join a dependent clause with an independent clause; however, the use of the comma depends on the location of the dependent clause.

If the dependent clause comes before the independent clause, then you MUST use a comma
If the dependent clause comes after the independent clause, then you MIGHT use a comma. You do so if the dependent clause begins with an adverb of concession.
Your concessional adverbs:

---

**Page 4**

supposing it rains, the party will be cancelled.
The party will be cancelled supposing it rains.
Even though I have no water, Vishesh keeps taking my water bottle.
Viheesh keeps taking my water bottle, even though I have no water.

Use 4: before and after a middle comma clause (also known as an appositive or non-essential clause)

Note: a middle comma clause can actually be punctuated in 3 ways
1. IC1/2, mcc, IC2/2
2. IC1/2 - mcc - IC2/2
3. IC1/2 ( mcc ) IC2/2
no mixing punctuation

Note: the SAT/ACT Trap
Subject, ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~ - Verb ~~~.

Use 5: to enter an ending comma clause (ecc)
Recall: most ending comma clauses begin with an -ing adverb.
Recall: ending comma clauses are usually defining or clarifying phrases.
Ex: Vishesh keeps taking the water bottle, hoping that he will find water.

Use 6: between two adjectives that describe the same noun.
Ex: Ashish is wearing a big, red t-shirt.
Ex: Viraj is wearing a light gray shirt.

---

**Page 5**

Colon (:)
Note: to use a colon, there MUST be a full sentence before the colon.

Use 1: to enter a list with no introductory phrase to the list.
* No for example
* No such as
* No to illustrate     } No introductory phrase
* No including
* No with

Ex: Aashni eats a big breakfast: waffles, pancakes, cereal, and fruit.
Note: do not ever enter a list with a semicolon.
Note: you can enter a list with no punctuation
Ex: Aashni eats waffles, pancakes, cereal, and fruit every morning.
Note: you can use an introductory phrase as long as you don't enter the list with a colon.
Ex: Aashni eats a big breakfast including pancakes, cereal, waffles, and fruit.

Use 2: to add information at the end of a sentence about the sentence.
Ex: Ashish studied eight long years for the SAT: he scored a perfect 1600.

DASH (-)

Use 1: before and after a MCC

Use 2: to add information at the end of a sentence about the sentence; however, this information is usually shocking.
Ex: Ashish studied eight long years for the SAT - he scored a 420.

---

**Page 6**

Semi-colon (;)

Use 1: to separate the items of a list when the items themselves contain commas.
Use 2: to join two full sentences without the use of a cc.

Apostrophe (')

Use 1: to make a contraction, the joining of two words into one.
CONTRACTIONS (crossed out)
the only acceptable contraction is "it is" = "it's"

Use 2: to show possession
Note: there are non-apostrophe possessives
* its
* their
* his
* her
* hers
* my
* mine
* our
* ours

its', its's, itss (crossed out and labeled "Not words")

to show possessive with an apostrophe:
* if the word does not end in -s: 's
* if the word ends in s:
  * simple noun: s' (examples: cars', car's)
  * proper noun: s's (example: Jesus's)

**Page 7**

MATH
DAY # 2

TOPICS OF THE DAY
* Quadratics

---

**Page 8**

Quadratics

a quadratic is a relationship between y and the square of x. (x^2)
If the quadratic passes through the origin, then y ∝ x^2.
∴ if x is doubled, y is quadrupled
if x is tripled, y is scaled by factor of 9.
if y is doubled, x is scaled by √2

all quadratics produce a curve called a parabola, a U shaped curve that opens either up or down.
* opens up (a > 0)
* opens down (a < 0)

all parabolashave two characteristics:
* a vertex AND
* an axis of symmetry

Vertex: this is a coordinate point (x_v, y_v) that corresponds to either the maximum point of the parabola (if parabola opens down) or the minimum point of the parabola (if parabola opens up)
* Vertex (minimum point)
* Vertex (maximum point)

Note: for any question involving a quadratic that refers to either maximum or minimum, simply find the vertex!!

---

**Page 9**

Axis of Symmetry: this is an imaginary vertical line that passes through the parabola's vertex, dividing the parabola into two equal halves
Aos

all quadratics can be expressed in one of three forms
1. Standard form: y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c (points to y-intercept)
2. Vertex form: y = f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k (opp under h, norm under k, points to vertex (h, k))
3. Intercept form: y = f(x) = a(x - p)(x - q) (opp under p, opp under q, points to x-intercepts (p, 0) (q, 0))

SAT: each form gives, as a constant or a coefficient, the __________ of the parabola.

finding the vertex:
* Vertex form: y = f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k (opp under h, Normal under k)
  Vertex: x_v = h
  y_v = k
  V(h, k)

* Standard form: y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c
  Vertex: x_v = -b / 2a
  y_v = f(-b / 2a)
  V(-b / 2a, f(-b / 2a))

* Intercept form: y = f(x) = a(x - p)(x - q) (opp under p, opp under q)
  Vertex: x_v = (p + q) / 2
  y_v = f((p + q) / 2)
  V((p + q) / 2, f((p + q) / 2))

finding the Axis of Symmetry:
x = x_v

Ex: y = f(x) = -2(x + 3)(x - 7)
x_v = (-3 + 7) / 2 = 2
y_v = f(2) = -2(5)(-5) = 50
V: (2, 50)

---

**Page 10**

CONVERSION TOOLBOX

Standard -> Vertex:
Complete the square
* if a=1:
  y = x^2 + 12x - 8  (1) group the x's)
  y = (x^2 + 12x) - 8  (2) factor the 'a')
  y = 1(x^2 + 12x) - 8  (3) add ___ inside and balance outside; ___ = (1/2 x coeff)^2)
  y = 1(x^2 + 12x + 36) - 8 - 1(36)
  y = 1(x + 6)^2 - 44  (4) factor the group into ( )^2)
  y = (x + 6)^2 - 44

* if a ≠ 1:
  y = 3x^2 + 10x - 8
  y = (3x^2 + 10x) - 8
  y = 3(x^2 + 10/3 x) - 8
  y = 3(x^2 + 10/3 x + 25/9) - 8 - 3(25/9)
  y = 3(x + 5/3)^2 - 8 - 25/3
  y = 3(x + 5/3)^2 - 49/3

  y = -x^2 + 5x - 2
  y = -(x^2 - 5x) - 2
  y = -1(x^2 - 5x + 25/4) - 2 + 1(25/4)
  y = -(x - 5/2)^2 - 2 + 25/4
  y = -(x - 5/2)^2 + 17/4

Ex: y = 3x^2 - 6x + 1

Vertex -> Standard:
FOIL
y = 3(x + 4)^2 - 8
y = 3(x^2 + 8x + 16) - 8
y = 3x^2 + 24x + 48 - 8
y = 3x^2 + 24x + 40

Standard -> Intercept:
factor, if possible
(if b^2 - 4ac = discriminant is either 0 or a perfect square, then the quadratic is factorable)
Ex: 6x^2 + 7x - 49
(2x + 7)(3x - 7)
= 6(x + 7/2)(x - 7/3)

Intercept -> Standard:
FOIL
y = 2(x + 1)(x - 3/2)
y = 2(x^2 - 1/2 x - 3/2)
y = 2x^2 - x - 3

Flowcharts:
VERTEX -> Foil -> Standard -> FACTOR -> INTERCEPT
Intercept -> Foil -> Standard -> CTS -> Vertex

--- Page 30 ---

Proportions :

a proportion is when two ratios are equivalent under different circumstances.

           Ratio 1        Ratio 2
Quantity 1 / Quantity 2 = [ ___________ ] = [ ___________ ]
                          [ Circumstance 1 ]   [ Circumstance 2 ]

Ex: Manasa is throwing a party, inviting f friends
and ordering p slices of pizza to feed them. However,
Avaneesh and Abi show up uninvited. Manasa, being
the gracious host she is, will order more pizza so as to
feed everyone at the same original rate. How many
additional slices should she order?

"same" (boxed)
"rate" (boxed)
Note under "rate.": proportion:

friends / pizza = f / p (Invited) = 2 / x (Uninvited) or f+2 / x (Uninvited)

Equations:
fx = 2p
x = 2p / f

f / p = (f+2) / x
fx = p(f+2)
x = p(f+2) / f

p(f+2) / f - pf / f
= (pf + 2p - pf) / f
= 2p / f

--- Page 31 ---

In Question 15:
"can travel an average of 25 miles per gallon of gasoline." (underlined)
"how many fewer average miles, m," (underlined)

Handwritten work:
miles / $ = 25/4 (Current)
m / s = m / 5 (Save)
4m = 25(5)
4/25 m = 5

Option C (checked)
Option D (circled)
Option A (checked but crossed out)

--- Page 32 ---

Lines and Angles :

Angle Pairs : this defines relationships between two angles

Vertical Angle Pairs : this is a set of two angles
that share a common vertex and whose two rays
extend in opposite directions

∠ DVP and ∠ AVC form a vertical angle pair.
[check] all vertical angle pairs form congruent angles.
∴ ∠ DVP ≅ ∠ AVC
[check] all vertical angle pairs form an adjacent vertical angle pair. (Note: "adjacent" is crossed out)
∴ ∠ DVC forms a vertical angle pair with ∠ PVA

Complementary Angle Pair : this is a set of 2 angles whose measures sum to 90°.
Note: there need not be any relationship b/t the angles besides their measures summing to 90°.

Angle | Complement
10° | 80°
42° | 48°
1° | 89°
x° | (90 - x)° (boxed)

Supplementary Angle Pair : this is a set of two angles whose measures sum to 180°. Again, there need not be any relationship b/t the two angles besides their measures summing to 180°.

Angle | Supplement
x° | (180 - x)°

Linear Pair : this is a set of two angles that share a common vertex and one common ray, and whose non common rays extend in opposite directions.

∠ APB and ∠ BPR form a linear pair.
(Diagram shows angle APB as x° and angle BPR as 180-x° on a straight line)
[check] all linear pairs are a supplementary pair.

--- Page 33 ---

Angle Pairs formed by a transversal cutting through two parallel lines

transversal
line m
line n

this cut creates eight angles, four at each cut

It is our goal to compare two angles, one at each cut.

Congruent Angle Pairs
1. Corresponding Angle Pairs - this is a set of two angles that lie in the same relative position in two locations.
[ ∠1, ∠5 ]
[ ∠2, ∠6 ]
[ ∠3, ∠7 ]
[ ∠4, ∠8 ]

2. Alternate Interior Angle Pair - this is a set of two angles that lie on the interior of the two parallel, but on opposite sides of the transversal.
[ ∠4, ∠6 ]
[ ∠3, ∠5 ]

3. Alternate Exterior Angle Pair - this is a set of 2 angles that live on the exterior of the 2 parallel, but on opposite sides of the transversal.
[ ∠1, ∠7 ]
[ ∠2, ∠8 ]

Supplementary Angle Pairs
1. Same-Side Interior Angle Pair : this is a set of two angles that lie on the interior of the two parallel lines, but on the same side of the transversal.
[ ∠4, ∠5 ]
[ ∠3, ∠6 ]

2. Same-side Exterior Angle Pair : this is a set of two angles that lie on the exterior of the two parallel lines, but on the same side of the transversal.
[ ∠1, ∠8 ]
[ ∠2, ∠7 ]

[purple angle] + [green angle] = 180°

--- Page 34 ---

Ex:
(Diagram of parallel lines m and n intersected by a triangle and transversals)
Left side: 130°, 50°
Center (triangle vertices): m, 10°, 120°, 60° = x° (boxed), 170°, 10°
Right side: 130°, 170°, 10°, 10°
Bottom intersection: 170°, 10°, n
(Below is a V-shape between parallel lines with angles 120°, x°, 130°)

**Shlok Bhatt**: Hello everyone,

ATL Cyber Clash (CTF) will take place on April 18, Saturday, from 10:30 AM to 2:00 PM at Sharon Forks Library. I’m co-hosting this event in collaboration with the LHS Cybersecurity Club and Alpharetta High School.

This is a Capture the Flag cybersecurity competition where you will work in teams of two to solve challenges in areas like web exploitation, cryptography, and forensics. The competition is designed to be beginner-friendly, so no prior experience is required.

The event is open to all high school students, and there will be cash prizes for top teams. Registration is open until April 17.

You can sign up here: https://luma.com/vqckv6aj

Join us—whether you’re new to cybersecurity or already experienced, this is a great chance to learn, challenge yourself, and compete!!

**Shlok Bhatt**: Hello everyone,

ATL Cyber Clash (CTF) will take place on April 18, Saturday, from 10:30 AM to 2:00 PM at Sharon Forks Library. I’m co-hosting this event in collaboration with the LHS Cybersecurity Club and Alpharetta High School.

This is a Capture the Flag cybersecurity competition where you will work in teams of two to solve challenges in areas like web exploitation, cryptography, and forensics. The competition is designed to be beginner-friendly, so no prior experience is required.

The event is open to all high school students, and there will be cash prizes for top teams. Registration is open until April 17.

You can sign up here: https://luma.com/vqckv6aj

Join us—whether you’re new to cybersecurity or already experienced, this is a great chance to learn, challenge yourself, and compete!!

--- DAILY NOTES (9A_MWF 10-1 Day 9.pdf) ---
READING / GRAMMAR
DAY # 9

* Grammar Review
* Act Grammar

---

MATH
DAY # 9

TOPICS OF THE DAY
* Triangles, Part 2

---

### Trigonometry
the study of the ratios of two sides of a right triangle as a function of one of the right triangle's acute angles.

#### Triangle Diagram
* Vertices: $A$ (top), $B$ (bottom-left, right angle), $C$ (bottom-right)
* Angle at $A$: $x^\circ$ [labeled as "A (acute angle)"]
* Angle at $C$: $(90-x)^\circ$ [labeled as "C (acute angle)"]
* Side $AB$: labeled "adjacent" (in relation to $A$) / "opposite" (in relation to $C$)
* Side $BC$: labeled "opposite" (in relation to $A$) / "adjacent" (in relation to $C$)
* Side $AC$: labeled "Hypotenuse" / "Hypotenuse"

trig function (acute angle) = ratio
there are six trigonometric functions

| Core 3 | Reciprocal 3 |
| :--- | :--- |
| $\sin(AA) = \text{opp}/\text{hyp}$ | $\csc(AA) = \frac{1}{\sin(AA)} = \text{hyp}/\text{opp}$ |
| $\cos(AA) = \text{adj}/\text{hyp}$ | $\sec(AA) = \frac{1}{\cos(AA)} = \text{hyp}/\text{adj}$ |
| $\tan(AA) = \text{opp}/\text{adj}$ | $\cot(AA) = \frac{1}{\tan(AA)} = \text{adj}/\text{opp}$ |

#### Example:
$\text{Ex: } \tan(A) = 4/7 = \text{opp}/\text{adj}$
* Right triangle with vertex $A$ at the top, right angle $B$ at bottom-left, vertex $C$ at bottom-right.
* Side $AB$ (adjacent) = $7\text{m}$
* Side $BC$ (opposite) = $4\text{m}$
* Side $AC$ (hypotenuse) = $\text{Hyp} = \text{m}\sqrt{65}$

$$\text{Hyp}^2 = (7\text{m})^2 + (4\text{m})^2$$
$$\text{Hyp}^2 = 49\text{m}^2 + 16\text{m}^2$$
$$\text{Hyp}^2 = 65\text{m}^2$$
$$\text{Hyp} = \text{m}\sqrt{65}$$

#### SAT Focus
**What is the relationship between sine and co-sine?**
They are co-functions, meaning that they produce equivalent ratios (meaning they are equal) when evaluated at complementary angles.
$$\therefore \sin(x^\circ) = \cos(90-x^\circ) \quad \text{OR}$$
$$\sin(A) = \cos(B) \text{ iff } A+B = 90^\circ$$

$\text{Ex: } \cos(3x+10) = \sin(5x-20)$
$$\therefore (3x+10) + (5x-20) = 90$$
$$8x - 10 = 90$$
$$8x = 100$$
$$x = 100/8 = 25/2 \text{ or } 12.5$$

$\text{Ex: } \cos(37^\circ) = \sin(?)$
$$? = 53^\circ$$

---

#### ACT Focus:

##### Using trigonometry to find a missing side
* $\text{\checked}$ the triangle must be right
* $\text{\checked}$ Given only one side and effectively both acute angles

* **Step 1:** choose an acute angle
* **Step 2:** next step - label all three sides in relation to your chosen angle
* **Step 3:** based on the labeling of the given side and the missing side, choose a trig function.
* **Step 4:** setup the trig equation
  $$\text{trig}(AA) = \frac{x}{V} \quad \text{OR} \quad \text{trig}(AA) = \frac{V}{x}$$
  $$\rightarrow x = V \cdot \text{trig}(AA) \quad \text{or} \quad x = \frac{V}{\text{trig}(AA)}$$

##### Using trigonometry to find a missing angle
* $\text{\checked}$ again, the triangle must be right
* $\text{\checked}$ Given at least two sides

* **Step 1:** based on the angle you wish to find, label all three sides
* **Step 2:** based on the labeling of the two sides, choose a trig function
* **Step 3:** Setup formula
  $$\text{trig}(x) = \text{ratio}$$
  $$x = \text{trig}^{-1}(\text{ratio})$$

---

### Isosceles Triangles
Any triangle that has two sides being congruent, called **legs**, **AND** also two angles being congruent, called **base angles**. The non-congruent side is called the **base**. The non-congruent angle is called the **vertex angle**.

#### Diagram
* Top angle: $Vt$ (vertex angle)
* Left and right sides: labeled "leg" (with single congruence tick marks)
* Bottom-left and bottom-right angles: labeled $BA$ (base angles, with double congruence curves)
* Bottom side: labeled "base" (with double congruence tick marks)

---

### Equilateral Triangle:
a triangle in which all sides are $\cong$ and all angles are $\cong$, each measuring $60^\circ$.

#### Diagram
* Three sides labeled $s$ with single congruence tick marks.
* Bottom-left and bottom-right angles labeled $60^\circ$.
* Altitude splits the top vertex into two $30^\circ$ angles, creating two right triangles at the base.
* Altitude length: $\frac{s\sqrt{3}}{2}$
* Base split into two segments of length: $\frac{s}{2}$

---

### Area of Triangles:
Generally: $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$

$$A_{\text{eq}\triangle} = \frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$$

$A_{\text{ACT}} \begin{cases} 
\text{1. } A_{\text{SSS}} = \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)} \quad \text{[Heron's Formula]} \\ 
\quad s = \text{semi-perimeter} = \frac{1}{2}(a+b+c) \\
\quad \text{[Diagram of a triangle with side lengths } a, b, c \text{]} \\ \\
\text{2. } A_{\text{SAS}} = \frac{1}{2}s_1s_2\sin(\theta) \\
\quad \text{[Diagram of a triangle with side lengths } s_1, s_2 \text{ and included angle } \theta \text{]}
\end{cases}$

---

### Similar Triangles:
Two triangles are similar ($\sim$) iff all three corresponding angles are $\cong$ and all three corresponding sides are proportional.

#### Diagram
* Large triangle $ABC$ and small triangle $XYZ$ with $\sim$ between them.

$$\triangle ABC \sim \triangle XYZ$$
$$\therefore \angle A \cong \angle X$$
$$\angle B \cong \angle Y$$
$$\angle C \cong \angle Z$$

$$\therefore \frac{\text{shortest side of } \triangle 1}{\text{shortest side of } \triangle 2} = \frac{\text{middle side of } \triangle 1}{\text{middle side of } \triangle 2} = \frac{\text{largest side of } \triangle 1}{\text{largest side of } \triangle 2}$$

To prove two triangles are similar, you can use the:
* **Angle-Angle Postulate:** if two angles of one triangle are congruent to two angles of another $\triangle$, then the two $\triangle$s are similar.
* **OR SSS**
* **OR SAS**

---

#### Special Similar Triangles
1. **Back to Back + Parallel**
   * [Diagram of two triangles sharing a vertex (hourglass shape) with parallel top and bottom lines]
2. **Intermeshed + Parallel**
   * [Diagram of a nested smaller triangle inside a larger triangle sharing the top vertex, with parallel bases]

---

3. **Triple Right $\triangle$**
   * [Diagram of a right triangle with an altitude $c$ perpendicular to the hypotenuse, dividing it into segments $a$ and $b$]
   $$\frac{a}{c} = \frac{c}{b}$$
   * [Diagram of the three constituent right triangles (small, medium, and large) separated and oriented similarly, with corresponding angles color-coded in orange, red, and purple to illustrate similarity]

#### Proving $\triangle$'s Congruent:
1. SSS
2. SAS
3. ASA
4. AAS
5. HL

Page 30:
Proportions:
a proportion is when two ratios are equivalent under
different circumstances.

              Ratio 1       Ratio 2
Quantity 1   [       ]     [       ]
---------- = [-------]  =  [-------]
Quantity 2   [       ]     [       ]
           Circumstance 1 Circumstance 2

Ex: Manasa is throwing a party, inviting her f friends
and ordering p slices of pizza to feed them. However,
Avaneesh and Abi show up uninvited. Manasa, being
the gracious host she is, will order more pizza so as to
feed everyone at the same [proportion:] original [rate.]
How many additional slices should she order?

friends   [   f   ]     [ 2  f+2 ]
------- = [-------]  =  [-------]
 pizza    [   p   ]     [ x   x  ]
           Invited       Uninvited

fx = 2p
x = 2p/f

f/p = (f+2)/x
fx = p(f+2)
x = p(f+2)/f

(p(f+2))/f - pf/f = (pf + 2p - pf)/f = 2p/f

Page 31:
- miles / $ =
  - Current: Yellow box with 25 on top and 4 on bottom (replacing crossed-out 100 and 16)
  - Save: Red box with m on top and 5 on bottom
- 4m = 25(5)
- 4/25 m = 5
- Option D (circled)

Page 32:
Lines and Angles:
Angle Pairs: this defines relationships between two angles

Vertical Angle Pairs: this is a set of two angles that share a common vertex and whose two rays extend in opposite directions

Complementary Angle Pair: this is a set of 2 angles whose measures sum to 90°.
Note: there need not be any relationship b/t the angles besides their measures summing to 90°.
Angle | Complement
10°   | 80°
42°   | 48°
1°    | 89°
x°    | (90-x)°

Vertical Angle Pair:
<DVP and <AVC form a vertical angle pair.
[Diagram showing intersecting lines forming vertical angles, labeled with rays VC, VD, VP, VA and vertex V]
[checkmark] all vertical angle pairs form congruent angles.
:. <DVP ~= <AVC
[checkmark] all vertical angle pairs form an adjacent
:. <DVC forms a vertical angle pair with <PVA

Linear Pair: this is a set of two angles that share a common vertex and one common ray, and whose non common rays extend in opposite directions.
<APB and <BPR form a linear pair.
[Diagram showing a straight line AR with ray PB from vertex P, angles labeled x° and (180-x)°]
[checkmark] all linear pairs are a supplementary pair.

Supplementary Angle Pair: this is a set of two angles whose measures sum to 180°. Again, there need not be any relationship b/t the two angles besides their measures summing to 180°.
Angle | Supplement
x°    | (180-x)°

Page 33:
- 2. Same-Side Exterior Angle Pair: this is a set of two angles that lie on the exterior of the two parallel lines, but on the same side of the transversal.
  - Box containing:
    <1 , <8
    <2 , <7
- purple dot + green dot = 180°

**Answer Questions**

* **step 1:** Answer the question yourself - yes, I want you to write your own answer to the question. Your response should be generated using evidence or context from the actual text.
* **step 2:** begin to proceed through the answer choices with the goal of eliminating at least two answer choices, called dummy answer choices. What makes an answer choice dummy?
  * [Checked Box] it provides a viewpoint that is not inherently discussed in the text.
  * [Checked Box] it provides a viewpoint that opposes the view of the author
  * [Checked Box] the response is significantly different than what you expressed
  * [Checked Box] the response is too extremely harsh.
  * [Checked Box] the response is too absolute in nature
    * Only
    * always
    * never
* **step 3:** if two choices remain, then find the wrong answer
  * every wrong answer has something wrong with it, even if it is one word wrong.
  * *let us assume that you identify A-C as dummy responses.* (in red)
  * *This does not ensure that D is correct blindly.* (in red)

---

**Evidence Analysis:**
You are effectively tasked to provide evidence from either the text or some graphic to support some claim.

* **Step 1:** highlight/underline the actual claim that you wish to find evidence for. This claim can be within the text or the question header.
* **Step 2:** effectively writing your own response.
  * ask yourself what evidence you would like to see that would support this claim.
  * then find this evidence in the text or the graphic
* **Step 3:** proceed through answer choices.

**Day 6 - Math**

* Factor of Change
* Ratios, Rates, and Proportions

---

**Factor of Change**

if given how certain quantities change by a certain percent or by a certain factor **AND** asked how a related quantity will thus change, then you can apply the factor of change approach

* **Step 1:** start with a formula (given or known) $\quad [A = \pi r^2]$
* **Step 2:** eliminate all constants $\quad [A \sim r^2]$
* **Step 3:** replace each variable with its factor of change $\quad [A \sim (1.1)^2]$
* **Step 4:** solve the unknown variable $\quad [A \sim 1.21]$
* **Step 5:** find how far from 1 and convert to a percent
  * **Note:** if larger than 1, an increase by the %
  * if smaller than 1, a decrease by the %

**Ex:** If an object's mass is decreased by 20% and its speed increased by 20%, then by what % does its kinetic energy change? **Note:** $k = \frac{1}{2}mv^2$
$$k = \frac{1}{2}mv^2$$
$$k \sim mv^2$$
$$k \sim (0.8)(1.2)^2$$
$$k \sim 1.152$$
$$\therefore \text{kinetic energy is increased by } 15.2\%$$

$$A = \frac{\pi r^3 c^2}{2\sqrt{d}} \quad \Bigg| \quad \begin{aligned} &\text{if } c \text{ is held constant, then} \\ &\text{by what } \% \text{ will } A \text{ change if} \\ &r \text{ is inc. by } 25\% \text{ and } d \text{ is} \\ &\text{inc. by } 10\%. \end{aligned}$$

---

**Ratios:**

a ratio is a comparison of two or more quantities by thier lowest corresponding parts $\to$ this means, ratios are always simplified.

This creates a problem $\to$ ratios do not give exact values

**Ex:** if the ratio of boys to girls is $2:3$, then:
* a) how many boys are there? IDK
* b) how many girls are there? IDK
* c) has many total people? IDK
* d) How many more girls than boys? IDK.

the solution to this problem: each part must be a multiple ($m$) of its corresponding value.

**Ex:** if the ratio of boys to girls is $2:3$, then:
* a) how many boys are there? $\cancel{\text{IDK}} \quad 2m$
* b) how many girls are there? $\cancel{\text{IDK}} \quad 3m$
* c) has many total people? $\cancel{\text{IDK}} \quad 5m$
* d) How many more girls than boys? $\cancel{\text{IDK}} \quad m$

**Ex:** If a rectangle's width and length are in a $5:2$ ratio and it has an area of 200, then what is its perimeter?
$$W = 5m$$
$$L = 2m$$
$$\begin{aligned} &A = 10m^2 &&\therefore 10m^2 = 200 \\ &P = 14m &&\quad\ \ m^2 = 20 \\ &&&\quad\ \ m = 2\sqrt{5} \end{aligned}$$
$$\therefore P = 28\sqrt{5}$$

---

**Rates**

a rate is a special ratio that defines some sort of quantity vs some amount of consumption.

$$\text{Formula: } \overset{\text{Q}}{\text{Quantity}} = \overset{\text{value}}{\text{Rate}} (\overset{\text{C}}{\text{Consumption}})$$
$$35\text{ mph} \to M = (35)(H)$$
$$\$3/\text{gal} \to C = (3)(g)$$

a special rate is called speed, which compares the quantity of distance to the consumption of time.
$$\therefore D = (\text{Speed})(T)$$

$$\text{average speed }(\bar{S}) = \frac{\text{total distance}}{\text{total time}}$$

**Note:** if a problem involves two or more speeds, then create the following chart:

| | Trip 1 at speed 1 | Trip 2 at speed 2 |
|---|---|---|
| D | | |
| S | | |
| T | | |

* **Annotations beside table:**
  * `I will allow a variable for one of these` (pointing to rows D and S)
  * $T = \frac{D}{S}$ (pointing to row T)

**Ex:** If Ashish drives to AHA at an avg. speed of 40mph and returns home, along the same route, at an avg. speed of 60mph, then what is his avg. speed for the whole trip?

| | Trip 1 (to AHA at 40mph) | Trip 2 (to home at 60mph) |
|---|---|---|
| D | x | x |
| S | 40 | 60 |
| T | $\frac{x}{40}$ | $\frac{x}{60}$ |

$$\bar{S} = \frac{\text{total d}}{\text{total t}} = \frac{x + x}{\frac{x}{40} + \frac{x}{60}} = \frac{2x \cdot 120}{\left(\frac{x}{40} + \frac{x}{60}\right) \cdot 120} = \frac{240x}{3x + 2x} = \frac{240\cancel{x}}{5\cancel{x}} = 48\text{mph}$$
$$\text{Boxed answer: } = 48\text{mph}$$

--- DAILY NOTES (5A_MWF 10-1 Day 5.pdf) ---
### Page 1

**READING / GRAMMAR**  
**DAY #** 5 *(handwritten)*

**TOPICS OF THE DAY**
* Verb Tenses *(handwritten)*
* Reading *(handwritten)*
* 

---

### Page 2

**Verb Tenses**

| Definition | Intermediate Helping Verb | Special Note |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Simple Present** *(vertical label)* <br><br> this refers to action that is occurring in the moment now **without** any reference to any other time period. | *(diagonal line crossing the cell)* | Multiple actions, though not all in the present, can be grouped into a single one time present action. <br><br> Ex: Manasa is attending SAT class today. She will also attend tomorrow and for the next six weeks. <br><br> ↓ <br><br> Manasa is attending SAT class for the next six weeks. |
| **Simple Past** *(vertical label)* <br><br> this refers to a single one time past action **without** reference to any other time period. | *(diagonal line crossing the cell)* | Multiple one time past actions can be grouped into a single one time past action. <br><br> Ex: Michael Jordan won the NBA championship in the '93-'94 season and won again in the '94-'95 season. <br><br> ↓ <br><br> Michael Jordan won two NBA championships, in the '93-'94 season and in the '94-'95 season. |
| **"Future"** *(vertical label)* <br><br> this, I will refer to as action that has not occurred. | will ───> <br> would ───> <br> could ───> <br><br> would have <br> could have | ───> Ashish **will buy** a Porsche <br> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <sub>H</sub> <sub>V</sub> <br> ───> Ashish **would buy** a Porsche if... <br> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <sub>H</sub> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <sub>V</sub> <br> ───> Ashish **could buy** a Porsche <br> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <sub>H</sub> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <sub>V</sub> |

---

### Page 3

| Definition | Intermediate Helping Verb | Special Note |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Past Perfect** *(vertical label)* <br><br> this refers to action that started in the past, occurred continuously in the past, and stopped in the past. | had (singular and plural) <br><br> Ex: It **had** **rained** for seven consecutive days. <br> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <sup>H</sup> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <sup>V</sup> <br><br> Ex: They had dated. | to use either of the perfect cases, there needs to be some word that references continuity in action. This can include phrases such as 'since,' 'until,' 'continuously,' 'consecutive,' etc... <br> *(arrow pointing from 'continuously' to the "Special Note" box below)* |
| **Present Perfect** *(vertical label)* <br><br> this refers to action that started in the past, occurred continuously in the past, and is still happening today. | has (singular) <br> have (plural) <br><br> It **has** **rained** since March. <br> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <sup>H</sup> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <sup>V</sup> <br> They **have** dated since high school. | Also, if you see a continuity in action words, it can override any other choice of tense. |

### Page 7

**READING vs READING**
* *(blue arrow)* kiddygarten reading - you simply follow each word but do not make connections between words.
* *(red arrow)* you read so well that you make connections between words, make connections between sentences and even foreshadow events.

To do this proper reading approach, you **MUST** *(underlined)* annotate the passage (SAT only); however, I do not care what **YOU** *(underlined)* think is important.

**Step 1:** highlight or circle any and all transitional words/phrases and any and all transitional punctuation.

| Transitional Words/Phrases | Transitional Punctuation |
| --- | --- |
| **\*** but <br> and (if it acts as a coordinating conjunction) <br> however &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; furthermore &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; for example &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; although &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ... <br> yet &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; if &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; in short &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; though <br> moreover &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; including &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; nevertheless &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; even still <br> *(handwritten: continuously)* | : <br> ; <br> — <br> ... <br> ( ) <br> *(arrow from "punctuation." pointing to "( )")* |

**Step 2:** focus on fully understanding the part after the transition — use it to foreshadow the events of the passage. **Write down your foreshadow.**

**Step 3:** as you continue to read **(every single word)**, confirm or deny your foreshadow. If confirmed, keep reading. If denied, stop and start over.

### Page 11

What is this **standard** *(underlined)* that I talked about?  
the standard is **always the second item listed** *(underlined)* in a comparison.

Percents are **always multiplied** *(underlined)* by the original value, the whole value, or the standard value.

the only time %s should be added is if the %'s represent different categories of a whole total.

**Ex:** A bag of Skittles contains 25% red, 30% green, 10% orange, 15% purple, and actually 6 yellow skittles. How many red skittles are in the bag?

| Color | % | Amount |
| --- | --- | --- |
| R | 25 | **?** |
| O | 10 | |
| G | 30 | |
| P | 15 | |
| y | **20** | **6** |
| T | 100 | **30** |

*(Handwritten math on the right of the table:)*
$$ \frac{25\%}{?} = \frac{20\%}{6} $$
$$ 150 = 20? $$
$$ \mathbf{? = 7.5} \text{ (boxed)} $$

$$ 0.20(T) = 6 $$
$$ T = 30 $$
$$ R = 0.25(30) $$
$$ \mathbf{= 7.5} \text{ (boxed)} $$

**Ex:** After receiving a **20% discount** *(underlined, arrow pointing to "80% -> 0.80")* and paying **10% sales tax** *(underlined, arrow pointing to "110% -> 1.10")*, Viraj buys the entire store for **S dollars** *(underlined)*. What was the price of the store before any discounts and taxes?

*effectively, we wish to know the original price of the store? (x)* *(handwritten in purple)*

*(Handwritten math below:)*
$$ x(0.80)(1.10) = S $$
$$ x(0.88) = S $$
$$ x = \frac{S}{0.88} \cdot \frac{100}{100} $$
$$ x = \frac{100S}{88} = \mathbf{\frac{25S}{22}} \text{ (boxed)} $$

---

### Page 12

**Finding percents**

there are effectively two types of %s to find.

1. **Basic %**
   $$ \% = \frac{\text{part}}{\text{whole}} \times 100 \quad \text{OR} \quad \% = \frac{\text{part}}{\text{standard}} \times 100 $$ *(boxed)*

2. **% of difference or % of change**
   $$ \% \text{ change} = \frac{\text{New} - \text{old}}{\text{old}} \times 100 \quad \text{or} \quad \% \text{ change} = \frac{\text{New} - \text{standard}}{\text{standard}} \times 100 $$ *(boxed, with "diff" written above "New - old")*

**Ex:** Aashni is 16 years old while Ashish is 42 years old.

| | |
| --- | --- |
| **Q1:** Aashni is what % younger than Ashish? <br><br> $$ \% \text{ younger} = \frac{As - Aa}{As} \times 100 $$ *(with diff written above As - Aa)* <br> $$ = \frac{42 - 16}{42} \times 100 $$ <br> $$ = 61.9\% $$ | **Q2:** Aashni's age is what % of Ashish's? <br><br> $$ \% = \frac{Aa}{As} \times 100 $$ <br><br> $$ = \frac{16}{42} \times 100 $$ <br><br> $$ = 38.1\% $$ |
| **Q3:** Ashish's age is what % of Aashni's? <br><br> $$ \% = \frac{As}{Aa} \times 100 $$ <br><br> $$ = \frac{42}{16} \times 100 $$ <br><br> $$ = 262.5\% $$ | **Q4:** Ashish is what % older than Aashni? <br><br> $$ \% \text{ older} = \frac{As - Aa}{Aa} \times 100 $$ <br> $$ = \frac{42 - 16}{16} \times 100 $$ <br> $$ = 162.5\% $$ |

**Ex:** if I am 25% older than Bob, then Bob is what % younger than me?
* Me = 1.25x
* Bob = x
$$ \% \text{ younger} = \frac{\text{Bob} - \text{Me}}{\text{Me}} \times 100 $$
$$ = \frac{x - 1.25x}{1.25x} \cdot 100 $$
$$ = \frac{-0.25\cancel{x}}{1.25\cancel{x}} \cdot 100 $$
$$ = -\frac{1}{5} \times 100 = \mathbf{20\%} \text{ (boxed)} $$

**Ex:** If I am 25% older than you today, what % older am I in 10 years?

| | Today | In a decade |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Me | 1.25x | 1.25x + 10 |
| you | x | x + 10 |

$$ \% \text{ older} = \frac{(1.25x + 10) - (x + 10)}{x + 10} \times 100 $$
$$ = \frac{0.25x}{x + 10} \times 100 $$

---

### Page 13

*(continued from previous page:)*
$$ \mathbf{= \frac{25x}{x + 10}} \text{ (boxed)} $$

I have **20% less $ than you** *(underlined)*. If I **stole $50 from Matias** *(underlined)*, then what % is my $ in terms of **yours** *(circled)*?

| | Currently: | After Robbery |
| --- | --- | --- |
| A: | 0.8x | 0.8x + 50 |
| M: | x | x - 50 |

$$ \% = \frac{0.8x + 50}{x - 50} \times 100 $$
$$ \mathbf{= \frac{80x + 5000}{x - 50}} \text{ (boxed)} $$

**Mixture Problems:**

| | Acid | Total |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Given Mix 1** <br> at **x** % of acid | $m \left( \frac{x}{100} \right)$ | $m$ |
| **Given Mix 2** <br> at **y** % of acid | $(50 - m) \left( \frac{y}{100} \right)$ | $50 - m$ |
| **Produce a Mix** <br> at **z** % of acid | $50 \left( \frac{z}{100} \right)$ | $50$ |

*(Note: The Total column is boxed in purple, with a bracket indicating addition, and labeled "fill first" in pink.)*
*(Note: In the Acid column, there is a plus sign and an equals sign indicating the equation.)*

Note: $x < z < y$
Eqn: $m \left( \frac{x}{100} \right) + (50 - m) \left( \frac{y}{100} \right) = 50 \left( \frac{z}{100} \right)$

**Ex:** How many liters of a 25% acidic solution must be added to 40L of a 40% acidic solution to make a solution that is 30% acidic?

| | A | T |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Mix 1 at 25% acidic | 0.25x | x |
| Mix 2 at 40% acidic | 0.40(40) | 40 |
| Make a 30% acidic | 0.30(x + 40) | x + 40 |

Eqn: $0.25x + 0.40(40) = 0.30(x + 40)$
$0.25x + 16 = 0.30x + 12$
$4 = 0.05x$
$x = 80\text{L}$ *(boxed)*

| TRUE RELATIONSHIP | COMMON TRANSITIONS | EXAMPLE |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| present an opposing point of view or balance an idea that is either too positive or too negative.<br><br>*Green notes:*<br>S1. claim $\leftarrow$<br>S2. another claim that either opposes or balances | however, fortunately, on the other hand, conversely, whereas, **while** (underlined), in contrast, on the contrary, yet. | **While** Final Fantasy is often considered the greatest video game franchise, there are many that deem the franchise boring.<br><br>*Annotations:*<br>- "greatest video game franchise" is underlined and labeled `claim` (yellow).<br>- "there are many that deem the franchise boring." is underlined with an arrow pointing to the label `balances` (purple). |

**Note:**
transitions can be expressed in two major ways
1. Transition S1, S2
*(Yellow note pointing to the comma: "IC")*
2. S1. Transition, S2

**Process**
**Step 1:** read either the IC or the sentence before the transition and determine its function.
**Step 2:** read the sentence including the transition and identify its relationship to the prior.
**Step 3:** identify an appropriate transition
**Step 4:** do not force

# Roots of a Quadratic

a root is a value of $x$ that makes the $y$ value of the quadratic equal to $0$.

$$\text{Root} = \text{Solution} = \text{Zeros}$$

```
                          [Root = Solution = Zeros]
                               /             \
                   x-intercepts               NOT x-intercepts
                   b² - 4ac ≥ 0                 b² - 4ac < 0
                             /                     \
                          Real                   Complex
                         /    \                 x₁ = a + bi
             b²-4ac = 0 /      \ b²-4ac > 0     xcp = a - bi
                       /        \                    \
     Rational + Repeated        Distinct              |
  Note: the root in this        /      \              |
  case is the same as xv  perfect      non-perfect    |
         |                 square        square       |
   [Graph: Parabola          /            \           |
   touching x-axis at    Rational       Irrational    |
   vertex; Root=vertex]  [Graph: two    x₁ = a + √b   /
                         intercepts]    xcp = a - √b /
                                             \      /
                                              \    /
                                      In both of these situations, 
                                      the two roots of the quadratic 
                                      come in conjugate pairs
```

**Note:** If a parabola does not cross the x axis, nor touch the x axis, then the quadratic has complex roots.
**Note:** From standard form ($y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$), there exists a value called the discriminant ($b^2 - 4ac$) that helps to classify the roots.

**Ex:**
$$3x^2 - 4x + p = 0$$
In the above quadratic equation, where $p$ is a constant, find the value of $p$ such that the quadratic has no real solution.

$$\therefore \text{both roots (both solutions) are complex}$$
$$\therefore b^2 - 4ac < 0$$

$$(-4)^2 - 4(3)(p) < 0$$
$$16 - 12p < 0$$
$$-12p < -16$$
$$p > \frac{16}{12}$$
$$\mathbf{p > \frac{4}{3}} \text{ (Boxed)}$$

--- Page 9 ---

**finding the roots:**
it is possible to find the roots of a quadratic in either vertex form or intercept form.

**Intercept form:** $0 = a(x-p)(x-q)$ *(originally $y = f(x)$ with $y$ replaced by 0)*
Method: Zero Product Property (zpp)
$$\therefore \text{Since } a(x-p)(x-q) = 0$$
$$\text{then either } (x-p) = 0 \text{ or } (x-q) = 0$$
$$x = p \quad \text{or} \quad x = q$$

**Ex:** if $-3(x-2)(x+7) = 0$
then which of the following must be correct.
I. $x = -3$
II. $x = 2$
III. $x = -7$
$$\mathbf{\text{None.}} \text{ (Boxed)}$$

**Vertex form:** $0 = a(x-h)^2 + k$ *(originally $y = f(x)$ with $y$ replaced by 0)*
Method: Square Root Method

| Left Column | Right Column |
| :--- | :--- |
| $$0 = a(x-h)^2 + k$$<br>$$-k = a(x-h)^2$$<br>$$\frac{-k}{a} = (x-h)^2$$<br>$$(x-h) = \pm \frac{\sqrt{-k}}{\sqrt{a}} \cdot \frac{\sqrt{a}}{\sqrt{a}}$$<br>$$(x-h) = \pm \frac{\sqrt{-ak}}{a}$$<br>$$x = h \pm \frac{\sqrt{-ak}}{a}$$<br>$$\mathbf{x = \frac{ah \pm \sqrt{-ak}}{a}} \text{ (Boxed)}$$ | **Ex:** $0 = 2(x+7)^2 + 15$ *(originally $y = f(x)$ with $y$ replaced by 0)*<br>find all roots.<br>$$0 = 2(x+7)^2 + 15$$<br>$$-15 = 2(x+7)^2$$<br>$$\frac{-15}{2} = (x+7)^2$$<br>$$(x+7) = \pm \frac{\sqrt{-15}}{\sqrt{2}} \cdot \frac{\sqrt{2}}{\sqrt{2}}$$<br>$$(x+7) = \pm \frac{i\sqrt{30}}{2}$$<br>$$x = -7 \pm \frac{i\sqrt{30}}{2}$$<br>$$\mathbf{x = \frac{-14 \pm i\sqrt{30}}{2}} \text{ (Boxed)}$$ |

--- Page 10 ---

**Standard form:** $$y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$$

Technically, there are no ways to find the roots of a quadratic in standard form.

```
                    FACTOR
                  /--------> Intercept form -- Zpp --> Roots
ax² + bx + c = 0 |
                  \--------> Vertex -- Square Root Method --> Roots
                     CTS
```

### Quadratic Formula
$$0 = ax^2 + bx + c$$ *(originally $y = f(x)$ with $y$ replaced by 0)*
$$0 = (ax^2 + bx) + c$$
$$0 = a\left(x^2 + \frac{b}{a}x\right) + c$$
$$0 = a\left(x^2 + \frac{b}{a}x + \frac{b^2}{4a^2}\right) + c - a\left(\frac{b^2}{4a^2}\right)$$ *(the first a and the a in 4a² are crossed out)*
$$0 = a\left(x + \frac{b}{2a}\right)^2 + c - \frac{b^2}{4a}$$
$$a\left(x + \frac{b}{2a}\right)^2 = \frac{b^2}{4a} - c$$
$$a\left(x + \frac{b}{2a}\right)^2 = \frac{b^2 - 4ac}{4a}$$
$$\left(x + \frac{b}{2a}\right)^2 = \frac{b^2 - 4ac}{4a^2}$$
$$x + \frac{b}{2a} = \pm \frac{\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$$
$$\mathbf{x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}} \text{ (Boxed)} \quad \text{QF}$$

--- Page 11 ---

**15**
$$x^2 - \frac{k}{2}x = 2p$$
In the quadratic equation above, $k$ and $p$ are constants. What are the solutions for $x$?
* A) $x = \frac{k}{4} \pm \frac{\sqrt{k^2 + 2p}}{4}$
* **B) $x = \frac{k}{4} \pm \frac{\sqrt{k^2 + 32p}}{4}$** [Boxed]
* C) $x = \frac{k}{2} \pm \frac{\sqrt{k^2 + 2p}}{2}$
* D) $x = \frac{k}{2} \pm \frac{\sqrt{k^2 + 32p}}{4}$

*Purple notes on the right:*
$$\left(x^2 - \frac{k}{2}x - 2p = 0\right)(2)$$
$$2x^2 - kx - 4p = 0$$
$$a = 2 \quad b = -k \quad c = -4p$$
$$x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$$
$$x = \frac{k \pm \sqrt{k^2 - 4(2)(-4p)}}{4}$$
$$x = \frac{k \pm \sqrt{k^2 + 32p}}{4} = \frac{k}{4} \pm \frac{\sqrt{k^2 + 32p}}{4}$$

Solve the roots of $$4x^2 + \frac{k}{3}x = \frac{2}{5}r$$

| **Sum of the roots** (Boxed) | **Product of the Roots** (Boxed) |
| :--- | :--- |
| $$S_R = -\frac{b}{a}$$ | $$P_R = \frac{c}{a}$$ |

--- Page 12 ---

**5.** The product of two positive numbers is 385. The second number is 13 greater than twice the first number. What is the value of one of the numbers?

*Purple notes:*
$$xy = 385$$
$$y = 13 + 2x$$
$$y = 13 + 2(11)$$
$$\mathbf{= 35} \text{ (Boxed)}$$

$$x(13 + 2x) = 385$$
$$2x^2 + 13x = 385$$
$$2x^2 + 13x - 385 = 0$$
$$a = 2 \quad b = 13 \quad c = -385$$
$$x = \frac{-13 \pm \sqrt{169 - 4(2)(-385)}}{4} = \frac{-13 \pm 57}{4}$$
* $$\text{Upper branch: } \frac{-13 + 57}{4} = \mathbf{11} \text{ (Boxed)}$$
* $$\text{Lower branch: } \frac{-13 - 57}{4} = -\frac{70}{4} \text{ (Crossed out)}$$

--- Page 13 ---

# Percents:

**Note:** a percent must be converted to a factor to be used.
to convert a % to a factor, either divide the % by 100 or move the decimal place two units to the left.

| % | factor |
| :--- | :--- |
| 20% | $\frac{20}{100} = \frac{1}{5}$ or $0.20$ |
| 0.5% | $\frac{0.5}{100} = \frac{1}{200}$ or $0.005$ |
| x% | $\mathbf{\frac{x}{100}}$ (Boxed) or $0.01(x)$ |

When given a % in the problem, you need to know which version of the % to use

**Version 1** (Boxed)
the % is of some total or of some standard
* use the % as is
* and convert to a factor

**Version 2** (Boxed)
the % is of some increase to some total or some standard
* use (100 + %)
* and then convert to some factor

**Version 3** (Boxed)
the % is of some decrease to some total or some standard
* (use 100 - %)
* and then convert to a factor.

### Page 1

**READING / GRAMMAR**  
**DAY #** 3

---

**TOPICS OF THE DAY**
* Punctuation

---

### Page 2

**Punctuation**

**Commas**

**Use 1:** to separate the items of a list
$$... \text{ item1}, \text{ item2}, \text{ item3}, \text{ and } \text{item 4}...$$
*(Note: Comma after "item3" is circled in orange with a red arrow pointing to it labeled "wrong")*

**Oxford Comma:** the oxford comma is not mandatory in writing. As such, you will not be tested on its use. However, you will be tested on its location - it is located before the "and".

**Note:** the items of a list can also be separated by the use of a semi-colon (;). You do so if the items of the list themselves contain commas.
*(Note in blue/green with an arrow pointing to the semicolon before "and": "this is the only exception where a semi-colon can be joined with 'and'")*

**Ex:** Ruhi plans to read Title 1, description 1; Title 2, description 2; and Title 3, description 3.
*(Labels: "Title 1, description 1" is labeled "item 1", "Title 2, description 2" is labeled "item 2", "Title 3, description 3" is labeled "item 3")*

**Use 2:** to join two full sentences with the use of a coordinating conjunction.
* $\therefore$ two expressed subjects
* two expressed verbs
* two full ideas

**form:** sentence 1, (cc) sentence 2.

Where your coordinating conjunctions (CC) are your **FANBOYS**:
* **F** - for  
* **A** - and  
* **N** - nor  
* **B** - but  
* **O** - or  
* **Y** - yet  
* **S** - so  

**Ex:** Ashish plans to buy a new car and will have it shipped to him. [$\checkmark$]
Ashish plans to buy a new car, and he will have it shipped to him. [$\boxtimes$]
*(Underlined: "Ashish" ($S_1$), "plans" ($V_1$) in the first sentence; "Ashish" ($S_1$), "plans" ($V_1$), "he" ($S_2$), "will have" ($V_2$) in the second sentence)*

---

### Page 3

**Correctly Joining 2 Independent Clauses**
1. $\text{IC} . \text{IC}$
2. $\text{IC} ; \text{IC}$
3. $\text{IC} , \text{cc } \text{IC}$
4. $\text{IC} : \text{IC}$

**Incorrectly Join 2 Independent Clauses**
1. $\text{IC } \text{IC}$ (no punctuation) (run-on sentence)
2. $\text{IC} , \text{IC}$ (no coordinating conjunction) (comma splice)
3. $\text{IC} ; \text{cc } \text{IC}$
4. $\text{IC} , \text{false cc } \text{IC}$

Most common false cc is **"however"**

[$\boxtimes$] Tom is dating Emily, however he is attracted to Julie.
*(Brackets under "Tom is dating Emily" and "he is attracted to Julie" are labeled "sentence". Yellow arrow points to "however" labeled "cc = false")*

**Correctly using "however"**
1. $\text{IC} . \text{However} , \text{IC}$ $\rightarrow$ Tom is dating Emily. However, he is attracted to Julie.
2. $\text{IC} ; \text{however} , \text{IC}$
3. $\text{IC} . \text{IC} , \text{however}.$ $\rightarrow$ Tom is dating Emily. He is attracted to Julie, however.
4. $\text{IC} ; \text{IC} , \text{however}.$
5. $\text{IC} . \text{IC}_{1/2} , \text{however} , \text{IC}_{2/2}$ $\rightarrow$ Tom is dating Emily. He, however, is attracted to Julie.
6. $\text{IC} ; \text{IC}_{1/2} , \text{however} , \text{IC}_{2/2}$

**Careful:** the SAT often tests which sentence the "however" is part of - it needs to be a part of the sentence performing the opposition.

**Use 3:** to join a dependent clause with an independent clause; however, the use of the comma depends on the location of the dependent clause.

* **If** the dependent clause comes before the independent clause, then you **MUST** use a comma.
* **If** the dependent clause comes after the independent clause, then you **MIGHT** use a comma. You do so if the dependent clause begins with an adverb of concession.

Your concessional adverbs:

---

### Page 4

supposing it rains, the party will be cancelled.  
The party will be cancelled supposing it rains.  

Even though I have no water, Vishesh keeps taking my water bottle.  
Viheesh keeps taking my water bottle, even though I have no water.  
*(Note: in "keeps taking taking", the first "taking" is crossed out)*

**Use 4:** before and after a middle comma clause (also known as an appositive or non-essential clause)

**Note:** a middle comma clause can actually be punctuated in 3 ways:
1. $\text{IC}_{1/2} , \text{ mcc } , \text{IC}_{2/2}$
2. $\text{IC}_{1/2} - \text{ mcc } - \text{IC}_{2/2}$
3. $\text{IC}_{1/2} \text{ ( mcc ) } \text{IC}_{2/2}$

*no mixing punctuation*

**Note: the SAT/ACT Trap**
$$\text{Subject} , \text{ ~~~~~~}$$
$$\text{~~~~~~~~~~~~~~}$$
$$\text{~~~~~~~~~~~~~~}$$
$$\text{~~~~~~~~~~~~~~}$$
$$\text{~~~~~~~~~~} - \text{ Verb}$$
$$\text{~~~.}$$

**Use 5:** to enter an ending comma clause (ecc)
* **Recall:** most ending comma clauses begin with an -ing adverb.
* **Recall:** ending comma clauses are usually defining or clarifying phrases.

**Ex:** Vishesh keeps taking the water bottle, hoping that he will find water.

**Use 6:** between two adjectives that describe the same noun.
**Ex:** Ashish is wearing a big, red t-shirt.
*(Note: swap arrows indicating "big" and "red" can be interchanged)*
**Ex:** Viraj is wearing a light gray shirt.
*(Note: swap arrows crossed out indicating "light" and "gray" cannot be interchanged)*

---

### Page 5

**Colon (:)**

**Note:** to use a colon, there **MUST** be a full sentence before the colon.

**Use 1:** to enter a list with no introductory phrase to the list.
* No for example
* No such as
* No to illustrate  
* No including
* No with
*(Brackets point to the label: "No introductory phrase")*

**Ex:** Aashni eats a big breakfast: waffles, pancakes, cereal, and fruit.
**Note:** do not ever enter a list with a semicolon.
**Note:** you can enter a list with no punctuation.
**Ex:** Aashni eats waffles, pancakes, cereal, and fruit every morning.
**Note:** you can use an introductory phrase as long as you don't enter the list with a colon.
**Ex:** Aashni eats a big breakfast including pancakes, cereal, waffles, and fruit.

**Use 2:** to add information at the end of a sentence about the sentence.
**Ex:** Ashish studied eight long years for the SAT: he scored a perfect 1600.

---

**DASH (-)**

**Use 1:** before and after a MCC

**Use 2:** to add information at the end of a sentence about the sentence; however, this information is usually shocking.
**Ex:** Ashish studied eight long years for the SAT - he scored a 420.

---

### Page 6

**Semi-colon (;)**

**Use 1:** to separate the items of a list when the items themselves contain commas.
**Use 2:** to join two full sentences without the use of a cc.

---

**Apostrophe (')**

**Use 1:** to make a contraction, the joining of two words into one.
~~CONTRACTIONS~~
the only acceptable contraction is "it is" = "it's"

**Use 2:** to show possession
**Note:** there are non-apostrophe possessives:
* its
* their
* his
* her
* hers
* my
* mine
* our
* ours

*(Note on the right inside a cloud: "its'", "its's", "itss" $\rightarrow$ Not words)*

```
                      to show possessive with
                           an apostrophe
                                /   \
                               /     \
                              /       \
      if the word does not end in -S   if the word ends in S
                   |                             |
                  's                             |
                                          /              \
                                         /                \
                                   Simple noun        proper noun
                                        |                  |
                                        s'                s's
                                        |                  |
                                      cars'             Jesus's
                                      car's
```

### Page 7

**MATH**  
**DAY #** 2

**TOPICS OF THE DAY**
* Quadratics

---

### Page 8

**Quadratics**

A quadratic is a relationship between $y$ and the square of $x$. ($x^2$)
If the quadratic passes through the origin, then $y \sim x^2$.
* $\therefore$ if $x$ is doubled, $y$ is quadrupled.
* If $x$ is tripled, $y$ is scaled by factor of 9.
* If $y$ is doubled, $x$ is scaled by $\sqrt{2}$.

All quadratics produce a curve called a **parabola**, a U shaped curve that opens either up or down.

* **opens up** ($a > 0$)  
  *(Graph showing an upward-opening parabola)*
* **opens down** ($a < 0$)  
  *(Graph showing a downward-opening parabola)*

All parabolas have two characteristics:
* a vertex **AND**
* an axis of symmetry

**Vertex:** this is a coordinate point $(x_v, y_v)$ that corresponds to either the maximum point of the parabola (if parabola opens down) or the minimum point of the parabola (if parabola opens up).

* **opens up** ($a > 0$)  
  *(Graph showing an upward-opening parabola with a vertex marked at the bottom)*  
  **Vertex (minimum point)**
* **opens down** ($a < 0$)  
  *(Graph showing a downward-opening parabola with a vertex marked at the top)*  
  **Vertex (maximum point)**

**Note:** for any question involving a quadratic that refers to either maximum or minimum, simply find the vertex!!

---

### Page 9

**Axis of Symmetry:** this is an imaginary vertical line that passes through the parabola's vertex, dividing the parabola into two equal halves.

* **opens up** ($a > 0$)  
  *(Graph showing an upward-opening parabola with a vertical dashed line passing through the vertex, labeled "AoS")*  
  **Vertex (minimum point)**
* **opens down** ($a < 0$)  
  *(Graph showing a downward-opening parabola with a vertical dashed line passing through the vertex, labeled "AoS")*  
  **Vertex (maximum point)**

All quadratics can be expressed in one of three forms:
1. **Standard form:** $y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$  
   *(The constant "c" has an arrow pointing to "y-intercept" in the SAT cloud)*
2. **Vertex form:** $y = f(x) = a(x-h)^2 + k$  
   *(Under "h" is labeled "opp", under "k" is labeled "norm"; arrow points to "vertex (h, k)" in the SAT cloud)*
3. **Intercept form:** $y = f(x) = a(x-p)(x-q)$  
   *(Under "p" is labeled "opp", under "q" is labeled "opp"; arrow points to "x-intercepts (p, 0), (q, 0)" in the SAT cloud)*

**SAT Cloud Note:**
each form gives, as a constant or a coefficient, the __________ of the parabola.
* y-intercept
* vertex (h, k)
* x-intercepts (p, 0), (q, 0)

**finding the vertex:**

* **Vertex form:** $y = f(x) = a(x-h)^2 + k$
  * Vertex: $x_v = h$
  * $y_v = k$
  * $$\text{V}(h, k)$$
* **Standard form:** $y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$
  * Vertex: $x_v = \frac{-b}{2a}$
  * $y_v = f\left(\frac{-b}{2a}\right)$
  * $$\text{V}\left(\frac{-b}{2a}, f\left(\frac{-b}{2a}\right)\right)$$
* **Intercept form:** $y = f(x) = a(x-p)(x-q)$
  * Vertex: $x_v = \frac{p+q}{2}$
  * $y_v = f\left(\frac{p+q}{2}\right)$
  * $$\text{V}\left(\frac{p+q}{2}, f\left(\frac{p+q}{2}\right)\right)$$

**finding the Axis of Symmetry:**
$$x = x_v$$

**Ex:** $y = f(x) = -2(x + 3)(x - 7)$
* $x_v = \frac{-3 + 7}{2} = 2$
* $y_v = f(2) = -2(5)(-5) = 50$
* $$\text{V}: (2, 50)$$

---

### Page 11

**Question 6**
A quadratic function [marked: Vertex (7, 1296)] models the height [marked: DV (y)], in feet, of an object above the ground in terms of the time [marked: IV (x)], in seconds, after the object is launched off an elevated surface. The model indicates the object hits the ground 16 seconds after being launched [marked: $\rightarrow$ (16,0)]; the object reaches its maximum height of 1,296 feet above the ground 7 seconds after being launched. Based on the model, at what time after the object is launched, in seconds, does the object first reach a height of 972 feet? [marked: $\rightarrow$ y, to find x]

**Solution:**
Vertex: $(7, 1296)$
$$y = a(x - 7)^2 + 1296$$
Using point $(16, 0)$:
$$0 = a(16 - 7)^2 + 1296$$
$$0 = a(9)^2 + 1296$$
$$-1296 = 81a$$
$$a = -16$$

$$\mathbf{y = -16(x - 7)^2 + 1296}$$

Setting $y = 972$:
$$972 = -16(x - 7)^2 + 1296$$
$$-324 = -16(x - 7)^2$$
$$(x - 7)^2 = 20.25$$
$$x - 7 = \pm 4.5$$
$$x = 7 \pm 4.5$$
$$x = 2.5, 11.5$$
*(The value 2.5 is circled)*

--- DAILY NOTES (1A_MWF 10-1 Day 1.pdf) ---
--- Page 1 ---

READING / GRAMMAR DAY # 1

TOPICS OF THE DAY
* The Useless Clauses

--- Page 2 ---

The 3 Useless Clauses

What is absolutely necessary for there to be a complete sentence.
[✓] Subject
[✓] Verb

Ex: Tom plays. (Sentence)
    (S)   (V)

Useless Clause 1: Relative Clause
(simply a word that can be used to replace a noun. -> pronoun)
this is a clause that begins with a relative pronoun and will often continue until a verb is reached.

there are 5 relative pronouns:
[✓] who
[✓] whom
[✓] whose
[✓] which
[✓] that

Note: that does not use a comma, neither before nor after

Ex: Tom plays an instrument that was given to him by his grandfather.
    (that = RP -> points to instrument; "that was given to him by his grandfather" = Relative Clause)

Useless Clause 2: Comma Clauses

there are 3 types of comma clauses
Type 1: Introductory comma clause (ICC) - dependent clauses
Most Introductory comma clauses begin with a subordinating conjunction, a preposition, or an -ing adverb.

Note: Tom is playing [is / playing] (is circled, playing crossed out) any word ending in -ing is never a verb.

--- Page 3 ---

Form: Introductory comma clause, Sentence
(sc)
Whereas others are studying for the SAT, Tom is playing.
(Whereas others are studying for the SAT = Introductory Comma Clause)

Type 2: Ending Comma Clause (ECC) - defining or clarifying phrase
most ending comma clauses will begin with an -ing adverb
form: Sentence, Ending Comma Clause

Ex: Tom plays football, spending more than twenty hours per week practicing.
    (spending = ing-adverb; "spending more than twenty hours per week practicing." = Ending Comma Clause)

Type 3: Middle Comma Clause (MCC) - appositive or non-essential clause
this is a clause that disrupts a full sentence. Usually, the first part of the sentence contains the subject and the second part of the sentence contains the verb.

form: SEN, Middle Comma Clause, TENCE
      (SEN -> usually, the subject of the sentence is found here)
      (TENCE -> usually, the verb is found here.)

Tricky: a middle comma clause can actually be found in an introductory comma clause.
Ex: During the hail storm, the first ever recorded in the city, Manasa hit her head.

(Middle Comma Clause)
Ex: Tom, usually very uncreative, wrote a beautiful song that was awared first place in the talent competition.

--- Page 4 ---

Note: a sentence could contain zero, one, two, or all three comma clauses.

Ex: Beyond studying for his academics, Tom, currently the valedictorian of his high school, enjoys playing frivously with his many video games, much to the dislike of his strict parents.
    (Beyond studying for his academics, = ICC)
    (Tom = (S))
    (currently the valedictorian of his high school, = MCC)
    (enjoys = (V))
    (Tom enjoys playing frivously with his many video games = SENTENCE)
    (much to the dislike of his strict parents. = Ecc)

Useless Clause 3: prepositional phrase
this is a phrase that begins with a preposition and continues until a noun is reached, called the direct object or the object of the preposition.

Ex: Tom plays football against the wishes of his strict parents.
    (against = P, wishes = N -> PP: against the wishes)
    (of = (P), strict parents = (N) -> PP: of his strict parents)

Consider the following sentence:
Gluing beautiful pieces of artwork that are drawn by classmates is an experience Tom will not forget.
(of artwork = crossed out; that are drawn by classmates = crossed out; Tom will not forget = crossed out)
Gluing (in a box)

Note: a subject of a sentence can NEVER be found within a relative clause, a comma clause, nor a prepositional phrase.

--- Page 5 ---

Because the above clauses are effectively useless, I want you to get into the habit of crossing them out. To confirm that you have done so correctly, confirm that the balance is still a sentence.

--- Page 6 ---
