MATH DAY # 1

TOPICS OF THE DAY
* linear functions

--- Page 9 ---

linear functions

a linear function is a relationship between x (independent variable) and y (dependent variable) such that for each additional (same) constant change in x, there exists a corresponding constant change in y.

(graph showing steps of +2/-1, +2/-1, +4/-2 from coordinate (x_0, y_0))

Note: the ratio of the constant change in y to the constant change in x is called the slope (slope in a box, ratio underlined)

slope formula: m = (y_2 - y_1) / (x_2 - x_1)

Since the slope of a line is a ratio, there are several instances where using the slope as a proportion is effective.

Instance 1: given three collinear points
m_12 = m_13

Ex: find a relationship b/t n and m.
    (graph showing Pt 1 at (0, 0), Pt 2 at (n, 4), Pt 3 at (6, m))
    m_12 = m_13
    (y_2 - y_1) / (x_2 - x_1) = (y_3 - y_1) / (x_3 - x_1)
    (4 - 0) / (n - 0) = (m - 0) / (6 - 0)
    4 / n = m / 6
    [mn = 24] or [m = 24 / n] or [n = 24 / m]

NOTE: parallel lines are lines that have the same slope
perpendicular lines are lines whose slopes product to -1 -> the two slopes are negative reciprocals of each other

--- Page 10 ---

Ex: find s
    (graph with two perpendicular lines intersecting at (3, s). Line 1 passes through (1, 4), Line 2 passes through (5, 3).)
    m_1 = (s - 4) / (3 - 1)   m_2 = (s - 3) / (3 - 5)
        = (s - 4) / 2             = (s - 3) / -2
    ((s - 4) / 2) * ((s - 3) / -2) = -1
    ((s - 4)(s - 3)) / -4 = -1
    [(s - 4)(s - 3) = 4]

Instance 2: if a problem demonstrates linear relationships and discusses changes in the dependent and independent variable

Ex: The number of ounces of spaghetti sauce needed for a certain number of ounces of spaghetti can be modeled as:
    ss = 2/3 (s) + 4
    where ss is the number of ounces of spaghetti sauce and s is the number of ounces of spaghetti

    If I were to add 15 ounces of spaghetti, then how many additional ounces of sauce would I need?
    DV: number of ounces of spaghetti sauce
    IV: number of ounces of spaghetti
    slope: 2/3
    2/3 = x / 15
    3x = 30
    [x = 10 oz]

--- Page 11 ---

linear function modelling:

model a horizontal line <---------> (m = 0)
[y = #]

model a vertical line <---------> (m = undefined)
[x = #]

model a diagonal line
there are 3 forms

1. slope-intercept form [y = mx + b]
   Best for: graphing a line, analysis of a line

2. point-slope form [y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)]
   Best for modelling

3. Standard form [Ax + By = C]
   A should normally be ⊕
   A, B, C should normally be integers
   Best for word problems and for standardized testing

Note: though the point-slope form is best for modeling, the answer is often in slope-intercept form or standard form. However, it is easy to convert from point-slope form to either slope intercept or standard.

--- Page 12 ---

[point-slope form]
y - 4 = -2/5 (x + 1)
S1) If the slope is a fraction, multiply all terms by the LCD. (multiply terms by 5)
5y - 20 = -2(x + 1)
S2) distribute the x-side
5y - 20 = -2x - 2

(branching paths)
-> slope-intercept form:
   S3) Isolate y
   5y = -2x + 18
   [y = -2/5 x + 18/5]

-> standard form:
   S3) get x and y together
   2x + 5y = 18
   S4) Confirm conditions for A, B, C.
   [2x + 5y = 18]

full modelling:
S1) identify both the dependent variable (y) and the independent variable (x)
Note: if you see the phrase "as a function of," then the term before is the DV, while the term after is the IV.
S2) identify either the slope and one coordinate point or two coordinate points
S3) if necessary, find the slope
    [m = (y_2 - y_1) / (x_2 - x_1)]
S4) Using the slope and either coordinate point, model the line in point-slope form.
    y - y_1 = m(x - x_1) (arrows pointing to y_1, m, x_1)
S5) Convert

--- Page 13 ---

Ex: In 2012, a scientist recorded 12 foxes and 40 rabbits in a forest. Ten years later, he recorded 20 foxes and 52 rabbits. Assuming a linear relationship, model the number of foxes as a function of the number of rabbits.

    DV: the number of foxes     in 2012: (40, 12) (labeled x_1, y_1)
    IV: the number of rabbits   in 2022: (52, 20)

    m = (y_2 - y_1) / (x_2 - x_1)
      = (20 - 12) / (52 - 40)
      = 8 / 12
      = 2 / 3 (circled)

    y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)
    [f - 12 = 2/3 (r - 40)] (point-slope form) (multiply by 3)
    3f - 36 = 2(r - 40)
    3f - 36 = 2r - 80

    (branching paths)
    -> 3f = 2r - 44
       [f = 2/3 r - 44/3] (slope-int. form)
    -> (-2r + 3f = -44)(-1)
       [2r - 3f = 44] (standard form)

Ex: In 1954, a can of Coke cost $0.50. By 2012, the same can of Coke would cost $2.00. Assuming linear growth in a can of Coke, model, in all three forms, the cost of a can of Coke as a function of the number of years since 1950.

--- Page 14 ---

slope interpretation:
first, ensure the slope is a fraction (a/b)

Interpretation:
the value of the DV will [increase (m = +) or decrease (m = -)] by |a| for each additional b units of the IV

Ex: In 2012, a scientist recorded 12 foxes and 40 rabbits in a forest. Ten years later, he recorded 20 foxes and 52 rabbits. Assuming a linear relationship, model the number of foxes as a function of the number of rabbits.

    DV: the number of foxes     in 2012: (40, 12) (labeled x_1, y_1)
    IV: the number of rabbits   in 2022: (52, 20)

    m = (y_2 - y_1) / (x_2 - x_1)
      = (20 - 12) / (52 - 40)
      = 8 / 12
      = 2 / 3 (circled)

    y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)
    [f - 12 = 2/3 (r - 40)] (point-slope form) (multiply by 3)
    3f - 36 = 2(r - 40)
    3f - 36 = 2r - 80

    (branching paths)
    -> 3f = 2r - 44
       [f = 2/3 r - 44/3] (slope-int. form)
    -> (-2r + 3f = -44)(-1)
       [2r - 3f = 44] (standard form)

Interpret the slope (2/3): the number of foxes will increase by 2 for each additional 3 rabbits in the forest.

--- Page 15 ---

the price of a can of coke will increase by $3 for each additional 116 years after 1950.

### MATH
**DAY #** 9
**TOPICS OF THE DAY**
* Triangles, Part 2

---

### Trigonometry
the study of the ratios of the sides of a right triangle as a function of one of the right triangle's acute angles.

**A (acute angle)**
[Right Triangle ABC]
* Angle A = $x^\circ$
* Angle C = $(90-x)^\circ$
* Leg AB:
  * adjacent (for A)
  * opposite (for C)
* Leg BC:
  * opposite (for A)
  * adjacent (for C)
* Hypotenuse (AC)

**C (acute angle)**

trig function (acute angle) = ratio

there are six trigonometric functions:

**Core 3**
* $\sin(\text{AA}) = \text{opp}/\text{hyp}$
* $\cos(\text{AA}) = \text{adj}/\text{hyp}$
* $\tan(\text{AA}) = \text{opp}/\text{adj}$

**Reciprocal 3**
* $\csc(\text{AA}) = \frac{1}{\sin(\text{AA})} = \text{hyp}/\text{opp}$
* $\sec(\text{AA}) = \frac{1}{\cos(\text{AA})} = \text{hyp}/\text{adj}$
* $\cot(\text{AA}) = \frac{1}{\tan(\text{AA})} = \text{adj}/\text{opp}$

**Ex:** $\tan(A) = 4/7 = \text{opp}/\text{adj}$
[Right Triangle ABC]
* $\text{adj} = 7\text{m}$
* $\text{opp} = 4\text{m}$
* $\text{Hyp} = m\sqrt{65}$

$\text{Hyp}^2 = (7\text{m})^2 + (4\text{m})^2$
$\text{Hyp}^2 = 49\text{m}^2 + 16\text{m}^2$
$\text{Hyp}^2 = 65\text{m}^2$
$\text{Hyp} = m\sqrt{65}$

---

#### SAT Focus
**What is the relationship between sine and co-sine?**

They are co-functions, meaning that they produce equivalent ratios (meaning they are equal) when evaluated at complementary angles.

$\therefore \sin(x^\circ) = \cos(90-x^\circ) \quad \text{OR}$
$\sin(A) = \cos(B) \text{ iff } A+B = 90^\circ$

**Ex:**
$\cos(3x+10) = \sin(5x-20)$
$\therefore (3x+10) + (5x-20) = 90$
$8x-10 = 90$
$8x = 100$
$x = 100/8 = 25/2 \text{ or } 12.5$

**Ex:**
$\cos(37^\circ) = \sin(?)$
$? = 53^\circ$

---

#### ACT Focus:

| Using trigonometry to find a missing side | Using trigonometry to find a missing angle |
| :--- | :--- |
| ✔ the triangle must be right | ✔ again, the triangle must be right |
| ✔ Given only one side and effectively both acute angles | ✔ Given at least two sides |
| **Step 1:** choose an acute angle | **Step 1:** based on the angle you wish to find, label all three sides |
| **Step 2:** head step - label all three sides in relation to your chosen angle | **Step 2:** based on the labeling of the two sides, choose a trig function |
| **Step 3:** based on the labeling of the given side and the missing side, choose a trig function | **Step 3:** Setup formula <br> $\text{trig}(x) = \text{ratio}$ <br> $x = \text{trig}^{-1}(\text{ratio})$ |
| **Step 4:** Setup the trig equation <br> $\text{trig}(\text{AA}) = \frac{X}{V}$ OR $\text{trig}(\text{AA}) = \frac{V}{X}$ <br> $x = \frac{V}{\text{trig}(\text{AA})}$ <br> $x = V \cdot \text{trig}(\text{AA})$ | |

---

### Isosceles Triangles
Any triangle that has two sides being congruent, called **legs**, **AND** also two angles being congruent, called **base angles**. The non-congruent side is called the **base**. The non-congruent angle is called the **vertex angle**.

[Diagram of Isosceles Triangle]
* legs (equal sides, marked with congruent markings)
* base angles (BA, equal angles)
* base (bottom side)
* vertex angle (VA, top angle)

---

### Equilateral Triangle:
a triangle in which all sides are $\cong$ and all angles are $\cong$, each measuring $60^\circ$.

[Diagram of Equilateral Triangle]
* All three sides are $s$
* Base is $s$, split into $s/2$ and $s/2$ by the altitude
* Angles are $60^\circ$
* Altitude = $\frac{s\sqrt{3}}{2}$
* Top angle is split into $30^\circ$ and $30^\circ$ by the altitude

---

### Area of Triangles:
Generally: $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$

$A_{\text{eq}\Delta} = \frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$

$A_{\text{CT}} \begin{cases} A_{\text{SSS}} = \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)} \quad \text{Heron's Formula} \\ \quad s = \text{semi-perimeter} = \frac{1}{2}(a+b+c) \\ \\ A_{\text{SAS}} = \frac{1}{2}s_1s_2\sin\theta \end{cases}$

---

### Similar Triangles:
Two triangles are similar ($\sim$) iff all three corresponding angles are $\cong$ and all three corresponding sides are proportional.

[Diagram of $\Delta\text{ABC} \sim \Delta\text{XYZ}$]

* $\Delta\text{ABC} \sim \Delta\text{XYZ}$
* $\therefore \angle A \cong \angle X$
* $\angle B \cong \angle Y$
* $\angle C \cong \angle Z$

$\therefore \frac{\text{shortest side of } \Delta 1}{\text{shortest side of } \Delta 2} = \frac{\text{middle side of } \Delta 1}{\text{middle side of } \Delta 2} = \frac{\text{largest side of } \Delta 1}{\text{largest side of } \Delta 2}$

to prove two triangles are similar, you can use the:
**Angle-Angle Postulate:** if two angles of one triangle are congruent to two angles of another $\Delta$, then the two $\Delta$s are similar.
**OR** SSS
**OR** SAS

#### Special Similar Triangles
1. **Back to Back + Parallel**
   [Diagram of hourglass shaped similar triangles with parallel bases]
2. **Intermeshed + Parallel**
   [Diagram of nested similar triangles with parallel bases]

---

### 3. Triple Right $\Delta$
[Diagram of right triangle with altitude from right angle to hypotenuse]
$\frac{a}{c} = \frac{c}{b}$

[Diagram showing separation into three similar right triangles: small, medium, and large]

#### Proving $\Delta$'s Congruent:
1. SSS
2. SAS
3. ASA
4. AAS
5. HL

**Rhetorical Synthesis:**  

given bulleted research on topics and asked to synthesize (to create in an orderly fashion for efficiency) the information to support some claim given in the question header.  

**Step 1:** go through each bullet and identify each as either background (B) or evidential (E)  
**Step 2:** read the question header and highlight the claim you wish to support  
**Step 3:** choose the bullets you would use to support the claim.

### Page 14

**Triangles**  

**Angles of a triangle**  

* [x] all triangles have three internal angles whose sum is 180°.  
  $$I_1 + I_2 + I_3 = 180^\circ$$  
* [x] reflexive angle - this is an angle on the outside of the shape that helps to complete a full circle with its corresponding interior angle  
  $$R_n + I_n = 360^\circ$$  
* [x] Exterior Angle - this is an angle on the outside of the shape that forms a linear pair with its corresponding interior angle  

| True for all shapes | True for only triangles |
| :--- | :--- |
| [x] the sum of all exterior angles is 360° | each exterior angle is the sum of the two remote interior angles |
| [x] each exterior angle forms a linear pair with the corresponding interior angle | $\therefore E_1 = I_2 + I_3$ |
| $\therefore I_n + E_n = 180^\circ$ | $E_2 = I_1 + I_3$ |
| | $E_3 = I_1 + I_2$ |

**Ex:**  
[Diagram of parallel lines intersected by transversal line segment forming a triangle with angles $130^\circ$, $50^\circ$, $160^\circ$, $20^\circ$, $110^\circ$, and exterior angle $E = x^\circ$]  
$$x = 50 + 20$$  
$$x = 70^\circ$$  

**Sides of a Triangle:**  
all triangles satisfy the triangle inequality relationships  
* [x] the shortest side of a triangle is opposite the smallest angle of the triangle AND the middle side of a triangle is opposite the middle angle of the triangle AND the largest side of a triangle is opposite the largest angle of the triangle.  
* [x] the third side of any triangle must be larger than the positive difference of the other two sides AND smaller than the sum of the other two sides  
  $$| 1^{\text{st}} - 2^{\text{nd}} | < 3^{\text{rd}} < 1^{\text{st}} + 2^{\text{nd}}$$  
  * **Ex:** 1m, 2m, 3m  
    $$3\text{m} - 1\text{m} < 2\text{m} < 1\text{m} + 3\text{m}$$  
    $$2\text{m} < 2\text{m} < 4\text{m} \quad [X]$$  

**Ex:** a triangle has sides of 8 and 11. find all possible values for its perimeter.  
$$22 < P < 38$$  
[Diagram of a triangle with side lengths 8, 11, and x]  
$$11 - 8 < x < 11 + 8$$  
$$3 < x < 19$$  
$$P = 8 + 11 + x$$  
$$P = 19 + x$$  
$$P_{\text{min}} = 19 + 3 = 22$$  
$$P_{\text{max}} = 19 + 19 = 38$$  
$$\therefore 22 < P < 38$$  

---

### Page 15

**Right Triangles:**  

A right triangle is one that has exactly one right angle, whose measure is 90°, and whose two other angles are acute and complementary.  

[Diagram of a right triangle with side lengths leg 1, leg 2, and Hypotenuse; and acute angles $x^\circ$ and $(90-x)^\circ$]  

**Note:** yes, the largest side of a right triangle, the side opposite the right angle, is called the hypotenuse. The other two sides are called legs.  

Yes, a right triangle does satisfy the triangle inequality relationships. However, a right triangle will more strictly satisfy the pythagorean theorem. This theorem states: $(\text{leg } 1)^2 + (\text{leg } 2)^2 = (\text{hypotenuse})^2$ where the legs are often denoted as a and b and the hypotenuse as c.  
$$\therefore (a)^2 + (b)^2 = (c)^2$$  

You can apply the pythagorean theorem iff:  
* [x] the triangle must be right AND  
* [x] you know exactly two of the sides OR  
* [x] you know exactly one side and the other two are known in terms of one variable OR  
* [x] all three sides are expressed in terms of one variable  

**Ex:**  
[Diagram of a right triangle with vertical leg $x \rightarrow a = 5$, horizontal leg $x+7 \rightarrow b=12$, and hypotenuse $x+8 \rightarrow c=13$]  
$$a^2 + b^2 = c^2$$  
$$(x)^2 + (x+7)^2 = (x+8)^2$$  
$$x^2 + x^2 + 14x + 49 = x^2 + 16x + 64$$  
$$2x^2 + 14x + 49 = x^2 + 16x + 64$$  
$$x^2 - 2x - 15 = 0$$  
$$(x - 5)(x + 3) = 0$$  
$$x = 5 \quad \text{or} \quad x = -3 \text{ (crossed out)}$$  
$$x = 5$$  

If all three sides of a right triangle are integers, then the set of all three sides is called a **Pythagorean Triple**.  

**Common Triples**  

| 3-4-5 | 5-12-13 | 7-24-25 | 8-15-17 |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| 6-8-10 | 10-24-26 | 14-48-50 | 16-30-34 |
| 9-12-15 | 15-36-39 | | |

---

### Page 16

**The Special Right Triangles:**  

#### $30^\circ - 60^\circ - 90^\circ$  
[Diagram of a $30^\circ-60^\circ-90^\circ$ triangle with legs $s$, $s\sqrt{3}$, and hypotenuse $2s$]  
**Note:** any right triangle in which the hypotenuse is double a leg must be a $30^\circ-60^\circ-90^\circ$ right triangle.  

**Ex:**  
[Diagram of a right triangle with hypotenuse $2s = 8\sqrt{3}$, leg opposite $30^\circ$ is $s = 4\sqrt{3}$, and leg opposite $60^\circ$ is $s\sqrt{3} = 12$]  
$$s\sqrt{3} = 12$$  
$$s = \frac{12}{\sqrt{3}} = 4\sqrt{3}$$  

#### $45^\circ - 45^\circ - 90^\circ$  
* [x] Isosceles Right Triangle  
* [x] Diagonal(s) of a Square  
[Diagrams illustrating square diagonals and a $45^\circ-45^\circ-90^\circ$ triangle with legs $s$, $s$, and hypotenuse $s\sqrt{2}$]  

---

**Trigonometry:**  
the study of the ratios of the sides of a right triangle as a function of one of the acute angles  

**Terminology:**  
[Diagram of a right triangle with vertices $A$, $B$, $C$, showing opposite, adjacent, and hypotenuse relative to angles $A$ and $B$]  
$$\text{SOH CAH TOA}$$  

There are six trigonometric functions:  

| Core 3 | Reciprocal 3 |
| :--- | :--- |
| $\sin(A) = \frac{\text{opp}}{\text{hyp}}$ | $\csc(A) = \frac{1}{\sin(A)} = \frac{\text{hyp}}{\text{opp}}$ |
| $\cos(A) = \frac{\text{adj}}{\text{hyp}}$ | $\sec(A) = \frac{1}{\cos(A)} = \frac{\text{hyp}}{\text{adj}}$ |
| $\tan(A) = \frac{\text{opp}}{\text{adj}}$ | $\cot(A) = \frac{1}{\tan(A)} = \frac{\text{adj}}{\text{opp}}$ |

**Ex:**  
[Diagram of a right triangle with side lengths $15\text{m}$, $8\text{m}$, and hypotenuse $17\text{m}$]  
$$\tan(A) = \frac{8}{15}$$  

**Sine and Cosine are known as co-functions**  
this means they produce equivalent ratios when evaluated at complementary angles.  
$$\therefore \sin(x) = \cos(90-x) \quad \text{OR} \quad \sin(A) = \cos(B) \text{ then } A + B = 90^\circ$$  

if $W$ lies on $\overline{AB}$ find:  
$$\cos(\angle ACW) - \sin(\angle BCW) = 0$$  
[Diagram of a right triangle $ABC$ with right angle at $C$ and point $W$ lying on hypotenuse $AB$, with a line segment connecting $C$ and $W$]

Cross-Text Analysis
here, you are given two texts that are somehow related to each other.
→ in reading text one, simply perform the transitive foreshadow analysis that I taught on Reading Day 1.
→ in reading the second text, you must identify its relationship to the first passage.
☑ it is a disagreement by providing a differing viewpoint
☑ it is a disagreement by an attack of the claims in the first text.
☑ it is a neutral discussion of a topic by providing a different perspective.
☑ they agree by providing further support.
― After finishing text 2, please write down how author two would respond to author 1,
ensuring to have evidence from the second text to support
your ~~position~~ claims

**Page 30**

Proportions:
a proportion is when two ratios are equivalent under different circumstances.

| Ratio 1 | | Ratio 2 |
| :---: | :---: | :---: |
| Quantity 1 | = | Quantity 2 |
| Quantity 2 | | Quantity 2 |
| Circumstance 1 | | Circumstance 2 |

Ex: Manasa is throwing a party, inviting her f friends and ordering p slices of pizza to feed them. However, Avaneesh and Abi show up uninvited. Manasa, being the gracious host she is, will order more pizza so as to feed everyone at the same original rate. How many additional slices should she order?
* (In text: "same" is boxed. "original rate." is boxed, with "proportion:" written underneath)

| friends | = | f | = | 2 | f + 2 |
| :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: |
| pizza | | p | | x | x |
| | | **Invited** | | **Uninvited** | |

* fx = 2p
* x = 2p/f

* f/p = (f+2)/x
* fx = p(f+2)
* x = p(f+2)/f

* p(f+2)/f - pf/f
* = (pf + 2p - pf)/f
* = 2p/f

---

Question 15

* (Option D is circled. Options A and C are crossed out)

| miles | = | 100 25 | = | m |
| :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: |
| $ | | 16 4 | | 5 |
| | | **Current** | | **Save** |

* 4m = 25(5)
* 4/25 m = 5

**Page 32**

Lines and Angles:
Angle Pairs: this defines relationships between two angles

Vertical Angle Pairs: this is a set of two angles that share a common vertex and whose two rays extend in opposite directions
* ∠DVP and ∠CVA form a vertical angle pair.
* ☑ all vertical angle pairs form congruent angles.
  * :. ∠DVP ≅ ∠CVA
* ☑ all vertical angle pairs form an adjacent vertical angle pair.
  * :. ∠DVC forms a vertical angle pair with ∠PVA

Complementary Angle Pair: this is a set of 2 angles whose measures sum to 90°.
Note: there need not be any relationship b/t the angles besides their measures summing to 90°.

| Angle | Complement |
| :---: | :---: |
| 10° | 80° |
| 42° | 48° |
| 1° | 89° |
| x° | (90-x)° |

Supplementary Angle Pair: this is a set of two angles whose measures sum to 180°. Again, there need not be any relationship b/t the two angles besides their measures summing to 180°.

| Angle | Supplement |
| :---: | :---: |
| x° | (180-x)° |

Linear Pair: this is a set of two angles that share a common vertex and one common ray, and whose non common rays extend in opposite directions.
* ∠APB and ∠BPR form a linear pair.
* ☑ all linear pairs are a supplementary pair.

---

**Page 33**

Angle Pairs formed by a transversal cutting through two parallel lines

It is our goal to compare two angles, one at each cut.

Congruent Angle Pairs
1. Corresponding Angle Pairs - this is a set of two angles that lie in the same relative position in two locations.
   * [ ∠1, ∠5 / ∠2, ∠6 / ∠3, ∠7 / ∠4, ∠8 ]
2. Alternate Interior Angle Pair - this is a set of two angles that lie on the interior of the two parallel, but on opposite sides of the transversal.
   * [ ∠4, ∠6 / ∠3, ∠5 ]
3. Alternate Exterior Angle Pair - this is a set of 2 angles that live on the exterior of the 2 parallel, but on opposite sides of the transversal.
   * [ ∠1, ∠7 / ∠2, ∠8 ]

Supplementary Angle Pairs
1. Same-Side Interior Angle Pair: this is a set of two angles that lie on the interior of the two parallel lines, but on the same side of the transversal.
   * [ ∠4, ∠5 / ∠3, ∠6 ]
2. Same-side Exterior Angle Pair: this is a set of two angles that lie on the exterior of the two parallel lines, but on the same side of the transversal.
   * [ ∠1, ∠8 / ∠2, ∠7 ]

* [purple circle] + [green circle] = 180°

---

**Page 34**

Ex:
* (Diagram of intersecting lines with values 130°, 50°, 10°, 170°, 120°, 60° = x°)
* (Diagram of three parallel lines with angles 120°, x°, 130°)

### Page 22
**Answer Questions**

**step 1:** Answer the question yourself - yes, I want you to write your own answer to the question. Your response should be generated using evidence or context from the actual text.

**step 2:** begin to proceed through the answer choices with the goal of eliminating at least two answer choices, called dummy answer choices. What makes an answer choice dummy?
* [x] it provides a viewpoint that is not inherently discussed in the text.
* [x] it provides a viewpoint that opposes the view of the author
* [x] the response is significantly different than what you expressed
* [x] the response is too extremely harsh.
* [x] the response is too absolute in nature
  * Only
  * always
  * never
  * :
  * .

**step 3:** if two choices remain, then find the wrong answer
bc every wrong answer has something wrong with it, even if it is one word wrong.

*let us assume that you identify A-C as dummy responses.*  
*This does not ensure that D is correct blindly.*

### Page 30
**Factor of Change**

it given how certain quantities change by a certain percent or by a certain factor AND asked how a related quantity will thus change, then you can apply the factor of change approach

**Step 1:** start with a formula (given or known) $[ A = \pi r^2 ]$
**Step 2:** eliminate all constants $[ A \sim r^2 ]$
**Step 3:** replace each variable with its factor of change $[ A \sim (1.1)^2 ]$
**Step 4:** solve the unknown variable $[ A \sim 1.21 ]$
**Step 5:** find how far from 1 and convert to a percent

**Note:** 
* if larger than 1, an increase by the %
* if smaller than 1, a decrease by the %

**Ex:** If an object's mass is decreased by 20% and its speed increased by 20%, then by what % does its kinetic energy change? Note: $k = \frac{1}{2}mv^2$
$$k = \frac{1}{2}mv^2$$
$$k \sim mv^2$$
$$k \sim (0.8)(1.2)^2$$
$$k \sim 1.152$$
$$\therefore \text{kinetic energy is increased by } 15.2\%$$

$$A = \frac{\pi r^3 c^2}{2 \sqrt{d}} \ \bigg| \ \text{if } c \text{ is held constant, then by what \% will } A \text{ change if } r \text{ is inc. by } 25\% \text{ and } d \text{ is inc. by } 10\%.$$

---

### Page 31
**Ratios:**

a ratio is a comparison of two or more quantities by thier lowest corresponding parts $\rightarrow$ this means, ratios are always simplified.

*This creates a problem $\rightarrow$ ratios do not give exact values*

**Ex:** if the ratio of boys to girls is 2:3, then:
* a) how many boys are there? IDK
* b) how many girls are there? IDK
* c) how many total people? IDK
* d) How many more girls than boys? IDK.

*the solution to this problem: each part must be a multiple (m) of its corresponding value.*

**Ex:** if the ratio of boys to girls is 2:3, then:
* a) how many boys are there? ~~IDK~~ $2m$
* b) how many girls are there? ~~IDK~~ $3m$
* c) how many total people? ~~IDK~~ $5m$
* d) How many more girls than boys? ~~IDK~~ $m$

**Ex:** If a rectangle's width and length are in a 5:2 ratio and it has an area of 200, then what is its perimeter?

$$\text{Box Contents:}$$
$$W = 5m$$
$$A = 10m^2 \qquad \therefore 10m^2 = 200$$
$$P = 14m \qquad m^2 = 20$$
$$\qquad \qquad m = 2\sqrt{5}$$
$$L = 2m$$

$$\therefore P = 28\sqrt{5}$$

---

### Page 32
**Rates**

a rate is a special ratio that defines some sort of quantity vs some amount of consumption.

**Formula:** $\text{Quantity} = \text{Rate} \cdot (\text{Consumption Q})$
* *Annotation above Quantity: checkmark*
* *Annotation above Rate: "value" (with arrow pointing to Rate)*
* *Annotation above Consumption Q: v*

* $35\text{ mph} \rightarrow M = (35)(H)$
* $\$3/\text{gal} \rightarrow C = (3)(g)$

a special rate is called speed, which compares the quantity of distance to the consumption of time.
$$\therefore D = (\text{Speed})(T)$$

$$\text{average speed }(\bar{s}) = \frac{\text{total distance}}{\text{total time}}$$

**Note:** if a problem involves two or more speeds, then create the following chart:

| | Trip 1 at speed 1 | Trip 2 at speed 2 |
|---|---|---|
| **D** | | |
| **s** | | |
| **T** | | |

* *Annotation to the right of D and s rows: "I will allow a variable for one of these"*
* *Annotation to the right of T row: "$T = \frac{D}{s}$"*

**Ex:** If Ashish drives to AHA at an avg. speed of 40 mph and returns home, along the same route, at an avg. speed of 60 mph, then what is his avg. speed for the whole trip?

| | Trip 1 (to AHA at 40 mph) | Trip 2 (to home at 60 mph) |
|---|---|---|
| **D** | $x$ | $x$ |
| **s** | 40 | 60 |
| **T** | $\frac{x}{40}$ | $\frac{x}{60}$ |

$$\bar{s} = \frac{\text{total d}}{\text{total t}} = \frac{x+x}{\frac{x}{40} + \frac{x}{60}} = \frac{2x \cdot 120}{\frac{x}{40}\cdot 120 + \frac{x}{60}\cdot 120} = \frac{240x}{3x+2x} = \frac{240x}{5x} = 48\text{ mph}$$

# Page 2
**Verb Tenses**

| | Definition | Intermediate Helping Verb | Special Note |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Simple Present** | this refers to action that is occurring in the moment now without any reference to any other time period. | | Multiple actions, though not all in the present, can be grouped into a single one time present action. <br><br> Ex: Manasa is attending SAT class today. She will also attend tomorrow and for the next six weeks. <br> ↓ <br> Manasa is attending SAT class for the next six weeks. |
| **Simple Past** | this refers to a single one time past action without reference to any other time period. | | Multiple one time past actions can be grouped into a single one time past action. <br><br> Ex: Michael Jordan won the NBA championship in the '93-'94 season and won again in the '94-'95 season. <br> ↓ <br> Michael Jordan won two NBA championships, in the '93-'94 season and in the '94-'95 season. |
| **"Future"** | this, I will refer to as action that has not occurred. | will <br><br> would <br><br> could <br><br><br> would have <br><br> could have | → Ashish will buy a Porsche <br> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; H&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; V <br> → Ashish would buy a Porsche if... <br> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; H&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; V <br> → Ashish could buy a Porsche <br> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; H&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; V |

---

# Page 3

| | Definition | Intermediate Helping Verb | Special Note |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Past Perfect** | this refers to action that started in the past, occurred continuously in the past, and stopped in the past. | had (singular and plural) <br><br> Ex: It had rained for seven consecutive days. <br> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; H&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; V <br><br> Ex: They had dated. | to use either of the perfect cases, there needs to be some word that references continuity in action. This can include phrases such as 'since', 'until', 'continuously', 'consecutive', etc... |
| **Present Perfect** | this refers to action that started in the past, occurred continuously in the past, and is still happening today. | has (singular) <br> have (plural) <br><br> It has rained since March. <br> They have dated since high school. | Also, if you see a continuity in action words, it can override any other choice of tense. *(Note: Arrow points here from the 'since', 'until'... list above)* |

# Page 7
**Reading vs Reading**
* **kiddygarten reading** - you simply follow each word but do not make connections between words.
* **you read so well that** you make connections between words, make connections between sentences and even foreshadow events.

To do this proper reading approach, you **MUST** annotate the passage (SAT only); however, I do not care what **YOU** think is important.

**Step 1:** highlight or circle any and all transitional words/phrases and any and all transitional punctuation.

| Transitional Words/Phrases | Transitional Punctuation |
| :--- | :--- |
| **\*** but <br> and (if it acts as a coordinating conjunction) <br> however &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; furthermore &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; for example &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; although &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ... <br> yet &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; if &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; in short &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; though <br> moreover &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; including &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; nevertheless &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; even still | **:** <br> **;** <br> **—** <br> **...** <br> **( )** |

**Step 2:** focus on fully understanding the part after the transition — use it to foreshadow the events of the passage. Write down your foreshadow.

**Step 3:** as you continue to read (every single word), confirm or deny your foreshadow. If confirmed, keep reading. If denied, stop and start over.

The following text is adapted from Lewis Carroll's 1889 satirical novel *Sylvie and Bruno*. A crowd has gathered outside a room belonging to the Warden, an official who reports to the Lord Chancellor.

<u>One man, who was more excited than the rest, flung his hat high into the air,</u> **[and]** <u>shouted</u> <u>(as well as I could make out)</u> "Who roar for the Sub-Warden?" <u>Everybody roared,</u> **[but]** <u>whether it was for the Sub-Warden, or not, did not clearly appear:</u> some were shouting "Bread!" and some "Taxes!", **[but]** <u>no one seemed to know what it was they really wanted.</u>

All this I saw from the open window of the Warden's breakfast-saloon, looking across the shoulder of the Lord Chancellor.

<u>"What can it all mean?"</u> he kept repeating to himself. "I never heard such shouting before**[—and]** <u>at this time of the morning, too!</u> **[And]** <u>with such unanimity!</u>"

# Page 10
**MATH**

* Percents

# Page 11
**What is this standard that I talked about?**  
the standard is **always** the second item listed in a comparison.

**Percents are always multiplied by the original value, the whole value, or the standard value.**

**the only time %s should be added is if the %'s represent different categories of a whole total.**

**Ex:** A bag of Skittles contains 25% red, 30% green, 10% orange, 15% purple, and actually 6 yellow skittles. How many red Skittles are in the bag?

| Color | % | Amount |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **R** | 25 | ? |
| **O** | 10 | |
| **G** | 30 | |
| **P** | 15 | |
| **y** | *[Handwritten: 20]* | 6 |
| **T** | 100 | 30 |

[Work:]
$$\frac{25\%}{?} = \frac{20\%}{6}$$
$$150 = 20(?) \implies ? = 7.5$$

Or:
$$0.20(T) = 6 \implies T = 30$$
$$R = 0.25(30) = 7.5 \text{ [boxed]}$$

**Ex:** After receiving a **20% discount** *[Handwritten arrow: 80% -> 0.80]* and paying **10% sales tax** *[Handwritten arrow: 110% -> 1.10]*, Viraj buys the entire store for **S dollars**. What was the price of the store before any discounts and taxes?

*[Handwritten note: "effectively, we wish to know the original price of the store? (x)"]*

[Work:]
$$x(0.80)(1.10) = S$$
$$x(0.88) = S$$
$$x = \frac{S}{0.88} \cdot \frac{100}{100}$$
$$x = \frac{100S}{88} = \frac{25S}{22} \text{ [boxed]}$$

---

# Page 12
**finding percents**  
there are effectively two types of %s to find.

1. **Basic %:**  
$$\boxed{\% = \frac{\text{part}}{\text{whole}} \times 100 \quad \text{OR} \quad \% = \frac{\text{part}}{\text{standard}} \times 100}$$

2. **% of difference or % of change:**  
$$\boxed{\% \text{ change} = \frac{\text{New} - \text{old}}{\text{old}} \times 100 \quad \text{or} \quad \% \text{ change} = \frac{\text{New} - \text{standard}}{\text{standard}} \times 100}$$

**Ex:** Aashni is 16 years old while Ashish is 42 years old.

* **Q1: Aashni is what % younger than Ashish?**  
  $$\% \text{ younger} = \frac{\text{Aa} - \text{As}}{\text{As}} \times 100 \quad \text{[Handwritten: "diff %" above Aa - As]}$$
  $$= \frac{16 - 42}{42} \times 100$$
  $$= 61.9\%$$

* **Q2: Aashni's age is what % of Ashish's?**  
  $$\% = \frac{\text{Aa}}{\text{As}} \times 100$$
  $$= \frac{16}{42} \times 100$$
  $$= 38.1\%$$

* **Q3: Ashish's age is what % of Aashni's?**  
  $$\% = \frac{\text{As}}{\text{Aa}} \times 100$$
  $$= \frac{42}{16} \times 100$$
  $$= 262.5\%$$

* **Q4: Ashish is what % older than Aashni?**  
  $$\% \text{ older} = \frac{\text{As} - \text{Aa}}{\text{Aa}} \times 100$$
  $$= \frac{42 - 16}{16} \times 100$$
  $$= 162.5\%$$

**Ex:** if I am 25% older than Bob, then Bob is what % younger than me?  
[Work:]
$$As = 1.25x$$
$$\text{Bob} = x$$
$$\% \text{ younger} = \frac{\text{Bob} - \text{Ashish}}{\text{Ashish}} \times 100$$
$$= \frac{x - 1.25x}{1.25x} \times 100$$
$$= \frac{-0.25x}{1.25x} \times 100$$
$$= -\frac{1}{5} \times 100 = 20\% \text{ [boxed]}$$

**Ex:** if I am 25% older than you today, what % older am I in 10 years?  
[Work:]

| | Today | In a decade |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Me** | $1.25x$ | $1.25x + 10$ |
| **you** | $x$ | $x + 10$ |

$$\% \text{ older} = \frac{(1.25x + 10) - (x + 10)}{x + 10} \times 100 \quad \text{[Handwritten: +10 in both terms crossed out]}$$
$$= \frac{0.25x}{x + 10} \times 100$$

---

$$= \frac{25x}{x + 10}$$

**Ex:** I have **20% less $ than you**. If I stole **$50 from Matias**, then what % is my $ in terms of **yours**? *(Note: "yours" is circled)*

| | Currently: | After Robbery |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **A:** | $0.8x$ | $0.8x + 50$ |
| **M:** | $x$ | $x - 50$ |

$$\% = \frac{0.8x + 50}{x - 50} \times 100$$
$$= \frac{80x + 5000}{x - 50} \text{ [boxed]}$$

**Mixture Problems:**

| | Acid | Total |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Given Mix 1 at x% of acid** | $m(\frac{x}{100})$ | $m$ |
| **Given Mix 2 at y% of acid** | $(50 - m)(\frac{y}{100})$ | $50 - m$ |
| **Produce a Mix at z% of acid** | $50(\frac{z}{100})$ | $50$ |

*[Note: The "Total" column is enclosed in a purple box labeled "fill first". Arrows indicate Mix 1 + Mix 2 = Produce Mix. Also annotated: x < z < y]*

$$\text{Eqn: } m\left(\frac{x}{100}\right) + (50 - m)\left(\frac{y}{100}\right) = 50\left(\frac{z}{100}\right)$$

**Ex:** How many liters of a 25% acidic solution must be added to 40L of a 40% acidic solution to make a solution that is 30% acidic?

| | A (Acid) | T (Total) |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Mix 1 at 25% acidic** | $0.25x$ | $x$ |
| **Mix 2 at 40% acidic** | $0.40(40)$ | $40$ |
| **Make a 30% acidic** | $0.30(x + 40)$ | $x + 40$ |

$$\text{Eqn: } 0.25x + 0.40(40) = 0.30(x + 40)$$
$$0.25x + 16 = 0.30x + 12$$
$$4 = 0.05x \implies x = 80\text{L [boxed]}$$

# Page 14
**Question 5**

*[Handwritten math annotations in red:*
$a = -2 \quad b = 8 \quad c = \left(-\frac{b}{a}\right)$
$d = -2 \quad e = 8 \quad f = -\frac{c}{a}$
$dx^2 + ex + f = 0$
$P_R = \frac{f}{d}$ *(with arrow pointing from "product of the solutions")*
*]*

$$-2x^2 + 8x - \frac{c}{a} = 0$$

In the equation above, $a$, $b$, and $c$ are positive constants. The product of the solutions to the given equation is $\frac{5}{7}$. The number $b$ is 64% less than the number $c$. The number $a$ is $p\%$ less than the number $c$. What is the value of $p$?

[Work in red:]
$$\frac{\frac{c}{a}}{2} = \frac{5}{7}$$
$$\frac{c}{2a} = \frac{5}{7}$$
$$c = \frac{10a}{7}$$
$$b = 0.36 c$$
$$p = \frac{c - a}{c} \times 100$$
$$= \frac{c - \frac{7}{10}c}{c} \times 100$$
$$= \frac{\frac{3}{10}c}{c} \times 100$$
$$= 30$$

### Trigonometry
The study of the ratios of two sides of a right triangle as a function of one of the right triangle's acute angles.

* **Right Triangle Diagram (vertices A, B, C):**
  * Vertex A: A (acute angle), angle labeled $x^\circ$
  * Vertex C: C (acute angle), angle labeled $(90-x)^\circ$
  * Side AB (vertical):
    * pink: adjacent
    * yellow: opposite
  * Side BC (horizontal):
    * pink: opposite
    * yellow: adjacent
  * Side AC (hypotenuse):
    * pink: Hypotenuse
    * yellow: Hypotenuse

trig function (acute angle) = ratio

There are six trigonometric functions:
* **Core 3:**
  * $\sin(AA) = \text{opp}/\text{hyp}$
  * $\cos(AA) = \text{adj}/\text{hyp}$
  * $\tan(AA) = \text{opp}/\text{adj}$
* **Reciprocal 3:**
  * $\csc(AA) = \frac{1}{\sin(AA)} = \text{hyp}/\text{opp}$
  * $\sec(AA) = \frac{1}{\cos(AA)} = \text{hyp}/\text{adj}$
  * $\cot(AA) = \frac{1}{\tan(AA)} = \text{adj}/\text{opp}$

#### SAT Focus
**What is the relationship between sine and cosine?**
They are co-functions, meaning that they produce equivalent ratios (meaning they are equal) when evaluated at complementary angles.
* $\therefore \sin(x^\circ) = \cos(90-x^\circ)$   **OR**   $\sin(A) = \cos(B) \text{ iff } A + B = 90^\circ$

* **Example:**
  $\cos(3x + 10) = \sin(5x - 20)$
  $\therefore (3x + 10) + (5x - 20) = 90$
  $8x - 10 = 90$
  $8x = 100$
  $x = \frac{100}{8} = 25/2 \text{ or } 12.5$

* **Example:**
  $\cos(37^\circ) = \sin(?)$
  $? = 53^\circ$

* **Example:**
  $\tan(A) = 4/7 = \text{opp}/\text{adj}$
  * **Triangle Diagram:**
    * Side AB: $\text{adj} = 7m$
    * Side BC: $\text{opp} = 4m$
    * Hypotenuse AC: $\text{Hyp} = m\sqrt{65}$
  * **Calculations:**
    * $\text{Hyp}^2 = (7m)^2 + (4m)^2$
    * $\text{Hyp}^2 = 49m^2 + 16m^2$
    * $\text{Hyp}^2 = 65m^2$
    * $\text{Hyp} = m\sqrt{65}$

#### ACT Focus
**Using trigonometry to find a missing side:**
* ☑ The triangle must be right.
* ☑ Given only one side and effectively both acute angles.
* **Step 1:** Choose an acute angle.
* **Step 2:** Head step - label all three sides in relation to your chosen angle.
* **Step 3:** Based on the labeling of the given side and the missing side, choose a trig function.
* **Step 4:** Setup the trig equation:
  * $\text{trig}(AA) = \frac{X}{V} \implies X = V \cdot \text{trig}(AA)$
  * **OR**
  * $\text{trig}(AA) = \frac{V}{X} \implies x = \frac{V}{\text{trig}(AA)}$

**Using trigonometry to find a missing angle:**
* ☑ Again, the triangle must be right.
* ☑ Given at least two sides.
* **Step 1:** Based on the angle you wish to find, label all three sides.
* **Step 2:** Based on the labeling of the two sides, choose a trig function.
* **Step 3:** Setup formula:
  * $\text{trig}(X) = \text{ratio}$
  * $x = \text{trig}^{-1}(\text{ratio})$

---

### Isosceles Triangles
Any triangle that has two sides being congruent, called legs, **AND** also two angles being congruent, called base angles. The non-congruent side is called the base. The non-congruent angle is called the vertex angle.

* **Isosceles Triangle Diagram:**
  * Top angle: VA (vertex angle)
  * Left and Right sides: leg (each marked with two tick marks)
  * Bottom-left and Bottom-right angles: BA (base angle) (each marked with an arc and tick mark)
  * Bottom side: base (divided by a perpendicular altitude line, with three tick marks on each side)

### Equilateral Triangle
A triangle in which all sides are $\cong$ and all angles are $\cong$, each measuring $60^\circ$.

* **Equilateral Triangle Diagram:**
  * All three sides labeled $s$ (each marked with a single tick mark)
  * Bottom angles: $60^\circ$ (each marked with an arc and two tick marks)
  * Top angle: Bisected into $30^\circ$ and $30^\circ$, total labeled $60^\circ$
  * Altitude: Labeled $\frac{s\sqrt{3}}{2}$ (perpendicular to the base)
  * Base: Split into two segments, each labeled $s/2$ (each marked with three tick marks)

### Area of Triangles
* **Generally:** $A = \frac{1}{2} bh$
* **Equilateral:** $A_{eq\triangle} = \frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$
* **ACT Formulations:**
  * **Heron's Formula:** $A_{SSS} = \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}$
    * *Diagram:* Triangle with sides $a$, $b$, $c$.
    * $s = \text{semi-perimeter} = \frac{1}{2}(a+b+c)$
  * **SAS Area:** $A_{SAS} = \frac{1}{2} s_1 s_2 \sin\theta$
    * *Diagram:* Triangle with adjacent sides $s_1$, $s_2$ and included angle $\theta$.

---

### Similar Triangles
Two triangles are similar ($\sim$) iff all three corresponding angles are $\cong$ and all three corresponding sides are proportional.

* **Similarity Statement & Diagram (△ABC ~ △xyz):**
  * $\triangle ABC \sim \triangle xyz$
  * $\therefore \angle A \cong \angle x$
  * $\quad \angle B \cong \angle y$
  * $\quad \angle C \cong \angle z$
  * $\therefore \frac{\text{shortest side of } \triangle 1}{\text{shortest side of } \triangle 2} = \frac{\text{middle side of } \triangle 1}{\text{middle side of } \triangle 2} = \frac{\text{largest side of } \triangle 1}{\text{largest side of } \triangle 2}$

**To prove two triangles are similar, you can use the:**
* **Angle-Angle Postulate:** If two angles of one triangle are congruent to two angles of another $\triangle$, then the two $\triangle$s are similar.
* **OR** SSS
* **OR** SAS

#### Special Similar Triangles
1. **Back to Back + Parallel:**
   * *Diagram:* An hourglass/bow-tie shape where the top and bottom bases are parallel (marked with $\gg$). Vertical angles are congruent, and alternate interior angles are congruent.
2. **Intermeshed + Parallel:**
   * *Diagram:* A smaller triangle nested inside a larger triangle sharing the top vertex, with parallel bases (marked with $\gg$). Corresponding angles are congruent.

---

### Special Right Triangle Properties
3. **Triple Right △:**
   * *Diagram:* A large right triangle with an altitude $c$ drawn to the hypotenuse, splitting it into segments $a$ and $b$.
   * Formula: $\frac{a}{c} = \frac{c}{b}$
   * *Diagrams:* The three constituent similar right triangles drawn separately:
     * Small triangle (legs $a$, $c$)
     * Medium triangle (legs $c$, $b$)
     * Large triangle (hypotenuse $a+b$)

**Proving △'s Congruent:**
1. SSS
2. SAS
3. ASA
4. AAS
5. HL

### Page 14

Triangles

Angles of a triangle
* [checkmark] all triangles have three internal angles whose sum is 180°.
  (Diagram of a triangle with internal angles labeled $I_1, I_2, I_3$)
  $$I_1 + I_2 + I_3 = 180^\circ$$
* [checkmark] reflexive angle - this is an angle on the outside of the shape that helps to complete a full circle with its corresponding interior angle
  (Diagram of a triangle with interior angles $I_1, I_2, I_3$ and exterior reflexive angles $R_1, R_2, R_3$)
  $$R_n + I_n = 360^\circ$$

* [checkmark] Exterior Angle - this is an angle on the outside of the shape that forms a linear pair with its corresponding interior angle
  (Diagram of a triangle with interior angles $I_1, I_2, I_3$ and exterior angles $E_1, E_2, E_3$ formed by extending the sides)

  * **True for all shapes:**
    * [checkmark] the sum of all exterior angles is 360°
    * [checkmark] each exterior angle forms a linear pair with the corresponding interior angle
    * $$\therefore I_n + E_n = 180^\circ$$

  * **True for only triangles:**
    * each exterior angle is the sum of the two remote interior angles
    * $$\therefore E_1 = I_2 + I_3$$
    * $$E_2 = I_1 + I_3$$
    * $$E_3 = I_1 + I_2$$

Ex:
(Diagram with two parallel lines intersected by transversals forming a triangle with angles $130^\circ$, $50^\circ$, $160^\circ$, $20^\circ$)
$$E = x^\circ$$
$$x = 50 + 20$$
$$x = 70^\circ$$

Sides of a Triangle:
all triangles satisfy the triangle inequality relationships
* [checkmark] the shortest side of a triangle is opposite the smallest angle of the triangle AND
* the middle side of a triangle is opposite the middle angle of the triangle AND
* the largest side of a triangle is opposite the largest angle of the triangle.
  (Diagram showing sides labeled Small (S), Medium (M), and Large (L) opposite their corresponding angles)

* [checkmark] the third side of any triangle must be larger than the positive difference of the other two sides AND smaller than the sum of the other two sides
  $$|1\text{st} - 2\text{nd}| < 3\text{rd} < 1\text{st} + 2\text{nd}$$

Ex: $1\text{m}, 2\text{m}, 3\text{m}$
$$3 - 1 < 2 < 1 + 3$$
$$2 < 2 < 4$$ (Marked with an [X] box - invalid triangle)

Ex: a triangle has sides of $8$ and $11$. find all possible values for its perimeter.
$$22 < P < 38$$
(Diagram of a triangle with sides $8$, $11$, $x$)
$$11 - 8 < x < 11 + 8$$
$$3 < x < 19$$

$$P = 8 + 11 + x$$
$$P = 19 + x$$

$$P_{\text{min}} = 19 + 3 = 22$$
$$P_{\text{max}} = 19 + 19 = 38$$

$$\therefore 22 < P < 38$$

---

### Page 15

Right Triangles:

A right triangle is one that has exactly one right angle, whose measure is 90°, and whose two other angles are acute and complementary
(Diagram of a right triangle with sides labeled $leg_1$, $leg_2$, $Hypoteneuse$, and angles $90^\circ$, $x^\circ$, $(90-x)^\circ$)
`Note: yes, the largest side of a right triangle, the side opposite the right angle, is called the hypotenuse. The other two sides are called legs.`

Yes, a right triangle does satisfy the triangle inequality relationships. However, a right triangle will more strictly satisfy the pythagorean theorem. This theorem states:
$$(leg_1)^2 + (leg_2)^2 = (hypotenuse)^2$$
where the legs are often denoted as $a$ and $b$ and the hypotenuse as $c$.
$$\therefore (a)^2 + (b)^2 = (c)^2$$

you can apply the pythagorean theorem iff:
* [checkmark] the triangle must be right AND
* [checkmark] you know exactly two of the sides OR
* [checkmark] you know exactly one side and the other two are known in terms of one variable OR
* [checkmark] all three sides are expressed in terms of one variable

Ex:
(Diagram of a right triangle with sides $x$, $x+7$, and hypotenuse $x+8$)
$$x \rightarrow a = 5$$
$$x+7 \rightarrow b = 12$$
$$x+8 \rightarrow c = 13$$

$$a^2 + b^2 = c^2$$
$$(x)^2 + (x+7)^2 = (x+8)^2$$
$$x^2 + x^2 + 14x + 49 = x^2 + 16x + 64$$
$$2x^2 + 14x + 49 = x^2 + 16x + 64$$
$$x^2 - 2x - 15 = 0$$
$$(x-5)(x+3) = 0$$
$$x=5 \text{ or } x=-3 \text{ (crossed out)}$$

if all three sides of a right triangle are integers, then the set of all three sides is called a Pythagorean Triple

Common Triples
| 3-4-5 | 5-12-13 | 7-24-25 | 8-15-17 |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| 6-8-10 | 10-24-26 | 14-48-50 | 16-30-34 |
| 9-12-15 | 15-36-39 | | |

---

### Page 16

The Special Right Triangles:

**30° - 60° - 90°**
(Diagram of a 30-60-90 triangle with side $s$ opposite $30^\circ$, $s\sqrt{3}$ opposite $60^\circ$, and $2s$ opposite $90^\circ$)
`Note: any right triangle in which the hypotenuse is double a leg must be a 30°-60°-90° right triangle.`

Ex:
(Diagram of a 30-60-90 triangle with hypotenuse $12$ and side $s\sqrt{3}$)
$$s\sqrt{3} = 12$$
$$s = \frac{12}{\sqrt{3}} = 4\sqrt{3}$$
$$Side \text{ (opposite } 30^\circ\text{): } s = 4\sqrt{3}$$
$$2s \text{ (hypotenuse): } 8\sqrt{3}$$

**45° - 45° - 90°**
* [checkmark] Isosceles Right Triangle
* [checkmark] Diagonal(s) of a Square
  (Diagram of a square with a diagonal and $45^\circ$ angles)
  (Diagram of a square with intersecting diagonals forming $45^\circ$ and $90^\circ$ angles)
  (Diagram of a 45-45-90 triangle with legs $s$, $s$ and hypotenuse $s\sqrt{2}$)

Trigonometry:
the study of the ratios of the sides of a right triangle as a function of one of the acute angles

Terminology:
(Diagram of a right triangle with vertices A, B, C. Right angle at C.)
* Purple text (from perspective of angle A):
  * AC: adjacent
  * BC: opposite
  * AB: hypotenuse
* Blue/Green text (from perspective of angle B):
  * AC: opposite
  * BC: adjacent
  * AB: hypotenuse
* `SOH CAH TOA`

there are six trigonometric functions:
**Core 3**
$$\sin(\angle A) = \frac{\text{opp}}{\text{hyp}}$$
$$\cos(\angle A) = \frac{\text{adj}}{\text{hyp}}$$
$$\tan(\angle A) = \frac{\text{opp}}{\text{adj}}$$

**Reciprocal 3**
$$\csc(\angle A) = \frac{1}{\sin(\angle A)} = \frac{\text{hyp}}{\text{opp}}$$
$$\sec(\angle A) = \frac{1}{\cos(\angle A)} = \frac{\text{hyp}}{\text{adj}}$$
$$\text{cot}(\angle A) = \frac{1}{\tan(\angle A)} = \frac{\text{adj}}{\text{opp}}$$

Ex:
(Diagram of a right triangle with side lengths $15\text{m}$, $8\text{m}$, $17\text{m}$)
$$\tan(A) = \frac{8}{15}$$

Sine and Cosine are known as co-functions
this means they produce equivalent ratios when evaluated at complementary angles.
$$\therefore \sin(x) = \cos(90-x) \text{ OR } \sin(A) = \cos(B) \text{ then } A+B=90^\circ$$

(Diagram of a right triangle ABC with right angle at C, point W on AB, and line segment CW)
if W lies on $\overline{AB}$ find:
$$\cos(\angle ACW) - \sin(\angle BCW) = 0$$

### Page 30
Proportions:

a proportion is when two ratios are equivalent under

different circumstances.

             Ratio 1         Ratio 2
Quantity 1  [       ]       [       ]
---------- = [ ----- ]   =  [ ----- ]
Quantity 2  [       ]       [       ]
          Circumstance 1  Circumstance 2

Ex: Manasa is throwing a party, inviting her f friends
and ordering p slices of pizza to feed them. However,
Avaneesh and Abi show up uninvited. Manasa, being
the gracious host she is, will order more pizza so as to
feed everyone at the [same] [original] [rate]. [proportion]
How many additional slices should she order?

friends    [  f  ]      [ 2   f+2 ]
-------  = [ --- ]  =   [ --  --- ]
pizza      [  p  ]      [ x    x  ]
           Invited      Uninvited

[fx = 2p]
[x = 2p/f]

f/p = (f + 2)/x
fx = p(f + 2)
x = p(f + 2)/f

(p(f + 2))/f - (pf)/f
= (pf + 2p - pf)/f
= 2p/f

### Page 32
Lines and Angles:

Angle Pairs: this defines relationships between two angles

Vertical Angle Pairs: this is a set of two angles
  that share a common vertex and whose two rays
  extend in opposite directions

[Diagram labeled with angle DVP and angle AVE]
∠DVP and ∠AVE form a
vertical angle pair.
∠DVP
[✓] all vertical angle pairs form congruent angles.
    ∴ ∠DVP ≅ ∠AVE
[✓] all vertical angle pairs form an adjacent
    vertical angle pair.
    ∴ ∠DVC forms a vertical angle pair with ∠PVA

Complementary Angle Pair: this is a set of
  2 angles whose measures sum to 90°.
  Note: there need not be any relationship
  b/w the angles besides their measures
  summing to 90°.

  Angle      Complement
   10°          80°
   42°          48°
    1°          89°
    x°       [(90-x)°]

Supplementary Angle Pair: this is a set of two
  angles whose measures sum to 180°. Again,
  there need not be any relationship b/w the
  two angles besides their measures summing to 180°.

  Angle      Supplement
    x°       [(180-x)°]

Linear Pair: this is a set of two angles that share
  a common vertex and one common ray, and whose
  non common rays extend in opposite directions.

[Diagram showing angles APB and BPR]
∠APB and ∠BPR form a
linear pair.
[✓] all linear pairs are a supplementary pair.

### Page 33
Angle Pairs formed by a transversal cutting through two
parallel lines
                     transversal
                  2 / 1
This cut creates ----- line m
                  3 / 4

                  6 / 5
eight angles,     ----- line n
four at each cut  7 / 8

It is our goal to compare two angles, one at each cut.

Congruent Angle Pairs
1. Corresponding Angle Pairs -
   this is a set of two angles that
   lie in the same relative position in
   two locations.
   [ ∠1, ∠5 ]
   [ ∠2, ∠6 ]
   [ ∠3, ∠7 ]
   [ ∠4, ∠8 ]

2. Alternate Interior Angle Pair - this is a set of
   two angles that lie on the interior of the
   two parallel, but on opposite sides of the
   transversal.
   [ ∠4, ∠6 ]
   [ ∠3, ∠5 ]

3. Alternate Exterior Angle Pair - this is
   a set of 2 angles that lie on the
   exterior of the 2 parallel, but on
   opposite sides of the transversal.
   [ ∠1, ∠7 ]
   [ ∠2, ∠8 ]

Supplementary Angle Pairs
1. Same-Side Interior Angle Pair: this is a set of
   two angles that lie on the interior of the two parallel
   lines, but on the same side of the transversal.
   [ ∠4, ∠5 ]
   [ ∠3, ∠6 ]

2. Same-side Exterior Angle Pair: this is a set of two
   angles that lie on the exterior of the two parallel lines,
   but on the same side of the transversal.
   [ ∠1, ∠8 ]
   [ ∠2, ∠7 ]

[Diagram representing transversal line and parallel lines]
______ + ______ = 180°

### Page 22
**ANSWER QUESTIONS**  

**step 1:** Answer the question yourself - yes, I want you to **write** your own answer to the question. Your response should be generated using evidence or context from the actual text.  

**step 2:** begin to proceed through the answer choices with the goal of eliminating at least two answer choices, called dummy answer choices. What makes an answer choice dummy?  
* [Checked] it provides a viewpoint that is not inherently discussed in the text.  
* [Checked] it provides a viewpoint that opposes the view of the author  
* [Checked] the response is significantly different than what you expressed  
* [Checked] the response is too extremely harsh.  
* [Checked] the response is too **absolute** in nature  
  * Only  
  * always  
  * never  
  * ...  

**step 3:** if two choices remain, then find the wrong answer  
bc every wrong answer has something wrong with it, even if it is one word wrong.  

*let us assume that you identify A-C as dummy responses.*  
*This does not ensure that D is correct blindly.*

### Page 23
**Evidence Analysis:**  
You are effectively tasked to provide evidence from either the text or some graphic to support some claim.  

**Step 1:** highlight/underline the actual claim that you wish to find evidence for. This claim can be within the text or the question header.  

**Step 2:** effectively writing your own response.  
* ask yourself what evidence you would like to see that would support this claim.  
* then find this evidence in the text or the graphic  

**Step 3:** proceed through answer choices.

### Page 30
**Factor of Change**  

it given how certain quantities change by a certain percent or by a certain factor **AND** asked how a related quantity will thus change, then you can apply the factor of change approach.  

**Step 1:** start with a formula (given or known) `[ A = \pi r^2 ]`  
**Step 2:** Eliminate all constants `[ A \sim r^2 ]`  
**Step 3:** **replace** each variable with its factor of change `[ A \sim (1.1)^2 ]`  
**Step 4:** solve the unknown variable `[ A \sim 1.21 ]`  
**Step 5:** find how far from 1 and convert to a percent  

**Note:** if larger than 1, an increase by the %  
if smaller than 1, a decrease by the %  

**Ex:** If an object's mass is decreased by 20% and its speed increased by 20%, then by what % does its kinetic energy change? **Note:** `k = 1/2 m v^2`  
`k = 1/2 m v^2`  
`k \sim m v^2`  
`k \sim (0.8) (1.2)^2`  
`k \sim 1.152`  
`:. kinetic energy is increased by 15.2%`  

`A = \frac{\pi r^3 c^2}{2 \sqrt{d}}`  
if c is held constant, then by what % will A change if r is inc. by 25% and d is inc. by 10%.  

---
### Page 31
**Ratios:**  

a ratio is a comparison of two or more quantities by their lowest corresponding parts -> this means, ratios are always simplified.  

*This creates a problem -> ratios do not give exact values*  

**Ex:** if the ratio of boys to girls is 2 : 3, then:  
* a) how many boys are there? IDK  
* b) how many girls are there? IDK  
* c) how many total people? IDK  
* d) How many more girls than boys? IDK.  

*the solution to this problem: each part must be a multiple (m) of its corresponding value.*  

**Ex:** if the ratio of boys to girls is 2 : 3, then:  
* a) how many boys are there? ~~IDK~~ `2m`  
* b) how many girls are there? ~~IDK~~ `3m`  
* c) how many total people? ~~IDK~~ `5m`  
* d) How many more girls than boys? ~~IDK~~ `m`  

**Ex:** If a rectangle's width and length are in a 5:2 ratio and it has an area of 200, then what is its perimeter?  
`W = 5m`  
`L = 2m`  
`A = 10m^2`  
`P = 14m`  

Inside box:  
`A = 10m^2`  
`:. 10m^2 = 200`  
`m^2 = 20`  
`m = 2\sqrt{5}`  

`:. P = 28\sqrt{5}` (boxed)  

---
### Page 32
**Rates**  

a rate is a special ratio that defines some sort of quantity vs some amount of consumption.  

`value` (written above "Rate")  
`v` (written above "Quantity" and "Consumption Q")  
**Formula:** Quantity = Rate (Consumption Q)  
35 mph -> M = (35)(H)  
$3/gal -> C = (3)(g)  

a special rate is called speed, which compares the quantity of distance to the consumption of time.  
`:. D = (Speed)(T)`  

Inside red box:  
`average speed (\bar{S}) = \frac{\text{total distance}}{\text{total time}}`  

**Note:** if a problem involves two or more speeds, then create the following chart:  

| | Trip 1 at speed 1 | Trip 2 at speed 2 |
|---|---|---|
| **D** | | |
| **S** | | |
| **T** | | |

`I will allow a variable for one of these` (bracket pointing to D and S rows)  
`T = D / S` (pointing to T row)  

**Ex:** If Ashish drives to AHA at an avg. speed of 40mph and returns home, along the same route, at an avg. speed of 60mph, then what is his avg. speed for the whole trip?  

| | Trip 1 (to AHA at 40mph) | Trip 2 (to home at 60mph) |
|---|---|---|
| **D** | x | x |
| **S** | 40 | 60 |
| **T** | x / 40 | x / 60 |

`\bar{S} = \frac{\text{total } d}{\text{total } t} = \frac{x + x}{\frac{x}{40} + \frac{x}{60}} = \frac{2x \cdot 120}{\left(\frac{x}{40} + \frac{x}{60}\right) \cdot 120} = \frac{240x}{3x + 2x} = \frac{240x}{5x} = 48\text{mph}`  
`[= 48mph]` (boxed)

# DAY 5: READING / GRAMMAR

## Topics of the Day
* Verb Tenses
* Reading

---

## Verb Tenses

| Tense | Definition | Intermediate Helping Verb | Special Note |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Simple Present** | This refers to action that is occurring in the moment now **without** any reference to any other time period. | *(None)* | Multiple actions, though not all in the present, can be grouped into a single one-time present action.<br><br>*Ex:* Manasa is attending SAT class today. She will also attend tomorrow and for the next six weeks.<br>$\downarrow$<br>Manasa is attending SAT class for the next six weeks. |
| **Simple Past** | This refers to a single one-time past action **without** reference to any other time period. | *(None)* | Multiple one-time past actions can be grouped into a single one-time past action.<br><br>*Ex:* Michael Jordan won the NBA championship in the '93-'94 season and won again in the '94-'95 season.<br>$\downarrow$<br>Michael Jordan **won two** NBA championships, in the '93-'94 season and in the '94-'95 season. |
| **"Future"** | This, I will refer to as action that has not occurred. | will<br>would<br>could<br><br>would have<br>could have | $\bullet$ **will** $\rightarrow$ Ashish **will** (H) **buy** (V) a Porsche<br>$\bullet$ **would** $\rightarrow$ Ashish **would** (H) **buy** (V) a Porsche if...<br>$\bullet$ **could** $\rightarrow$ Ashish **could** (H) **buy** (V) a Porsche |

---

| Tense | Definition | Intermediate Helping Verb | Special Note |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Past Perfect** | This refers to action that started in the past, occurred continuously in the past, and stopped in the past. | **had** (singular and plural)<br><br>*Ex:* It **had** (H) **rained** (V) for seven consecutive days.<br><br>*Ex:* They had dated. | To use either of the perfect cases, there needs to be some word that references continuity in action. This can include phrases such as 'since,' 'until,' 'continuously,' 'consecutive,' etc... *(Arrow points from "continuously" to the note below)* |
| **Present Perfect** | This refers to action that started in the past, occurred continuously in the past, and is still happening today. | **has** (singular)<br>**have** (plural)<br><br>It **has** rained **since** March.<br>They **have** dated **since** high school. | Also, if you see a continuity in action words, it can override any other choice of tense. |

---

## Reading vs Reading

* **kiddygarten reading** $\rightarrow$ you simply follow each word but do not make connections between words.
$$\Downarrow$$
* **reading so well** $\rightarrow$ you make connections between words, make connections between sentences, and even foreshadow events.

To do this proper reading approach, you **MUST** annotate the passage (SAT only); however, I do not care what **YOU** think is important.

### Step 1:
Highlight or circle any and all transitional words/phrases and any and all transitional punctuation.

| Transitional Words/Phrases | Transitional Punctuation |
| :--- | :--- |
| $\ast$ but<br>and (if it acts as a coordinating conjunction)<br>however<br>furthermore<br>for example<br>although ...<br>yet<br>if<br>in short<br>though<br>moreover<br>including *(underlined, with arrow pointing to Step 2)*<br>nevertheless<br>even still | :<br>;<br>—<br>...<br>( ) |

### Step 2:
Focus on fully understanding the part after the transition — use it to foreshadow the events of the passage. **Write down your foreshadow.**

### Step 3:
As you continue to read (every single word), confirm or deny your foreshadow. If confirmed, keep reading. If denied, stop and start over.

The following text is adapted from Lewis Carroll's 1889 satirical novel *Sylvie and Bruno*. A crowd has gathered outside a room belonging to the Warden, an official who reports to the Lord Chancellor.

One man, who was more excited than the rest, flung his hat high into the air, **and** *(circled)* shouted (as well as I could make out) "Who roar for the Sub-Warden?" Everybody roared, **but** *(circled)* whether it was for the Sub-Warden, or not, did not clearly appear: some were shouting "Bread!" and some "Taxes!", **but** *(circled)* no one seemed to know what it was they really wanted.

All this I saw from the open window of the Warden's breakfast-saloon, looking across the shoulder of the Lord Chancellor.

"What can it all mean?" he kept repeating to himself. "I never heard such shouting before **—and** *(circled)* at this time of the morning, too! **And** *(circled)* with such unanimity!"

Some animal-behavior studies involve observing wild animals in their natural habitat, **and** *(circled)* some involve capturing wild animals and observing them in a laboratory. Each approach has advantages over the other. In wild studies, researchers can more easily presume that the animals are behaving normally, **and** *(circled)* in lab studies, researchers can more easily control factors that might affect the results. **But** *(circled)* if, for example, the results from a wild study and a lab study of Western scrub-jays (*Aphelocoma californica*) contradict each other *(underlined)*, one or both of the studies must have failed to account for some factor that was relevant to the birds' behavior.

# DAY 5: MATH

## Topics of the Day
* Percents

---

* What is this **standard** that I talked about?
  * the standard is **always the second item listed** in a comparison.
* Percents are **always multiplied** by the original value, the whole value, or the standard value.
* The only time %s should be added is if the %'s **represent different categories of a whole total.**

### Skittles Example
*Ex:* A bag of Skittles contains 25% red, 30% green, 10% orange, 15% purple, and **actually 6 yellow skittles.** **How many red skittles are in the bag?**

| Color | % | Amount |
| :---: | :---: | :---: |
| R | 25 | **?** |
| O | 10 | |
| G | 30 | |
| P | 15 | |
| y | **20** *(in red)* | **6** *(in red)* |
| **T** | **100** | **30** |

**Calculations:**
$$\frac{25\%}{?} = \frac{20\%}{6}$$
$$150 = 20(?)$$
$$\mathbf{? = 7.5} \quad \text{(boxed)}$$

**Alternative Method:**
$$0.20(T) = 6 \implies T = 30$$
$$R = 0.25(30)$$
$$\mathbf{= 7.5} \quad \text{(boxed)}$$

### Store Purchase Example
*Ex:* After receiving a **20% discount** *(orange annotation: $80\% \rightarrow 0.80$)* and paying **10% sales tax** *(red annotation: $110\% \rightarrow 1.10$)*, Viraj buys the entire store for **$S$ dollars.** What was the price of the store before any discounts and taxes?

**Calculations:**
*Effectively, we wish to know the original price of the store ($x$):*
$$x(0.80)(1.10) = S$$
$$x(0.88) = S$$
$$x = \frac{S}{0.88} \cdot \frac{100}{100}$$
$$\mathbf{x = \frac{100S}{88} = \frac{25S}{22}} \quad \text{(boxed)}$$

---

## Finding Percents

There are effectively two types of %s to find:

### 1. Basic %
$$\mathbf{\% = \frac{\text{part}}{\text{whole}} \times 100 \quad \text{OR} \quad \% = \frac{\text{part}}{\text{standard}} \times 100} \quad \text{(boxed)}$$

### 2. % of difference OR % of change
$$\mathbf{\% \text{ change} = \frac{\text{New} - \text{Old}}{\text{Old}} \times 100 \quad \text{OR} \quad \% \text{ change} = \frac{\text{New} - \text{standard}}{\text{standard}} \times 100} \quad \text{(boxed)}$$

*Ex:* Aashni is 16 years old while Ashish is 42 years old.

* **Q1: Aashni is what % younger than Ashish?**
  $$\% \text{ younger} = \frac{\text{Aa} - \text{As} \ (\text{diff})}{\text{As}} \times 100$$
  $$= \frac{16 - 42}{42} \times 100$$
  $$= 61.9\%$$

* **Q2: Aashni's age is what % of Ashish's?**
  $$\% = \frac{\text{Aa}}{\text{As}} \times 100$$
  $$= \frac{16}{42} \times 100$$
  $$= 38.1\%$$

* **Q3: Ashish's age is what % of Aashni's?**
  $$\% = \frac{\text{As}}{\text{Aa}} \times 100$$
  $$= \frac{42}{16} \times 100$$
  $$= 262.5\%$$

* **Q4: Ashish is what % older than Aashni?**
  $$\% \text{ older} = \frac{\text{As} - \text{Aa}}{\text{Aa}} \times 100$$
  $$= \frac{42 - 16}{16} \times 100$$
  $$= 162.5\%$$

---

*Ex:* If I am 25% older than Bob, then Bob is what % younger than me?
$$\text{As} = 1.25x$$
$$\text{Bob} = x$$
$$\% \text{ younger} = \frac{\text{Bob} - \text{Ashish}}{\text{Ashish}} \times 100$$
$$= \frac{x - 1.25x}{1.25x} \times 100$$
$$= \frac{-0.25x}{1.25x} \times 100$$
$$= \frac{1}{5} \times 100 = \mathbf{20\%} \quad \text{(boxed)}$$

---

*Ex:* If I am 25% older than you today, what % older am I in 10 years?

| | Today | In a decade |
| :--- | :---: | :---: |
| **Me** | $1.25x$ | $1.25x + 10$ |
| **You** | $x$ | $x + 10$ |

$$\% \text{ older} = \frac{(1.25x + 10) - (x + 10)}{x + 10} \times 100$$
$$= \frac{0.25x}{x + 10} \times 100$$
$$= \mathbf{\frac{25x}{x + 10}} \quad \text{(boxed)}$$

---

*Ex:* I have **20% less \$ than you.** If I stole \$50 from Matias, then what % is my \$ in terms of **yours** *(circled)*?

| | Currently | After Robbery |
| :--- | :---: | :---: |
| **A:** | $0.8x$ | $0.8x + 50$ |
| **M:** | $x$ | $x - 50$ |

$$\% = \frac{0.8x + 50}{x - 50} \times 100$$
$$= \mathbf{\frac{80x + 5000}{x - 50}} \quad \text{(boxed)}$$

---

## Mixture Problems

| | Acid | Total |
| :--- | :---: | :---: |
| **Given Mix 1** at **$x\%$** of acid | $m\left(\frac{x}{100}\right)$ *(arrow to Total)* | $m$ |
| **Given Mix 2** at **$y\%$** of acid | $(50 - m)\left(\frac{y}{100}\right)$ *(arrow to Total)* | $50 - m$ |
| **Produce a Mix** at **$z\%$** of acid | $50\left(\frac{z}{100}\right)$ *(arrow to Total)* | $50$ |

*(A box highlights the Total column values $m$, $50-m$, and $50$ indicating they sum together, annotated with "fill first")*

*Note:* $x < z < y$

$$\text{Eqn: } m\left(\frac{x}{100}\right) + (50 - m)\left(\frac{y}{100}\right) = 50\left(\frac{z}{100}\right)$$

---

*Ex:* How many liters of a 25% acidic solution must be added to 40L of a 40% acidic solution to make a solution that is 30% acidic?

| | A | T |
| :--- | :---: | :---: |
| **Mix 1 at 25% acidic** | $0.25x$ | $x$ |
| **Mix 2 at 40% acidic** | $0.40(40)$ | $40$ |
| **Make a 30% acidic** | $0.30(x + 40)$ | $x + 40$ |

$$\text{Eqn: } 0.25x + 0.40(40) = 0.30(x + 40)$$
$$0.25x + 16 = 0.30x + 12$$
$$4 = 0.05x$$
$$\mathbf{x = 80L} \quad \text{(boxed)}$$

---

$$p = \frac{a - c}{c} \times 100$$
$$= \frac{\frac{7}{10}b - \frac{1}{0.36}b}{\frac{1}{0.36}b} \times 100$$

Transitions

True Relationship | Common Transitions | Example
------------------|--------------------|--------
present an opposing point of view or balance an idea that is either too positive or too negative.
S1. claim <-- opposes or balances -- S2. another claim that either opposes or balances | however, fortunately, on the other hand, conversely, whereas, while, in contrast, on the contrary, yet. | While Final Fantasy [claim] is often considered the greatest video game franchise, there are many that deem the franchise boring. [Annotation: Arrow pointing from "there are many..." back to the beginning of the sentence, labeled "balances".]
------------------|--------------------|--------
to give emphasis to a claim by adding a specific piece of evidence (1st piece)
S1. claim <-- (1st piece of support) -- S2. a piece of evidence to support | In fact, as a matter of fact, indeed, to illustrate, for instance, for example. | Biology is an amazing scientific subject. Indeed, there are many hands-on applications such as in the field of forensic sciences. [Annotation: "Biology..." labeled as "claim". "Indeed..." labeled as "evidence" and "1st".]
------------------|--------------------|--------
to add new and supporting evidence (2nd + evidence)
S3. a piece of evidence to support (2nd + piece of support) [points to the arrow from the 1st piece of support to S1] | furthermore, in addition, also, moreover, and, too, as well, additionally, not to mention. | Furthermore, Biology blends the best applications of many other disciplines of study.
------------------|--------------------|--------
to show a cause and effect relationship
S1. cause
S2. effect | Consequently, as a result, because, hence, therefore, thus, accordingly, so, for this reason. | "The Backrooms" is a movie devoid of any logic. Therefore, the movie is horrible.

True Relationship | Common Transitions | Example
------------------|--------------------|--------
to concede a prior claim so as to give space to provide a claim that is more important.
S1. claim <-- [as if it is useless] -- S2. another claim | Granted, nevertheless, although, regardless, despite, even if, nonetheless, still, even so. | Although Matias has no desire to study, Ashish plans to lock him in a cage with so many factoring exercises that he has no option but to complete.
------------------|--------------------|--------
to show order or sequencing of events that either move forwards or backwards
S1. an event or action
S2. an event or action that logically moves forwards or backwards | finally, subsequently, previously, afterwards, next, then, eventually, before. | Divyang skipped his SAT class to play basketball. Next, he walked inside to see that his entire wardrobe had been stolen by his infamous SAT instructor.
------------------|--------------------|--------
to show similarity with some constant theme.
S1. claim <-- [some constant theme] -- S2. another claim that has some similarity (some constant theme) to | In the same way, similarly, likewise, by the same token. | Goldfish is the best snack, especially when listening to Ashish's boring lecture. Likewise, popcorn is the best for when watching an exciting movie such as "The Backrooms".

S1. an idea

Note:
  transitions can be expressed in two major ways
         ↓ ICC
  1. Transition S1, S2
  2. S1. Transition, S2

Process
Step 1: read either the ICC or the sentence before the transition and determine its function.
Step 2: read the sentence including the transition and identify its relationship to the prior.
Step 3: identify an appropriate transition
Step 4: do not force

25. 
In the early 1900s, American dancer and choreographer Isadora Duncan revolutionized the world of dance with her free-form movements and expressive style. Despite her success, she struggled to find acceptance in the traditional dance world. Duncan persevered, and is still remembered today as a pioneer of modern contemporary dance.

  A) meanwhile,

Roots of a Quadratic

a root is a value of x that makes the y value of the quadratic equal to 0.

                ┌───────────────────────────┐
                │ ROOT = SOLUTION = ZEROS   │
                └─────────────┬─────────────┘
                              │
               ┌──────────────┴──────────────┐
               ▼                             ▼
         x-intercepts                 Not x-intercepts
         b^2 - 4ac >= 0                b^2 - 4ac < 0
               │                             │
             Real                         Complex
       ┌───────┴───────┐                     │
       ▼               ▼                     ▼
 b^2 - 4ac = 0   b^2 - 4ac > 0         x_1 = a + bi
       │               │               x_cp = a - bi
 Rational +      Distinct                    │
 Repeated              │                     │
       │       ┌───────┴───────┐             │
       ▼       ▼               ▼             │
[Parabola with b^2-4ac =   b^2-4ac =         │
vertex touching perfect    non-perfect       │
x-axis]        square      square            │
       │       │               │             │
       ▼       ▼               ▼             │
    Root =   Rational      Irrational        │
    vertex     │         x_1 = a + √b        │
               ▼         x_cp = a - √b       │
         [Parabola with        │             │
          2 roots on x-axis]   │             │
               │               ▼             ▼
               └───────────────┴─────────────┘
                               │
                               ▼
            In both of these situations, the two
            roots of the quadratic come in conjugate pairs

[Note: If a parabola does not cross the x axis, nor touch the x axis, then the quadratic has complex roots]
[Note: from standard form (y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c), there exists a value called the discriminant (b^2 - 4ac) that helps to classify the roots]

Ex: 3x^2 - 4x + p = 0
In the above quadratic equation, where p is a constant, find the value of p such that the quadratic has no real solution.
  ∴ both roots (both solutions) are complex
  ∴ b^2 - 4ac < 0
  
  (-4)^2 - 4(3)(p) < 0
  16 - 12p < 0
  -12p < -16
  p > 16/12
  ∴ p > 4/3


--- Page 9 ---

finding the roots:
it is possible to find the roots of a quadratic in either vertex form or intercept form.

Intercept form: y = f(x) = a(x - p)(x - q) [Annotation: red '0' above y]
Method: Zero Product Property (zpp)
  ∴ Since a(x - p)(x - q) = 0
    then either (x - p) = 0 or (x - q) = 0
    x = p or x = q

Ex: if -3(x - 2)(x + 7) = 0
    then which of the following must be correct.
    I. x = -3
    II. x = 2
    III. x = -7
    
    [Boxed: None.]

Vertex form: y = f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k [Annotation: red '0' above y]
Method: Square Root Method

General Derivation:                    Example:
  0 = a(x - h)^2 + k                     Ex: y = f(x) = 2(x + 7)^2 + 15 [Annotation: red '0' above y]
  -k = a(x - h)^2                       find all roots.
  -k/a = (x - h)^2                       0 = 2(x + 7)^2 + 15
  (x - h) = ± (√(-k) / √a) * (√a / √a)   -15 = 2(x + 7)^2
  (x - h) = ± √(-ak) / a                 -15/2 = (x + 7)^2
  x = h ± √(-ak) / a                     (x + 7) = ± (√(-15) / √2) * (√2 / √2)
  [Boxed: x = (ah ± √(-ak)) / a]         (x + 7) = ± i√30 / 2
                                         x = -7 ± i√30 / 2
                                         [Boxed: x = (-14 ± i√30) / 2]


--- Page 10 ---

Standard form: y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c
Technically, there are no ways to find the roots of a quadratic in standard form.

                 ax^2 + bx + c = 0
                   ┌─────┴─────┐
                   ▼           ▼
                FACTOR        CTS (Completing the Square)
                   │           │
                   ▼           ▼
             Intercept form  Vertex
               (zpp)      (Square Root Method)
                   │           │
                   ▼           ▼
                 Roots       Roots

Quadratic Formula
y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c [Annotation: red '0' above y]
0 = ax^2 + bx + c
0 = (ax^2 + bx) + c
0 = a(x^2 + b/a x) + c
0 = a(x^2 + b/a x + b^2/(4a^2)) + c - a(b^2/(4a^2))
0 = a(x + b/(2a))^2 + c - b^2/(4a)

a(x + b/(2a))^2 = b^2/(4a) - c
a(x + b/(2a))^2 = (b^2 - 4ac) / (4a)
(x + b/(2a))^2 = (b^2 - 4ac) / (4a^2)
x + b/(2a) = ± √(b^2 - 4ac) / (2a)

[Boxed: x = (-b ± √(b^2 - 4ac)) / (2a)]  QF

--- Page 13 ---

Percents:

Note: a percent must be converted to a factor to be used.
to convert a % to a factor, either divide the % by 100 or move the decimal place two units to the left.

  %   | factor
──────┼───────────────────────
 20%  | 20/100 = 1/5 or 0.20
 0.5% | 0.5/100 = 1/200 or 0.005
  x%  | [Boxed: x/100] or 0.01(x)

When given a % in the problem, you need to know which version of the % to use

[Boxed: Version 1] the % is of some total or of some standard
  use the % as is
  and convert to a factor

[Boxed: Version 2] the % is of some increase to some total or some standard
  use (100 + %)
  and then convert to some factor

[Boxed: Version 3] the % is of some decrease to some total or some standard
  (use 100 - %)
  and then convert to a factor.

### Page 1

**READING / GRAMMAR**
