### Page 2

**Punctuation**

**Commas**

**Use 1:** to separate the items of a list
... item1, item2, item3, and item4...
*(Note with arrow pointing to the comma after item3: "wrong")*

**Oxford Comma:** the oxford comma is not mandatory in writing. As such, you will not be tested on its use. However, you will be tested on its location - it is located before the "and".

**Note:** the items of a list can also be separated by the use of a semi-colon (;). You do so if the items of the list themselves contain commas.
*(Annotation: "this is the only exception where a semi-colon can be joined with 'and'")*

**Ex:** Ruhi plans to read Title 1, description 1; Title 2, description 2; and Title 3, description 3.
*   Item 1: Title 1, description 1
*   Item 2: Title 2, description 2
*   Item 3: Title 3, description 3

**use 2:** to join two full sentences with the use of a coordinating conjunction.
*   ∴ two expressed subjects
*   two expressed verbs
*   two full ideas

**form:** sentence 1 , (cc) sentence 2 .

Where your coordinating conjunctions (CC) are your **FANBOYS**:
*   F - for
*   A - and
*   N - nor
*   B - but
*   O - or
*   Y - yet
*   S - so

**Ex:** Ashish plans to buy a new car and will have it shipped to him. [✓]
*(Note on first sentence: S1 = Ashish, V1 = plans, V2 = will have)*
Ashish plans to buy a new car, and he will have it shipped to him. [✗]
*(Note on second sentence: S1 = Ashish, V1 = plans, S2 = he, V2 = will have)*

---

### Page 3

**Correctly Joining 2 Independent Clauses**
1. IC . IC
2. IC ; IC
3. IC , cc IC
4. IC : IC

**Incorrectly Join 2 Independent Clauses**
1. IC IC (no punctuation) (run-on sentence)
2. IC , IC (no coordinating conjunction) (comma splice)
3. IC ; cc IC
4. IC , false cc IC

**Most common false cc is "however"**

[✗] Tom is dating Emily, however he is attracted to Julie.
*(Note: "however" has annotation "cc = false". "Tom is dating Emily" is labeled "Sentence", and "he is attracted to Julie." is labeled "Sentence")*

[✓] **Correctly using "however"**
1. IC. However, IC  |  Tom is dating Emily. However, he is attracted to Julie.
2. IC; however, IC
3. IC. IC, however.  |  Tom is dating Emily. He is attracted to Julie, however.
4. IC; IC, however.
5. IC. IC 1/2, however, IC 2/2  |  Tom is dating Emily. He, however, is attracted to Julie.
6. IC; IC 1/2, however, IC 2/2

**Careful:** the SAT often tests which sentence the "however" is part of - it needs to be a part of the sentence performing the opposition.

**Use 3:** to join a dependent clause with an independent clause; however, the use of the comma depends on the location of the dependent clause.

*   **If** the dependent clause comes before the Independent clause, then you **MUST** use a comma.
*   **If** the dependent clause comes after the Independent clause, then you **MIGHT** use a comma. You do so if the dependent clause begins with an adverb of concession.

Your concessional adverbs:

---

### Page 4

supposing it rains, the party will be cancelled.
The party will be cancelled supposing it rains.
Even though I have no water, Vishesh keeps taking my water bottle.
Viheesh keeps taking my water bottle, even though I have no water.

**USE 4:** before and after a middle comma clause (also known as an appositive or non-essential clause)

**Note:** a middle comma clause can actually be punctuated in 3 ways:
1. IC 1/2 , mcc , IC 2/2
2. IC 1/2 - mcc - IC 2/2
3. IC 1/2 ( mcc ) IC 2/2
*No mixing punctuation*

**Note: the SAT/ACT Trap**
Subject, ~~~~~~~~~~
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~ - Verb
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~.

**use 5:** to enter an ending comma clause (ecc)
*   **Recall:** most ending comma clauses begin with an -ing adverb.
*   **Recall:** ending comma clauses are usually defining or clarifying phrases.

**Ex:** Vishesh keeps taking the water bottle, hoping that he will find water.

**use 6:** between two adjectives that describe the same noun.
*   **Ex:** Ashish is wearing a big, red t-shirt.
*   **Ex:** Viraj is wearing a light gray shirt.

---

### Page 5

**Colon (:)**
**Note:** to use a colon, there MUST be a full sentence before the colon.

**USE 1:** to enter a list with no introductory phrase to the list.
*   No for example  \
*   No such as       \
*   No to illustrate  > No introductory phrase
*   No including     /
*   No with         /

**Ex:** Aashni eats a big breakfast: waffles, pancakes, cereal, and fruit.
**Note:** do not ever enter a list with a semicolon.
**Note:** you can enter a list with no punctuation
*   **Ex:** Aashni eats waffles, pancakes, cereal, and fruit every morning.
**Note:** you can use an introductory phrase as long as you don't enter the list with a colon.
*   **Ex:** Aashni eats a big breakfast including pancakes, cereal, waffles, and fruit.

**USE 2:** to add information at the end of a sentence about the sentence.
*   **Ex:** Ashish studied eight long years for the SAT: he scored a perfect 1600.

---

**DASH (-)**

**USE 1:** before and after a MCC

**use 2:** to add information at the end of a sentence about the sentence; however, this information is usually shocking.
*   **Ex:** Ashish studied eight long years for the SAT - he scored a 420.

---

### Page 6

**Semi-colon (;)**

**USE 1:** to separate the items of a list when the items themselves contain commas.

**use 2:** to join two full sentences without the use of a cc.

---

**Apostrophe (')**

**USE 1:** to make a contraction, the joining of two words into one.
*   ~~CONTRACTIONS~~
*   the only acceptable contraction is "it is" = "it's"

**use 2:** to show possession
**Note:** there are non-apostrophe possessives:
*   its
*   their
*   his
*   her
*   hers
*   my
*   mine
*   our
*   ours

*(Note in cloud: "its'", "its's", "itss" -> Not words)*

**To show possessive with an apostrophe:**
*   If the word does not end in -s:
    *   's
*   If the word ends in s:
    *   Simple noun: s' (e.g., cars', car's)
    *   Proper noun: s's (e.g., Jesus's)

### Page 8

**Quadratics**

A quadratic is a relationship between y and the square of x (x^2).
If the quadratic passes through the origin, then y ~ x^2.
*   ∴ if x is doubled, y is quadrupled
*   If x is tripled, y is scaled by factor of 9.
*   If y is doubled, x is scaled by √2.

All quadratics produce a curve called a parabola, a U shaped curve that opens either up or down.
*   Graph of parabola opening up: (a > 0)
*   Graph of parabola opening down: (a < 0)

All parabolas have two characteristics:
*   a vertex AND
*   an axis of symmetry

**Vertex:** this is a coordinate point (xv, yv) that corresponds to either the maximum point of the parabola (if parabola opens down) or the minimum point of the parabola (if parabola opens up).
*   Graph of parabola opening up: Vertex (minimum point) at bottom
*   Graph of parabola opening down: Vertex (maximum point) at top

**Note:** for any question involving a quadratic that refers to either maximum or minimum, simply find the vertex!!

---

### Page 9

**Axis of Symmetry:** this is an imaginary vertical line that passes through the parabola's vertex, dividing the parabola into two equal halves.
*(Graphs show AoS line through vertex with symmetric halves)*

All quadratics can be expressed in one of three forms:
1.  **Standard form:** y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c
2.  **Vertex form:** y = f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k *(h is opp, k is norm)*
3.  **Intercept form:** y = f(x) = a(x - p)(x - q) *(p is opp, q is opp)*

**SAT Note:** each form gives, as a constant or a coefficient, the __________ of the parabola.
*   Standard form (c) -> y-intercept
*   Vertex form (h, k) -> vertex (h, k)
*   Intercept form (p, q) -> x-intercepts (p, 0) (q, 0)

**Finding the vertex:**

**Vertex form:** y = f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k
*   Vertex: xv = h, yv = k
*   V(h, k)

**Standard form:** y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c
*   Vertex: xv = -b / 2a, yv = f(-b / 2a)
*   V(-b / 2a, f(-b / 2a))

**Intercept form:** y = f(x) = a(x - p)(x - q)
*   Vertex: xv = (p + q) / 2, yv = f((p + q) / 2)
*   V((p + q)/2, f((p + q)/2))

**Finding the Axis of Symmetry:**
x = xv

**Ex:** y = f(x) = -2(x + 3)(x - 7)
xv = (-3 + 7) / 2 = 2
yv = f(2) = -2(5)(-5) = 50
V: (2, 50)

---

### Page 11

**Question 6**
A quadratic function models the height, in feet, of an object above the ground in terms of the time, in seconds, after the object is launched off an elevated surface. The model indicates the object hits the ground 16 seconds after being launched; the object reaches its maximum height of 1,296 feet above the ground 7 seconds after being launched. Based on the model, at what time after the object is launched, in seconds, does the object first reach a height of 972 feet?

*Handwritten Notes & Work:*
*   "quadratic function" -> Vertex (7, 1296)
*   "height" -> DV (y)
*   "time" -> IV (x)
*   "hits the ground 16 seconds after being launched" -> (16, 0)
*   "maximum height of 1,296 feet... 7 seconds after" -> Vertex (7, 1296)
*   "reach a height of 972 feet" -> y = 972 (to find x)
*   Arrow from (16, 0) to equation below ("to find a")

*Calculations:*
y = a(x - 7)^2 + 1296
0 = a(9)^2 + 1296   *(16 - 7 = 9)*
-1296 = 81a
a = -16

y = -16(x - 7)^2 + 1296

972 = -16(x - 7)^2 + 1296
-324 = -16(x - 7)^2
(x - 7)^2 = 20.25
x - 7 = ± 4.5
x = 7 ± 4.5
x = 2.5, 11.5
*(2.5 is circled)*

# --- Page 1 ---

READING / GRAMMAR                                                       DAY # 1

TOPICS OF THE DAY
* The Useless Clauses

# --- Page 2 ---

The 3 Useless Clauses

What is absolutely necessary for there to be a complete sentence.
[x] Subject
[x] Verb

Ex: Tom plays. (Sentence)
     (s)   (v)

[Useless Clause 1] Relative Clause
                   simply a word that can be used to replace a noun. -> pronoun
this is a clause that begins with a relative pronoun and will often continue until a verb is reached.

there are 5 relative pronouns:
[x] who
[x] whom
[x] whose
[x] which
[x] that

Note: that does not use a comma, neither before nor after

                 RP
Ex: Tom plays an instrument that was given to him by his grandfather.
                            \_______________________/
                                 Relative Clause

[Useless Clause 2] Comma Clauses

there are 3 types of comma clauses
Type 1: Introductory comma clause (ICC) - dependent clauses
Most Introductory comma clauses begin with a subordinating conjunction, a preposition, or an -ing adverb.

Note: Tom is playing. [is] / [playing] (playing is crossed out)
any word ending in -ing is never a verb.

# --- Page 3 ---

Form: Introductory comma clause, Sentence

  (sc)
Whereas others are studying for the SAT, Tom is playing.
\____________________________________/
       Introductory Comma Clause

Type 2: Ending Comma Clause (ECC) - defining or clarifying phrase
most ending comma clauses will begin with an -ing adverb
Form: Sentence, Ending Comma Clause

                                   ing-adverb
                                       |
Ex: Tom plays football, spending more than twenty hours per week practicing.
                        \__________________________________________________/
                                        Ending comma clause

Type 3: Middle Comma Clause (MCC) - appositive or non-essential clause
this is a clause that disrupts a full sentence. Usually, the first part of the sentence contains the subject and the second part of the sentence contains the verb.

Form: SEN, Middle Comma Clause, TENCE
       |                           |
       v                           v
  usually, the subject        usually, the verb is
  of the sentence is found    found here.
  here

Tricky: a middle comma clause can actually be found in an introductory comma clause.
Ex: During the hail storm, the first ever recorded in the city, Manasa hit her head.

                       Middle Comma Clause
                       /_________________\
Ex: Tom, usually very uncreative, wrote a beautiful song that was awarded first place in the talent competition.

# --- Page 4 ---

Note: a sentence could contain zero, one, two, or all three comma clauses.

     [ICC]                           (S)          [MCC]
Ex: Beyond studying for his academics, Tom, currently the valedictorian
    _________________________________  ___  ___________________________
    
                    (V) [SENTENCE]
    of his high school, enjoys playing furiously with his many video games,
    __________________  ________________________ __________________________
    
    [ECC]
    much to the dislike of his strict parents.
    __________________________________________

[Useless Clause 3] prepositional phrase

this is a phrase that begins with a preposition and continues until a noun is reached, called the direct object or the object of the preposition.

                 P            N         (P)            (N)
Ex: Tom plays football against the wishes of his strict parents.
                       \________________/ \____________________/
                              PP                   PP

Consider the following sentence:
Gluing beautiful pieces of artwork [of artwork is crossed out] that are drawn by classmates [that are drawn by classmates is crossed out] is an experience Tom [Tom is crossed out] will not forget.

[ Gluing ]

Note: a subject of a sentence can NEVER be found within a relative clause, a comma clause, nor a prepositional phrase.

# --- Page 5 ---

Because the above clauses are effectively useless, I want you to get into the habit of crossing them out. To confirm that you have done so correctly, confirm that the balance is still a sentence.

# --- Page 8 ---

MATH                                                                    DAY # 1

TOPICS OF THE DAY
* linear functions

# --- Page 9 ---

linear functions
a linear function is a relationship between x (independent variable) and y (dependent variable) such that for each additional constant (same) change in x, there exists a corresponding constant change in y.

[Graph showing steps down:
- from (x_0, y_0) go right +2, down -1
- go right +2, down -1
- go right +4, down -2]

Note: the ratio of the constant change in y to the constant change in x is called the slope

slope formula:  m = (y_2 - y_1) / (x_2 - x_1)

Since the slope of a line is a ratio, there are several instances where using the slope as a proportion is effective

Instance 1: given three collinear points
               m_12 = m_13

Ex:
[Graph showing a line passing through (0,0) [Pt 1], (n,4) [Pt 2], and (6,m) [Pt 3]]

find a relationship b/t n and m.
     m_12 = m_13
(y_2 - y_1) / (x_2 - x_1) = (y_3 - y_1) / (x_3 - x_1)
(4 - 0) / (n - 0) = (m - 0) / (6 - 0)
4 / n = m / 6

[ mn = 24 ]  or  [ m = 24 / n ]  or  [ n = 24 / m ]

NOTE: parallel lines are lines that have the same slope
      perpendicular lines are lines whose slopes product to -1 -> the two slopes are negative reciprocals of each other

# --- Page 10 ---

Ex:
[Graph showing two perpendicular lines intersecting at (3,s), one line passing through (1,4), and the other through (5,3)]

find s
m_1 = (s - 4) / (3 - 1)        m_2 = (s - 3) / (3 - 5)
    = (s - 4) / 2                  = (s - 3) / -2

((s - 4) / 2) * ((s - 3) / -2) = -1
(s - 4)(s - 3) / -4 = -1
[ (s - 4)(s - 3) = 4 ]

Instance 2: if a problem demonstrates linear relationships and discusses changes in the dependent and independent variable

Ex: The number of ounces of spaghetti sauce needed for a certain number of ounces of spaghetti can be modeled as:
               ss = 2/3 (s) + 4

where ss is the number of ounces of spaghetti sauce and s is the number of ounces of spaghetti

If I were to add 15 ounces of spaghetti, then how many additional ounces of sauce would I need?

DV: number of ounces of spaghetti sauce
IV: number of ounces of spaghetti
slope: 2/3

2/3 = x/15
3x = 30
[ x = 10 oz ]

# --- Page 11 ---

linear function modelling:

[ model a horizontal line ] <------------> (m = 0)
        |
     [ y = # ]

[ model a vertical line ]   ^              (m = undefined)
        |                   |
     [ x = # ]              v

[ model a diagonal line ]
  there are 3 forms
  1. slope-intercept form   [ y = mx + b ]
     Best for: graphing a line
               analysis of a line
               
  2. point-slope form       [ y - y_1 = m(x - x_1) ]
     Best for modelling
     
  3. Standard form          [ Ax + By = C ]      A should normally be (+)
     Best for word problems and                  A, B, C should normally be integers
     for standardized testing

Note: though the point-slope form is best for modeling, the answer is often in slope-intercept form or standard form. However, it is easy to convert from point-slope form to either slope intercept or standard.

# --- Page 12 ---

                     [ point-slope form ]
                    y - 4 = -2/5 (x + 1)
                    
                    s1) If the slope is a fraction,
                        multiply all terms by the LCD.
                    5y - 20 = -2(x + 1)
                    
                    s2) distribute the x-side
                    5y - 20 = -2x - 2
                    /               \
                   /                 \
  [ slope-intercept form ]         [ standard form ]
  s3) Isolate y                    s3) get x and y together
  5y = -2x + 18                    2x + 5y = 18
  [ y = -2/5 x + 18/5 ]            s4) Confirm conditions for A, B, C.
                                   [ 2x + 5y = 18 ]

full modelling:
s1) identify both the dependent variable (y) and the independent variable (x)
    Note: if you see the phrase "as a function of," then the term before is the DV, while the term after is the IV.
s2) identify either the slope and one coordinate point or two coordinate points
s3) if necessary, find the slope                 s4) Using the slope and either coordinate point,
    [ m = (y_2 - y_1) / (x_2 - x_1) ]                model the line in point-slope form.
                                                     y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)
                                                     ^   ^   ^
                                                     |   |   |
                                                    (pointing to y_1, m, x_1)

                                  s5) Convert

# --- Page 13 ---

Ex: In 2012, a scientist recorded 12 foxes and 40 rabbits in a forest.
    Ten years later, he recorded 20 foxes and 52 rabbits. Assuming a
    linear relationship, model the number of foxes as a function of
    the number of rabbits.

    DV: the number of foxes             in 2012: (40, 12)  [labeled x_1, y_1]
    IV: the number of rabbits           in 2022: (52, 20)

    m = (y_2 - y_1) / (x_2 - x_1)
      = (20 - 12) / (52 - 40)
      = 8 / 12
      = 2/3

    y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)
    [ f - 12 = 2/3 (r - 40) ]           point-slope form
    
    3f - 36 = 2(r - 40)
    3f - 36 = 2r - 80
    /                 \
   /                   \
  3f = 2r - 44         (-2r + 3f = -44)(-1)
  [ f = 2/3 r - 44/3 ] [ 2r - 3f = 44 ]
  slope-int. form      standard form

Ex: In 1954, a can of Coke cost $0.50. By 2012, the same can of
    Coke would cost $2.00. Assuming linear growth in a can of Coke,
    model, in all three forms, the cost of a can of Coke as a function
    of the number of years since 1950.

# --- Page 14 ---

slope interpretation:
first, ensure the slope is a fraction (a/b)

Interpretation:
                  (m = +)       (m = -)
the value of the DV will increase or decrease by |a|
for each additional b units of the IV

Ex: In 2012, a scientist recorded 12 foxes and 40 rabbits in a forest.
    Ten years later, he recorded 20 foxes and 52 rabbits. Assuming a
    linear relationship, model the number of foxes as a function of
    the number of rabbits.

    DV: the number of foxes             in 2012: (40, 12)  [labeled x_1, y_1]
    IV: the number of rabbits           in 2022: (52, 20)

    m = (y_2 - y_1) / (x_2 - x_1)
      = (20 - 12) / (52 - 40)
      = 8 / 12
      = 2/3

    y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)
    [ f - 12 = 2/3 (r - 40) ]           point-slope form
    
    3f - 36 = 2(r - 40)
    3f - 36 = 2r - 80
    /                 \
   /                   \
  3f = 2r - 44         (-2r + 3f = -44)(-1)
  [ f = 2/3 r - 44/3 ] [ 2r - 3f = 44 ]
  slope-int. form      standard form

Interpret the slope (2/3): the number of foxes will increase by 2
                           for each additional 3 rabbits in the forest.

# --- Page 15 ---

the price of a can of coke will increase by $3 for each additional 116 years after 1950.

**Shlok Bhatt**: Hello everyone,

ATL Cyber Clash (CTF) will take place on April 18, Saturday, from 10:30 AM to 2:00 PM at Sharon Forks Library. I’m co-hosting this event in collaboration with the LHS Cybersecurity Club and Alpharetta High School.

This is a Capture the Flag cybersecurity competition where you will work in teams of two to solve challenges in areas like web exploitation, cryptography, and forensics. The competition is designed to be beginner-friendly, so no prior experience is required.

The event is open to all high school students, and there will be cash prizes for top teams. Registration is open until April 17.

You can sign up here: https://luma.com/vqckv6aj

Join us—whether you’re new to cybersecurity or already experienced, this is a great chance to learn, challenge yourself, and compete!!

**MATH**

**TOPICS OF THE DAY**
* Triangles, Part 2

---

### PAGE 8

#### Trigonometry
The study of the ratios of two sides of a right triangle as a function of one of the right triangle's acute angles.

```
      A (acute angle)
      /\
     /  \
    /    \  Hypotenuse / Hypoteneuse
   /      \
  /________\
 B          C (acute angle)
```
* Angle at **A** is $x^\circ$. Side **AB** is "adjacent" (pink) / "opposite" (yellow).
* Angle at **B** is a right angle.
* Angle at **C** is $(90-x)^\circ$. Side **BC** is "opposite" (pink) / "adjacent" (yellow).

$$\text{trig function (acute angle)} = \text{ratio}$$

There are six trigonometric functions:

| Core 3 | Reciprocal 3 |
| :--- | :--- |
| $\sin(\text{AA}) = \text{opp}/\text{hyp}$ | $\csc(\text{AA}) = \frac{1}{\sin(\text{AA})} = \text{hyp}/\text{opp}$ |
| $\cos(\text{AA}) = \text{adj}/\text{hyp}$ | $\sec(\text{AA}) = \frac{1}{\cos(\text{AA})} = \text{hyp}/\text{adj}$ |
| $\tan(\text{AA}) = \text{opp}/\text{adj}$ | $\cot(\text{AA}) = \frac{1}{\tan(\text{AA})} = \text{adj}/\text{opp}$ |

**Ex:** $\tan(A) = 4/7 = \text{opp}/\text{adj}$
```
   A
   /|
  / |  adj = 7m
 /  |
/___|
C   B
 opp = 4m
```
$$\text{Hyp}^2 = (7\text{m})^2 + (4\text{m})^2$$
$$\text{Hyp}^2 = 49\text{m}^2 + 16\text{m}^2$$
$$\text{Hyp}^2 = 65\text{m}^2$$
$$\text{Hyp} = \text{m}\sqrt{65}$$

---

#### SAT Focus
**What is the relationship between sine and cosine?**
They are co-functions, meaning that they produce equivalent ratios (meaning they are equal) when evaluated at complementary angles.
$$\therefore \sin(x^\circ) = \cos(90 - x^\circ) \quad \text{OR}$$
$$\sin(A) = \cos(B) \text{ iff } A + B = 90^\circ$$

**Ex:** $\cos(3x+10) = \sin(5x-20)$
$$\therefore (3x+10) + (5x-20) = 90$$
$$8x - 10 = 90$$
$$8x = 100$$
$$x = 100/8 = 25/2 \text{ or } 12.5$$

**Ex:** $\cos(37^\circ) = \sin(?)$
$$? = 53^\circ$$

---

#### ACT Focus

##### Using trigonometry to find a missing side
* $\square$ the triangle must be right
* $\square$ Given only one side and effectively both acute angles

* **Step 1:** choose an acute angle
* **Step 2:** next step - label all three sides in relation to your chosen angle
* **Step 3:** based on the labeling of the given side and the missing side, choose a trig function
* **Step 4:** setup the trig equation
$$\text{trig}(\text{AA}) = \frac{X}{V} \quad \text{OR} \quad \text{trig}(\text{AA}) = \frac{V}{X}$$
$$X = V \cdot \text{trig}(\text{AA}) \quad \text{OR} \quad X = \frac{V}{\text{trig}(\text{AA})}$$

##### Using trigonometry to find a missing angle
* $\square$ again, the triangle must be right
* $\square$ Given at least two sides

* **Step 1:** based on the angle you wish to find, label all three sides
* **Step 2:** based on the labeling of the two sides, choose a trig function
* **Step 3:** setup formula
$$\text{trig}(X) = \text{ratio}$$
$$X = \text{trig}^{-1}(\text{ratio})$$

---

### PAGE 9

#### Isosceles Triangles
Any triangle that has two sides being congruent, called **legs**, **AND** also two angles being congruent, called **base angles**. The non-congruent side is called the **base**. The non-congruent angle is called the **vertex angle**.

```
       /\ Vt (vertex angle)
      /  \
 leg /    \ leg
    /______\
   BA  base BA
```

#### Equilateral Triangle:
A triangle in which all sides are $\cong$ and all angles are $\cong$, each measuring $60^\circ$.

```
         /\ 60° (split into 30° and 30°)
      s /  \ s
       /|h |\
      /_|_ |_\
    60° s/2  60°
         s
```
* Height (altitude) $h = \frac{s\sqrt{3}}{2}$

#### Area of Triangles:
Generally: $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$

$$A_{\text{eq}\triangle} = \frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$$

$$A_{\text{SSS}} = \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)} \quad \text{[Heron's Formula]}$$
$$\text{where } s = \text{semi-perimeter} = \frac{1}{2}(a+b+c)$$

$$A_{\text{SAS}} = \frac{1}{2} s_1 s_2 \sin(\theta)$$

---

### PAGE 10

#### Similar Triangles:
Two triangles are similar ($\sim$) iff all three corresponding angles are $\cong$ and all three corresponding sides are proportional.

```
     A                 X
    /\                /\
   /  \              /  \
  /____\            /____\ Z
 B      C          Y
```
$$\triangle ABC \sim \triangle XYZ$$
$$\therefore \angle A \cong \angle X, \quad \angle B \cong \angle Y, \quad \angle C \cong \angle Z$$
$$\therefore \frac{\text{shortest side of } \triangle 1}{\text{shortest side of } \triangle 2} = \frac{\text{middle side of } \triangle 1}{\text{middle side of } \triangle 2} = \frac{\text{largest side of } \triangle 1}{\text{largest side of } \triangle 2}$$

To prove two triangles are similar, you can use the:
* **Angle-Angle Postulate:** if two angles of one triangle are congruent to two angles of another $\triangle$, then the two $\triangle$s are similar.
* **OR SSS**
* **OR SAS**

#### Special Similar Triangles
1. **Back to Back + Parallel** (hourglass shaped vertical triangles sharing a vertex with parallel top and bottom bases)
2. **Intermeshed + Parallel** (nested triangles sharing the top vertex with parallel horizontal bases)

---

### PAGE 11

3. **Triple Right $\triangle$**
```
      /\
     /  \
    /  c \
   /______\
      a  b
```
$$\frac{a}{c} = \frac{c}{b}$$

*(Diagrams showing three similar right triangles of small, medium, and large sizes drawn separately).*

#### Proving $\triangle$'s Congruent:
1. SSS
2. SAS
3. ASA
4. AAS
5. HL

### Page 3: Words in Context

Words in Context

S1. the definition of the word that is to be chosen will be found contextually in the text - highlight this definition
S2. You write a word that could fit this definition
S3. Choose that word or a similie.

### Page 7: Rhetorical Synthesis

Rhetorical Synthesis:

Given bulleted research on topics and asked to synthesize (to create in an orderly fashion for efficiency) the information to support some claim given in the question header.

Step 1: go through each bullet and identify each as either background (B) or evidential (E)
Step 2: read the question header and highlight the claim you wish to support
Step 3: choose the bullets you would use to support the claim.

### Page 14: Triangles

**Angles of a triangle**

* [x] all triangles have three internal angles whose sum is 180°.
  * Formula: $I_1 + I_2 + I_3 = 180^\circ$
  * *(Diagram of a triangle with interior angles $I_1, I_2, I_3$)*
* [x] reflexive angle - this is an angle on the outside of the shape that helps to complete a full circle with its corresponding interior angle
  * Formula: $R_n + I_n = 360^\circ$
  * *(Diagram showing interior angles $I_1, I_2, I_3$ and exterior circular arrows for $R_1, R_2, R_3$ in red)*
* [x] Exterior Angle - this is an angle on the outside of the shape that forms a linear pair with its corresponding interior angle
  * *(Diagram with triangle showing exterior angles $E_1, E_2, E_3$ as side extensions)*

* [x] **True for all shapes:**
  * [x] the sum of all exterior angles is 360°
  * [x] each exterior angle forms a linear pair with the corresponding interior angle
    * $\therefore I_n + E_n = 180^\circ$

* [x] **True for only triangles:**
  * each exterior angle is the sum of the two remote interior angles
    * $\therefore E_1 = I_2 + I_3$
    * $E_2 = I_1 + I_3$
    * $E_3 = I_1 + I_2$

**Ex:**
*(Diagram showing parallel lines intersected by transversal lines forming a triangle with interior angles $50^\circ$ and $20^\circ$)*
* $E = x^\circ$
* $x = 50 + 20$
* $x = 70^\circ$

**Sides of a Triangle:**

all triangles satisfy the triangle inequality relationships
* [x] the shortest side of a triangle is opposite the smallest angle of the triangle AND the middle side of a triangle is opposite the middle angle of the triangle AND the largest side of a triangle is opposite the largest angle of the triangle.
  * *(Diagram of a triangle with vertex labeled M and arrows pointing to the opposite sides)*
* [x] the third side of any triangle must be larger than the positive difference of the other two sides AND smaller than the sum of the other two sides
  * Formula: $|1^{st} - 2^{nd}| < 3^{rd} < 1^{st} + 2^{nd}$
  * **Ex:** 1m, 2m, 3m
    * $3 - 1 < 2 < 1 + 3$
    * $2 < 2 < 4$  [X] *(Checkbox marked with X, indicating invalid triangle)*
  * **Ex:** a triangle has sides of 8 and 11. find all possible values for its perimeter.
    * [ $22 < P < 38$ ]
    * *(Diagram of triangle with sides 8, 11, and x)*
    * $11 - 8 < x < 11 + 8$
    * $3 < x < 19$
    * $P = 8 + 11 + x$
    * $P = 19 + x$
    * $P_{min} = 19 + 3 = 22$
    * $P_{max} = 19 + 19 = 38$
    * $\therefore 22 < P < 38$

---

### Page 15: Right Triangles

**Right Triangles:**

A right triangle is one that has exactly one right angle, whose measure is 90°, and whose two other angles are acute and complementary.
*(Diagram of a right triangle: vertical side "leg 1", horizontal side "leg 2", hypotenuse "Hypotenuse", right angle $90^\circ$, acute angles $x^\circ$ and $(90 - x)^\circ$)*
**Note:** yes, the largest side of a right triangle, the side opposite the right angle, is called the hypotenuse. The other two sides are called legs.

Yes, a right triangle does satisfy the triangle inequality relationships. However, a right triangle will more strictly satisfy the pythagorean theorem. This theorem states: $(\text{leg } 1)^2 + (\text{leg } 2)^2 = (\text{hypotenuse})^2$
where the legs are often denoted as a and b and the hypotenuse as c.
* $\therefore (a)^2 + (b)^2 = (c)^2$

you can apply the pythagorean theorem iff:
* [x] the triangle must be right AND
* [x] you know exactly two of the sides OR
* [x] you know exactly one side and the other two are known in terms of one variable OR
* [x] all three sides are expressed in terms of one variable

**Ex:**
*(Diagram of a right triangle with legs $x$ and $x + 7$, and hypotenuse $x + 8$)*
* $x \to a = 5$
* $x + 7 \to b = 12$
* $x + 8 \to c = 13$
* Equations:
  * $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$
  * $(x)^2 + (x + 7)^2 = (x + 8)^2$
  * $x^2 + x^2 + 14x + 49 = x^2 + 16x + 64$
  * $2x^2 + 14x + 49 = x^2 + 16x + 64$
  * $x^2 - 2x - 15 = 0$
  * $(x - 5)(x + 3) = 0$
  * [ $x = 5$ ] or ~~$x = -3~~

if all three sides of a right triangle are integers, then the set of all three sides is called a Pythagorean Triple

**Common Triples**
| 3-4-5 | 5-12-13 | 7-24-25 | 8-15-17 |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| 6-8-10 | 10-24-26 | 14-48-50 | 16-30-34 |
| 9-12-15 | 15-36-39 | | |

---

### Page 16: Special Right Triangles & Trigonometry

**The Special Right Triangles:**

**30° - 60° - 90°**
*(Diagram of a $30^\circ - 60^\circ - 90^\circ$ triangle with short leg $s$, hypotenuse $2s$, and long leg $s\sqrt{3}$)*
**Note:** any right triangle in which the hypotenuse is double a leg must be a 30° - 60° - 90° right triangle.

**Ex:**
*(Diagram of a $30^\circ - 60^\circ - 90^\circ$ triangle with long leg $12$, short leg $s$, and hypotenuse $2s$)*
* $s\sqrt{3} = 12$
* $s = \frac{12}{\sqrt{3}} = 4\sqrt{3}$ (in an orange box)
* Short leg: $s = 4\sqrt{3}$
* Hypotenuse: $2s = 8\sqrt{3}$

**45° - 45° - 90°**
* [x] Isosceles Right Triangle
* [x] Diagonal(s) of a Square
* *(Two diagrams of squares showing diagonals dividing them into $45^\circ$ angles)*
* *(Diagram of a $45^\circ - 45^\circ - 90^\circ$ triangle with legs $s, s$ and hypotenuse $s\sqrt{2}$)*

**Trigonometry:**

the study of the ratios of the sides of a right triangle as a function of one of the acute angles

**Terminology:**
*(Diagram of a right triangle with acute angles A and B, right angle C. For angle A: vertical side is adjacent, horizontal side is opposite. For angle B: vertical side is opposite, horizontal side is adjacent)*
* SOH CAH TOA

there are six trigonometric functions:
* **Core 3**
  * $\sin(A) = \frac{\text{opp}}{\text{hyp}}$
  * $\cos(A) = \frac{\text{adj}}{\text{hyp}}$
  * $\tan(A) = \frac{\text{opp}}{\text{adj}}$
* **Reciprocal 3**
  * $\csc(A) = \frac{1}{\sin(A)} = \frac{\text{hyp}}{\text{opp}}$
  * $\sec(A) = \frac{1}{\cos(A)} = \frac{\text{hyp}}{\text{adj}}$
  * $\cot(A) = \frac{1}{\tan(A)} = \frac{\text{adj}}{\text{opp}}$

**Ex:**
*(Diagram of a right triangle ABC with vertical leg 15m, horizontal leg 8m, and hypotenuse 17m)*
* $\tan(A) = \frac{8}{15}$

**Sine and Cosine are known as co-functions**
this means they produce equivalent ratios when evaluated at complementary angles.
* $\therefore \sin(X) = \cos(90-X)$ OR
* if $\sin(A) = \cos(B)$ then $A+B=90^\circ$

**Ex:**
*(Diagram of a right triangle ABC with W lying on the hypotenuse AB, and a line segment CW from vertex C to W)*
if W lies on $\overline{AB}$, find:
* [ $\cos(\angle ACW) - \sin(\angle BCW) = 0$ ]

### Page 22
**Cross-Text Analysis**  

here, you are given two texts that are somehow related to each other.  

* **in reading text one, simply perform the transitive foreshadow analysis that I taught on Reading Day 1.**  
* **in reading the second text, you must identify its relationship to the first passage.**  
  * [Checked] it is a disagreement by providing a differing viewpoint  
  * [Checked] it is a disagreement by an attack of the claims in the first text.  
  * [Checked] it is a neutral discussion of a topic by providing a different perspective.  
  * [Checked] they agree by providing further support.  

* **After finishing text 2, please write down how author two would respond to author 1,**  
  * ensuring to have evidence from the second text to support  
  * your written claims.  

---

### Page 25
**Text Structuring:**  

Here, you are focusing on how the text is structured (put together)  

and sometimes asked how that functions to accomplish a  

task.  

**Question Type I:** how is the text structured - in this question,  
* determine the function of each sentence. (write these functions down)  

**Question Type II:** what is the purpose of ________?  
* here, your foreshadows should guide the understanding of what the author is trying to do.

### Page 30
**Proportions:**  

a proportion is when two ratios are equivalent under  

different circumstances.  

$$\frac{\text{Quantity 1}}{\text{Quantity 2}} \text{ under } \begin{array}{|c|} \hline \text{Ratio 1} \\ \hline \text{Circumstance 1} \end{array} = \begin{array}{|c|} \hline \text{Ratio 2} \\ \hline \text{Circumstance 2} \end{array}$$  

**Ex:** Manasa is throwing a party, inviting her $f$ friends and ordering $p$ slices of pizza to feed them. However, Avaneesh and Abi show up uninvited. Manasa, being the gracious host she is, will order more pizza so as to feed everyone at the same $\begin{array}{|c|} \hline \text{original rate} \\ \hline \text{proportion:} \end{array}$. How many additional slices should she order?  

$$\frac{\text{friends}}{\text{pizza}}$$  

$$\text{Invited: } \begin{array}{|c|} \hline f \\ \hline p \\ \hline \end{array} = \text{Uninvited: } \begin{array}{|c|c|} \hline 2 & f+2 \\ \hline x & x \\ \hline \end{array}$$  

* **Solving directly:**  
  $$fx = 2p$$  
  $$\begin{array}{|c|} \hline x = \frac{2p}{f} \\ \hline \end{array}$$  

* **Solving with proportions:**  
  $$\frac{f}{p} = \frac{f+2}{x}$$  
  $$fx = p(f+2)$$  
  $$x = \frac{p(f+2)}{f}$$  
  $$\frac{p(f+2)}{f} - \frac{pf}{f}$$  
  $$= \frac{pf + 2p - pf}{f}$$  
  $$= \frac{2p}{f}$$  

---

* Math scratchwork:  
  * $$\frac{\text{miles}}{\$} = \begin{array}{|c|} \hline \frac{25}{\cancel{100}} \\ \hline \cancel{16}\ 4 \\ \hline \end{array} \text{ (Current)}$$  
  * $$\frac{m}{s} \text{ (Save)}$$  
  * $$4m = 25(5)$$  
  * $$\frac{4}{25}m = 5$$

### Page 32
**Lines and Angles:**  

**Angle Pairs:** this defines relationships between two angles  

#### **Vertical Angle Pairs:** this is a set of two angles that share a common vertex and whose two rays extend in opposite directions.  

* $$\angle DVP \text{ and } \angle AVC \text{ form a vertical angle pair.}$$  
  *(Drawing shows two intersecting lines forming opposite angles: $\angle DVP$ and $\angle AVC$ are opposite/vertical; $\angle DVC$ and $\angle PVA$ are opposite/vertical).*  
* [Checked] all vertical angle pairs form congruent angles.  
  $$\therefore \angle DVP \cong \angle AVC$$  
* [Checked] ~~all vertical angle pairs form an adjacent~~ Vertical angle pair.  
  $$\therefore \angle DVC \text{ forms a vertical angle pair with } \angle PVA$$  

#### **Complementary Angle Pair:** this is a set of 2 angles whose measures sum to $90^\circ$.  
* Note: there need not be any relationship b/t the angles besides their measures summing to $90^\circ$.  

$$\begin{array}{c|c} \text{Angle} & \text{Complement} \\ \hline 10^\circ & 80^\circ \\ 42^\circ & 48^\circ \\ 1^\circ & 89^\circ \\ \hline x^\circ & \begin{array}{|c|} \hline (90-x)^\circ \\ \hline \end{array} \end{array}$$  

#### **Supplementary Angle Pair:** this is a set of two angles whose measures sum to $180^\circ$. Again, there need not be any relationship b/t the two angles besides their measures summing to $180^\circ$.  

$$\begin{array}{c|c} \text{Angle} & \text{Supplement} \\ \hline x^\circ & (180-x)^\circ \end{array}$$  

#### **Linear Pair:** this is a set of two angles that share a common vertex and one common ray, and whose non-common rays extend in opposite directions.  

* $$\angle APB \text{ and } \angle BPR \text{ form a linear pair.}$$  
  *(Drawing shows adjacent angles $\angle APB = x^\circ$ and $\angle BPR = (180-x)^\circ$ sharing ray $PB$ along line $AR$).*  
* [Checked] all linear pairs are a supplementary pair.  

---

### Page 33
**Angle Pairs formed by a transversal cutting through two parallel lines**  
*(Drawing shows parallel lines $m$ and $n$ cut by a transversal. Angles at the top intersection are labeled: top-left $2$, top-right $1$, bottom-left $3$, bottom-right $4$. Angles at the bottom intersection are labeled: top-left $6$, top-right $5$, bottom-left $7$, bottom-right $8$.)*  

It is our goal to compare two angles, one at each cut.  

#### **Congruent Angle Pairs**  
1. **Corresponding Angle Pairs -** this is a set of two angles that lie in the same relative position in two locations.  
   $$\left[ \begin{array}{c} \angle 1, \angle 5 \\ \angle 2, \angle 6 \\ \angle 3, \angle 7 \\ \angle 4, \angle 8 \end{array} \right]$$  
2. **Alternate Interior Angle Pair -** this is a set of two angles that lie on the interior of the two parallel, but on opposite sides of the transversal.  
   $$\left[ \begin{array}{c} \angle 4, \angle 6 \\ \angle 3, \angle 5 \end{array} \right]$$  
3. **Alternate Exterior Angle Pair -** this is a set of 2 angles that lie on the exterior of the 2 parallel, but on opposite sides of the transversal.  
   $$\left[ \begin{array}{c} \angle 1, \angle 7 \\ \angle 2, \angle 8 \end{array} \right]$$  

#### **Supplementary Angle Pairs**  
1. **Same-Side Interior Angle Pair:** this is a set of two angles that lie on the interior of the two parallel lines, but on the same side of the transversal.  
   $$\left[ \begin{array}{c} \angle 4, \angle 5 \\ \angle 3, \angle 6 \end{array} \right]$$  
2. **Same-side Exterior Angle Pair:** this is a set of two angles that lie on the exterior of the two parallel lines, but on the same side of the transversal.  
   $$\left[ \begin{array}{c} \angle 1, \angle 8 \\ \angle 2, \angle 7 \end{array} \right]$$  
*(At the bottom: transversal drawing showing $\angle 3$ and $\angle 6$ highlighted, with note: $[ \text{angle } 3 ] + [ \text{angle } 6 ] = 180^\circ$)*  

---

### Page 34
**Ex:**  
*(Drawing shows parallel lines $m$ and $n$ intersected by two transversals that form a triangle between them. At the top-left vertex on line $m$, the exterior angle is $130^\circ$, making the interior angle $50^\circ$. At the top-right vertex on line $m$, the interior angle is $10^\circ$. The bottom vertex of the triangle has an interior angle of $120^\circ$ because the interior angles of a triangle sum to $180^\circ$ ($180 - 50 - 10 = 120^\circ$). The angle opposite/vertical to this bottom vertex is $x^\circ$. Thus, the vertical angle $x$ is $180 - 120 = 60$.)*  

$$\begin{array}{|c|} \hline 60^\circ = x^\circ \\ \hline \end{array}$$  

*(Second drawing shows three parallel lines intersected by two line segments forming a right-pointing vertex in the middle. The angle on the top parallel line is $120^\circ$. The angle on the bottom parallel line is $130^\circ$. The angle at the middle vertex is labeled $x^\circ$.)*

### Page 22
ANSWER QUESTIONS

step 1: Answer the question yourself - yes, I want you to write [underlined in red] your own answer to the question. Your response should be generated using evidence or context from the actual text.

step 2: begin to proceed through the answer choices with the goal of eliminating at least two answer choices, called dummy Answer choices. What makes an answer choice dummy?
[checked] it provides a viewpoint that is not inherently discussed in the text.
[checked] it provides a viewpoint that opposes the view of the author
[checked] the response is significantly different than what you expressed
[checked] the response is too extremely harsh.
[checked] the response is too absolute in nature
Only
always
never
•
•
•

step 3: if two choices remain, then find the wrong answer
b/c every wrong answer has something wrong with it, even if it is one word wrong.

let us assume that you identify A-C as dummy responses.
This does not ensure that D is correct blindly.

### Page 23
Evidence Analysis:

You are effectively tasked to provide evidence from either the text or some graphic to support some claim.

Step 1: highlight/underline the actual claim that you wish to find evidence for. This claim can be within the text or the question header.

Step 2: effectively writing your own response.
- ask yourself what evidence you would like to see that would support this claim.
- then find this evidence in the text or the graphic.

Step 3: proceed through answer choices.

Birds of many species ingest foods containing carotenoids, pigmented molecules that are converted into feather coloration [underlined]. Coloration tends to be especially saturated in male birds’ feathers, and [circled] because carotenoids also confer health benefits, the deeply saturated colors generally serve to communicate what is known as an honest signal of a bird’s overall fitness to potential mates [underlined]. However [circled], ornithologist Allison J. Shultz and others have found that males in several species of the tanager genus Ramphocelus use microstructures in their feathers to manipulate light, creating the appearance of deeper saturation without the birds necessarily having to maintain a carotenoid-rich diet. These findings suggest that ______

Which choice most logically completes the text?
A. individual male tanagers can engage in honest signaling without relying on carotenoid consumption. [wavy line]
B. [crossed out with line] feather microstructures may be less effective than deeply saturated feathers for signaling overall fitness.
C. [crossed out with line] scientists have yet to determine why tanagers have a preference for mates with colorful appearances.
D. [circled] a male tanager’s appearance may function as a dishonest signal of the individual’s overall fitness.

### Page 30
Factor of Change

if given how certain quantities change by a certain percent or by a certain factor AND [underlined] asked how a related quantity will thus change, then you can apply the factor of change approach

Step 1: start with a formula (given or known) [ A = \pi r^2 ]
Step 2: Eliminate all constants             [ A ~ r^2 ]
Step 3: replace [underlined] each variable with its factor of change [ A ~ (1.1)^2 ]
Step 4: solve the unknown variable          [ A ~ 1.21 ]
Step 5: find how far from 1 and convert to a percent

Note: if larger than 1, an increase by the %
      if smaller than 1, a decrease by the %,

Ex: If an object's mass is decreased by 20% and its speed increased by 20%, then by what % [written above what] does its kinetic energy change? Note: k = 1/2 mv^2

k = 1/2 mv^2
k ~ mv^2
k ~ (0.8)(1.2)^2
k ~ 1.152

∴ kinetic energy is increased by 15.2%

A = \frac{\pi r^3 c^2}{2 \sqrt{d}} | if c is held constant, then
                    | by what % will A change if
                    |
                    | r is inc. by 25% and d is
                    |
                    | inc. by 10%.

---

### Page 31
Ratios:

a ratio is a comparison of two or more quantities by their lowest [with arrow] corresponding parts -> this means, ratios are always simplified.

This creates a problem -> ratios do not give exact values

Ex: if the ratio of boys to girls is 2:3, then:
a) how many boys are there? IDK
b) how many girls are there? IDK
c) how many total people? IDK
d) How many more girls than boys? IDK.

the solution to this problem: each part must be a multiple (m) of its corresponding value.

Ex: if the ratio of boys to girls is 2:3, then:
a) how many boys are there? IDK [crossed out] -> 2m
b) how many girls are there? IDK [crossed out] -> 3m
c) how many total people? IDK [crossed out] -> 5m
d) How many more girls than boys? IDK. [crossed out] -> m

Ex: If a rectangle's width and length are in a 5:2 ratio [underlined] and it has an area of 200 [boxed], then what is its perimeter?

W = 5m
L = 2m [outside box]

[inside box]:
A = 10m^2     ∴ 10m^2 = 200
P = 14m          m^2 = 20
                  m = 2\sqrt{5}

[boxed]: ∴ P = 28\sqrt{5}

---

### Page 32
Rates

a rate is a special ratio that defines some sort of quantity vs some amount of consumption.

                 v       value     v
Formula: Quantity = Rate (Consumption Q)

35 mph -> M = (35)(H)
$3/gal -> C = (3)(g)

a special rate is called speed, which compares the quantity of distance to the consumption of time.
∴ D = (Speed)(T)

[boxed]: average speed (\bar{s}) = \frac{\text{total distance}}{\text{total time}}

Note: if a problem involves two or more speeds, then create the following chart:

| | Trip 1 at speed 1 | Trip 2 at speed 2 |
|---|---|---|
| D | | | [row D and s bracketed together: "I will allow a variable for one of these"]
| s | | |
| T | | | [pointing to row T: "T = D/s"]

Ex: If Ashish drives to AHA at an avg. speed of 40mph [underlined] and returns home, along the same route, at an avg. speed of 60mph [underlined], then what is his avg. speed for the whole trip?

| | Trip 1 (to AHA at 40mph) | Trip 2 (to home at 60mph) |
|---|---|---|
| D | x | x |
| s | 40 | 60 |
| T | \frac{x}{40} | \frac{x}{60} |

\bar{s} = \frac{\text{total } d}{\text{total } t} = \frac{x + x}{\frac{x}{40} + \frac{x}{60}} = \frac{2x \cdot 120}{\frac{x}{40} \cdot 120 + \frac{x}{60} \cdot 120} = \frac{240x}{3x + 2x} = \frac{240x}{5x} [x's crossed out]

[boxed]: = 48mph

### Page 2
Verb Tenses

| | Definition | Intermediate Helping Verb | Special Note |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Simple Present** | this refers to action that is occurring in the moment now without any reference to any other time period. | | Multiple actions, though not all in the present, can be grouped into a single one time present action.<br><br>Ex: Manasa is attending SAT class today. She will also attend tomorrow and for the next six weeks.<br>↓<br>Manasa is attending SAT class for the next six weeks. |
| **Simple Past** | this refers to a single one time past action without reference to any other time period. | | Multiple one time past actions can be grouped into a single one time past action.<br><br>Ex: Michael Jordan won the NBA championship in the '93-'94 season and won again in the '94-'95 season.<br>↓<br>Michael Jordan won two NBA championships, in the '93-'94 season and in the '94-'95 season. |
| **"Future"** | this, I will refer to as action that has not occurred. | will<br>would<br>could<br><br>would have<br>could have | (from will) → Ashish will buy a Porsche (will: H, buy: V)<br>(from would) → Ashish would buy a Porsche if... (would: H, buy: V)<br>(from could) → Ashish could buy a Porsche (could: H, buy: V) |

---

### Page 3
| | Definition | Intermediate Helping Verb | Special Note |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Past Perfect** | this refers to action that started in the past, occurred continuously in the past, and stopped in the past. | had (singular and plural)<br><br>Ex: It had rained for seven consecutive days. (had: H, rained: V)<br><br>Ex: They had dated. | to use either of the perfect cases, there needs to be some word that references continuity in action. This can include phrases such as 'since,' 'until,' 'continuously,' 'consecutive,' etc... |
| **Present Perfect** | this refers to action that started in the past, occurred continuously in the past, and is still happening today. | has (singular)<br>have (plural)<br><br>It has rained since March.<br>They have dated since high school. | Also, if you see a continuity in action words, it can override any other choice of tense. |

*(Note: A purple arrow points from 'continuously' in the Past Perfect Special Note to 'Also, if you see...' in the Present Perfect Special Note.)*

### Page 7
Reading vs Reading
* From the first "Reading": → kiddygarten reading - you simply follow each word but do not make connections between words.
* From the second "Reading": → you read so well that you make connections between words, make connections between sentences and even foreshadow events.

To do this proper reading approach, you MUST annotate the passage (SAT only); however, I do not care what YOU think is important.

Step 1: highlight or circle any and all transitional words/phrases and any and all transitional punctuation.

| Transitional Words/Phrases | Transitional Punctuation |
|---|---|
| * but<br>and (if it acts as a coordinating conjunction)<br>however, furthermore, for example, although, ...<br>yet, if, in short, though<br>moreover, including, nevertheless, even still | :<br>;<br>—<br>...<br>() |

Step 2: focus on fully understanding the part after the transition — use it to foreshadow the events of the passage. Write down your foreshadow.

Step 3: as you continue to read (every single word), confirm or deny your foreshadow. If confirmed, keep reading. If denied, stop and start over.

### Page 10
MATH
DAY # 5

TOPICS OF THE DAY
* Percents

---

### Page 11
What is this standard that I talked about?
the standard is always the second item listed in a comparison.

Percents are always multiplied by the original value, the whole value, or the standard value.

the only time %'s should be added is if the %'s represent different categories of a whole total.

Ex: A bag of Skittles contains 25% red, 30% green, 10% orange, 15% purple, and actually 6 yellow skittles. How many red skittles are in the bag?

| Color | % | Amount |
|---|---|---|
| R | 25 | ? |
| O | 10 | |
| G | 30 | |
| P | 15 | |
| y | 20 | 6 |
| T | 100 | 30 |

Calculation on right:
25% / ? = 20% / 6
150 = 20?
? = 7.5

Or:
0.20(T) = 6
T = 30
R = 0.25(30)
= 7.5

Ex: After receiving a 20% discount [80% -> 0.80] and paying 10% sales tax [110% -> 1.10], Viraj buys the entire store for S dollars. What was the price of the store before any discounts and taxes?

effectively, we wish to know the original price of the store? (x)
x (0.80)(1.10) = S
x (0.88) = S
x = S / 0.88 * 100 / 100
x = 100S / 88 = 25S / 22

---

### Page 12
finding percents
there are effectively two types of %'s to find.

1. Basic %
% = part / whole * 100 OR % = part / standard * 100

2. % of difference or % of change
% change = (New - Old) / Old * 100 OR % change = (New - standard) / standard * 100

Ex: Aashni is 16 years old while Ashish is 42 years old.

Q1: Aashni is what % younger than Ashish?
% younger = (Aa - As) / As * 100   (with "diff %" written above Aa - As)
= (16 - 42) / 42 * 100
= 61.9%

Q2: Aashni's age is what % of Ashish's?
% = Aa / As * 100
= 16 / 42 * 100
= 38.1%

Q3: Ashish's age is what % of Aashni's?
% = As / Aa * 100
= 42 / 16 * 100
= 262.5%

Q4: Ashish is what % older than Aashni?
% older = (As - Aa) / Aa * 100
= (42 - 16) / 16 * 100
= 162.5%

Ex: if I am 25% older than Bob, then Bob is what % younger than me?
As = 1.25x
Bob = x

% younger = (Bob - Ashish) / Ashish * 100
= (x - 1.25x) / 1.25x * 100
= -0.25x / 1.25x * 100
= 1/5 * 100 = 20%

Ex: if I am 25% older than you today, what % older am I in 10 years?

| | Today | In a decade |
|---|---|---|
| Me | 1.25x | 1.25x + 10 |
| you | x | x + 10 |

% older = ((1.25x + 10) - (x + 10)) / (x + 10) * 100
= 0.25x / (x + 10) * 100

---

### Page 13
= 25x / (x + 10)

I have 20% less $ than you. If I stole $50 from Matias, then what % is my $ in terms of yours?

| | Currently: | After Robbery |
|---|---|---|
| A: | 0.8x | 0.8x + 50 |
| M: | x | x - 50 |

% = (0.8x + 50) / (x - 50) * 100
= (80x + 5000) / (x - 50)

Mixture Problems:

| | Acid | Total |
|---|---|---|
| Given Mix 1 at x% of acid | m (x/100) | m |
| Given Mix 2 at y% of acid | (50 - m)(y/100) | 50 - m |
| Produce a Mix at z% of acid | 50 (z/100) | 50 |

*(Note: "fill first" is written next to the Total column, and there are arrows indicating additions.)*
Note: x < z < y
Eqn: m (x/100) + (50 - m)(y/100) = 50 (z/100)

Ex: How many liters of a 25% acidic solution must be added to 40L of a 40% acidic solution to make a solution that is 30% acidic?

| | A | T |
|---|---|---|
| Mix 1 at 25% acidic | 0.25x | x |
| Mix 2 at 40% acidic | 0.40(40) | 40 |
| Make a 30% acidic | 0.30(x + 40) | x + 40 |

Eqn: 0.25x + 0.40(40) = 0.30(x + 40)
0.25x + 16 = 0.30x + 12
4 = 0.05x
x = 80L

### Page 14
Question 5
a = -2, b = 8, c = (-c/a)
d = -2, e = 8, f = -c/a

-2x^2 + 8x - c/a = 0
dx^2 + ex + f = 0
P_R = f/d (Product of solutions is 5/7)

((c/a) / 2) = 5/7
c / 2a = 5/7
c = 10a / 7
a = 0.7c

b = 0.36c
p = ((c - a) / c) * 100
= ((c - 0.7c) / c) * 100
= (0.3c / c) * 100
= 30

--- Page 2 ---

TRANSITIONS

TRUE RELATIONSHIP | COMMON TRANSITIONS | EXAMPLE
present an opposing point of view or balance an idea that is either too positive or too negative. S1. claim, S2. another claim that either opposes or balances | however, fortunately, on the other hand, conversely, whereas, while, in contrast, on the contrary, yet. | While Final Fantasy is often considered the greatest video game franchise, there are many that deem the franchise boring. (Annotations: claim above "Final Fantasy... franchise", balances pointing to "there are many... boring.")
to give emphasis to a claim by adding a specific piece of evidence (1st piece). S1. claim, S2. a piece of evidence to support (1st piece of support) | In fact, as a matter of fact, indeed, to illustrate, for instance, for example. | Biology is an amazing scientific subject. Indeed, there are many hands-on applications such as in the field of forensic sciences. (Annotations: claim above "Biology is...", evidence pointing to "Indeed, there are many...", 1st above "hands-on")
to add new and supporting evidence (2nd+ evidence). S3. a piece of evidence to support (2nd+ piece of support) | furthermore, in addition, also, moreover, and, too, as well, additionally, not to mention | Furthermore, Biology blends the best applications of many other disciplines of study.
to show a cause and effect relationship. S1. cause, S2. effect | Consequently, as a result, because, hence, therefore, thus, accordingly, so, for this reason. | "The Backrooms" is a movie devoid of any logic. Therefore, the movie is horrible.

--- Page 3 ---

TRUE RELATIONSHIP | COMMON TRANSITIONS | EXAMPLE
to concede a prior claim so as to give space to provide a claim that is more important. S1. claim, S2. another claim as if it is useless. | Granted, nevertheless, although, regardless, despite, even if, nonetheless, still, even so | Although Matias has no desire to study, Ashish plans to lock him in a cage with so many factoring exercises that he has no option but to complete.
to show order or sequencing of events that either move forwards or backwards. S1. an event or action, S2. an event or action that logically moves forwards or backwards | finally, subsequently, previously, afterwards, next, then, eventually, before | Divyang skipped his SAT class to play basketball. Next, he walked inside to see that his entire wardrobe had been stolen by his infamous SAT instructor.
to show similarity with some constant theme. S1. claim, S2. another claim that has some similarity (some constant theme) to | In the same way, similarly, likewise, by the same token | Goldfish is the best snack, especially when listening to Ashish's boring lecture. Likewise, popcorn is the best for when watching an exciting movie such as "The Backrooms."
to provide a conclusion or a restatement of an idea. S1. an idea, S2. a summary or restatement of that idea | In conclusion, in summary, to sum up, in short, in other words | (No example provided)

--- Page 4 ---

Note:
transitions can be expressed in two major ways
1. Transition S1, S2 (Annotation: ICC above "Transition")
2. S1. Transition, S2

Process
Step 1: read either the ICC or the sentence before the transition and determine its function.
Step 2: read the sentence including the transition and identify its relationship to the prior.
Step 3: identify an appropriate transition
Step 4: do not force

Which choice completes the text with the most logical transition?

MATH DAY # 4

TOPICS OF THE DAY
• Quadratics, Part II

--- Page 8 ---

Roots of a Quadratic

a root is a value of x that makes the y value of the quadratic equal to 0.

Root = Solution = Zeros

- Real (x-intercepts, b^2 - 4ac >= 0)
  - Rational + Repeated (b^2 - 4ac = 0)
    - Note: the root in this case is the same as x_v
  - Distinct (b^2 - 4ac > 0)
    - Rational (b^2 - 4ac = perfect square)
    - Irrational (b^2 - 4ac = non-perfect square)
      - x_1 = a + \sqrt{b}
      - x_cp = a - \sqrt{b}
- Complex (Not x-intercepts, b^2 - 4ac < 0)
  - x_1 = a + bi
  - x_cp = a - bi

Note: if a parabola does not cross the x axis, nor touch the x axis, then the quadratic has complex roots
Note: from standard form (y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c), there exists a value called the discriminant (b^2 - 4ac) that helps to classify the roots
In both of these situations, the two roots of the quadratic come in conjugate pairs

Ex: 3x^2 - 4x + p = 0
In the above quadratic equation, where p is a constant, find the value of p such that the quadratic has no real solution.
∴ both roots (both solutions) are complex
∴ b^2 - 4ac < 0

(-4)^2 - 4(3)(p) < 0
16 - 12p < 0
-12p < -16
p > 16/12
∴ p > 4/3

--- Page 9 ---

finding the roots:
it is possible to find the roots of a quadratic in either vertex form or intercept form.

Intercept form: y = f(x) = a(x - p)(x - q) (Annotation: f(x) has an arrow to 0)
Method: Zero Product Property (zpp)
∴ Since a(x - p)(x - q) = 0
then either (x - p) = 0 or (x - q) = 0
x = p or x = q.

Ex: if -3(x - 2)(x + 7) = 0
then which of the following must be correct.
I. x = -3
II. x = 2
III. x = -7
None.

Vertex form: y = f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k (Annotation: f(x) has an arrow to 0)
Method: Square Root Method

Derivation (Left Column):
0 = a(x - h)^2 + k
-k = a(x - h)^2
-k/a = (x - h)^2
(x - h) = ± \sqrt{-k}/\sqrt{a} * \sqrt{a}/\sqrt{a}
(x - h) = ± \sqrt{-ak}/a
x = h ± \sqrt{-ak}/a
x = (ah ± \sqrt{-ak})/a

Example (Right Column):
Ex: y = f(x) = 2(x + 7)^2 + 15 (Annotation: f(x) has an arrow to 0)
find all roots.
0 = 2(x + 7)^2 + 15
-15 = 2(x + 7)^2
-15/2 = (x + 7)^2
(x + 7) = ± \sqrt{-15}/\sqrt{2} * \sqrt{2}/\sqrt{2}
(x + 7) = ± i\sqrt{30}/2
x = -7 ± i\sqrt{30}/2
x = (-14 ± i\sqrt{30})/2

--- Page 10 ---

Standard form: y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c
Technically, there are no ways to find the roots of a quadratic in standard form.

ax^2 + bx + c = 0
--> FACTOR --> Intercept form -- zpp --> Roots
--> CTS --> Vertex -- Square Root Method --> Roots

Quadratic Formula
y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c (Annotation: f(x) has an arrow to 0)
0 = ax^2 + bx + c
0 = (ax^2 + bx) + c
0 = a(x^2 + b/a x) + c
0 = a(x^2 + b/a x + b^2 / (4a^2)) + c - a(b^2 / (4a^2))
0 = a(x + b/(2a))^2 + c - b^2 / (4a)

a(x + b/(2a))^2 = b^2 / (4a) - c
a(x + b/(2a))^2 = (b^2 - 4ac) / (4a)
(x + b/(2a))^2 = (b^2 - 4ac) / (4a^2)
x + b/(2a) = ± \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac} / (2a)
x = (-b ± \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}) / (2a)   QF

--- Page 11 ---

15
x^2 - k/2 x = 2p
In the quadratic equation above, k and p are constants. What are the solutions for x?
A) x = k/4 ± \sqrt{k^2 + 2p}/4
B) x = k/4 ± \sqrt{k^2 + 32p}/4 (Selected)
C) x = k/2 ± \sqrt{k^2 + 2p}/2
D) x = k/2 ± \sqrt{k^2 + 32p}/4

Handwritten work:
(x^2 - k/2 x - 2p = 0)(2)
2x^2 - kx - 4p = 0
a = 2, b = -k, c = -4p
x = (-b ± \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}) / (2a)
x = (k ± \sqrt{k^2 - 4(2)(-4p)}) / 4
x = (k ± \sqrt{k^2 + 32p}) / 4 = k/4 ± \sqrt{k^2 + 32p}/4

Below:
Solve the roots of 4x^2 + k/3 x = 2/5

Sum of the roots
S_R = -b/a

Product of the roots
P_R = c/a

--- Page 12 ---

5. The product of two positive numbers is 385. The second number is 13 greater than twice the first number. What is the value of one of the numbers?

Handwritten work:
xy = 385
x(13 + 2x) = 385
2x^2 + 13x = 385
2x^2 + 13x - 385 = 0
a = 2, b = 13, c = -385
x = (-13 ± \sqrt{169 - 4(2)(-385)}) / 4 = (-13 ± 57) / 4
- (-13 + 57) / 4 = 11 (Selected)
- (-13 - 57) / 4 = -70/4 (Crossed out)

y = 13 + 2x
y = 13 + 22 = 35

--- Page 13 ---

Percents:

Note: a percent must be converted to a factor to be used.
to convert a % to a factor, either divide the % by 100 or move the decimal place two units to the left.

% | factor
20% | 20/100 = 1/5 or 0.20
0.5% | 0.5/100 = 1/200 or 0.005
x% | x/100 or 0.01(x)

When given a % in the problem, you need to know which version of the % to use:
Version 1: the % is of some total or of some standard
use the % as is and convert to a factor

Version 2: the % is of some increase to some total or some standard
use (100 + %) and then convert to some factor

Version 3: the % is of some decrease to some total or some standard
use (100 - %) and then convert to a factor.

# Page 2

**Punctuation**

**Commas**

* **Use 1: to separate the items of a list**
  * `... item1, item2, item3, and item 4...`  
    * (Arrow pointing to the comma before "and", labeled "wrong" in red)
  * **[Oxford Comma Note]:** *Oxford Comma: the oxford comma is not mandatory in writing. As such, you will not be tested on its use. However, you will be tested on its location - it is located before the "and".*
  * **Note:** The items of a list can also be separated by the use of a semi-colon (`;`). You do so if the items of the list themselves contain commas.
  * **Ex:** Ruhi plans to read `Title 1, description 1` [item 1]; `Title 2, description 2` [item 2]; and `Title 3, description 3` [item 3].
    * (Note pointing to `; and` reads: *this is the only exception where a semi-colon can be joined with "and"*)

* **Use 2: to join two full sentences with the use of a coordinating conjunction.**
  * ∴ two expressed subjects, two expressed verbs, two full ideas
  * **form:** sentence 1 `, (cc)` sentence 2.
  * Where your coordinating conjunctions (cc) are your **FANBOYS**:
    * **F** - for, **B** - but
    * **A** - and, **O** - or, **S** - so
    * **N** - nor, **Y** - yet
  * **Ex:** 
    * `[✓]` Ashish plans to buy a new car and will have it shipped to him.
    * `[✗]` Ashish plans to buy a new car, and he will have it shipped to him.
      * (Under "Ashish plans", labeled "S1 V1"; under "he will have", labeled "S2 V2")

---

# Page 3

* **Correctly Joining 2 Independent Clauses**
  1. IC. IC
  2. IC; IC
  3. IC, cc IC
  4. IC: IC

* **Incorrectly Join 2 Independent Clauses**
  1. IC IC (no punctuation) (run-on sentence)
  2. IC, IC (no coordinating conjunction) (comma splice)
  3. IC; cc IC
  4. IC, false cc IC

* **Most common false cc is "however"**
  * `[✗]` Tom is dating Emily, however he is attracted to Julie.
    * (Labeled: "Sentence 1", "cc = false" over "however", "Sentence 2")

* **`[✓]` Correctly using "however"**
  1. IC. However, IC. (Ex: Tom is dating Emily. However, he is attracted to Julie.)
  2. IC; however, IC
  3. IC. IC, however. (Ex: Tom is dating Emily. He is attracted to Julie, however.)
  4. IC; IC, however.
  5. IC. IC_1/2, however, IC_2/2 (Ex: Tom is dating Emily. He, however, is attracted to Julie.)
  6. IC; IC_1/2, however, IC_2/2

* **Careful:** *the SAT often tests which sentence the "however" is part of - it needs to be a part of the sentence performing the opposition.*

* **Use 3: to join a dependent clause with an independent clause; however, the use of the comma depends on the location of the dependent clause.**
  * **If** the dependent clause comes before the independent clause, then you **MUST** use a comma.
  * **If** the dependent clause comes after the independent clause, then you **MIGHT** use a comma. You do so if the dependent clause begins with an adverb of concession.
  * **Your concessional adverbs:**

---

# Page 4

* `supposing it rains, the party will be cancelled.`
* `The party will be cancelled supposing it rains.`
* `Even though I have no water, Vishesh keeps taking [my water bottle]`
* `Vishesh keeps taking my water bottle, even though I have no water.`

* **USE 4: before and after a middle comma clause (also known as an appositive or non-essential clause)**
  * **Note:** a middle comma clause can actually be punctuated in 3 ways:
    1. $IC_{1/2}$, mcc, $IC_{2/2}$
    2. $IC_{1/2}$ - mcc - $IC_{2/2}$
    3. $IC_{1/2}$ ( mcc ) $IC_{2/2}$
    * *no mixing punctuation*
  * **Note: the SAT/ACT Trap**
    * subject, ~~~~~~~~~~~~~
    * ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
    * ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
    * ~~~~~~~~~~~~ — verb
    * ~~~~~~~~~~~~.

* **USE 5: to enter an ending comma clause (ecc)**
  * **Recall:** most ending comma clauses begin with an -ing adverb.
  * **Recall:** ending comma clauses are usually defining or clarifying phrases.
  * **Ex:** Vishesh keeps taking the water bottle, hoping that he will find water.

* **Use 6: between two adjectives that describe the same noun.**
  * **Ex:** Ashish is wearing a big, red t-shirt. (Arrows show "big" and "red" can swap)
  * **Ex:** Viraj is wearing a light gray shirt. (Arrow shows "light" modifies "gray")

---

# Page 5

**Colon (:)**

* **Note:** *to use a colon, there MUST be a full sentence before the colon.*
* **USE 1: to enter a list with no introductory phrase to the list.**
  * No "for example"
  * No "such as"
  * No "to illustrate"
  * No "including"
  * No "with"
  * (All grouped under: *No introductory phrase*)
  * **Ex:** Aashni eats a big breakfast: waffles, pancakes, cereal, and fruit.
  * **Note:** *do not ever enter a list with a semicolon.*
  * **Note:** *you can enter a list with no punctuation.*
    * **Ex:** Aashni eats waffles, pancakes, cereal, and fruit every morning.
  * **Note:** *you can use an introductory phrase as long as you don't enter the list with a colon.*
    * **Ex:** Aashni eats a big breakfast including pancakes, cereal, waffles, and fruit.
* **USE 2: to add information at the end of a sentence about the sentence.**
  * **Ex:** Ashish studied eight long years for the SAT: he scored a perfect 1600.

---

**DASH (—)**

* **USE 1: before and after a MCC**
* **USE 2: to add information at the end of a sentence about the sentence; however, this information is usually shocking.**
  * **Ex:** Ashish studied eight long years for the SAT — he scored a 420.

---

# Page 6

**Semi-colon (;)**

* **USE 1:** to separate the items of a list when the items themselves contain commas.
* **USE 2:** to join two full sentences without the use of a cc.

---

**Apostrophe (')**

* **USE 1: to make a contraction, the joining of two words into one.**
  * CONTRACTIONS (crossed out)
  * The only acceptable contraction is: `"it is" = "it's"`
* **USE 2: to show possession**
  * **Note:** there are non-apostrophe possessives:
    * its, their, his, her, hers, my, mine, our, ours
  * (The words `its'`, `its's`, `itss` are grouped inside a red cloud labeled: *Not words*)

```
               [to show possessive with an apostrophe]
                               │
               ┌───────────────┴───────────────┐
               ▼                               ▼
     if the word does not                 if the word
          end in -s                        ends in s
               │                               │
               ▼                               ▼
              's                        ┌──────┴──────┐
                                        ▼             ▼
                                   simple noun   proper noun
                                        s'            s's
                                     (cars')       (Jesus's)
                                     (car's)
```

# Page 7

**MATH**  
**DAY #** 2 (handwritten)

**TOPICS OF THE DAY**
* Quadratics (handwritten)
* 
* 

---

# Page 8

**Quadratics**

* A quadratic is a relationship between $y$ and the square of $x$ ($x^2$).
* If the quadratic passes through the origin, then $y \propto x^2$.
  * ∴ if $x$ is doubled, $y$ is quadrupled (scaled by a factor of 4)
  * If $x$ is tripled, $y$ is scaled by a factor of 9
  * If $y$ is doubled, $x$ is scaled by $\sqrt{2}$
* All quadratics produce a curve called a **parabola**, a U-shaped curve that opens either up or down.
  * **opens up:** $(a > 0)$
  * **opens down:** $(a < 0)$
* All parabolas have two characteristics:
  * a vertex **AND**
  * an axis of symmetry
* **Vertex:** this is a coordinate point $(x_v, y_v)$ that corresponds to either the maximum point of the parabola (if the parabola opens down) or the minimum point of the parabola (if the parabola opens up).
  * (Diagrams show: minimum point for opens up, maximum point for opens down)
* **Note:** *for any question involving a quadratic that refers to either maximum or minimum, simply find the vertex!!*

---

# Page 9

* **Axis of Symmetry:** this is an imaginary vertical line that passes through the parabola's vertex, dividing the parabola into two equal halves.
* All quadratics can be expressed in one of three forms:
  1. **Standard form:** $y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$
     * (Points to "y-intercept: c")
  2. **Vertex form:** $y = f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k$ (labeled opp under $h$, norm under $k$)
     * (Points to "vertex: (h, k)")
  3. **Intercept form:** $y = f(x) = a(x - p)(x - q)$ (labeled opp under $p$, opp under $q$)
     * (Points to "x-intercepts: (p, 0), (q, 0)")
  * **SAT Cloud Note:** *each form gives, as a constant or a coefficient, the _______________ of the parabola.*

* **Finding the vertex:**
  * **Vertex form:** $y = f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k$
    * $x_v = h$
    * $y_v = k$
    * $V(h, k)$
  * **Standard form:** $y = f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$
    * $x_v = -\frac{b}{2a}$
    * $y_v = f\left(-\frac{b}{2a}\right)$
    * $V\left(-\frac{b}{2a}, f\left(-\frac{b}{2a}\right)\right)$
  * **Intercept form:** $y = f(x) = a(x - p)(x - q)$
    * $x_v = \frac{p + q}{2}$
    * $y_v = f\left(\frac{p + q}{2}\right)$
    * $V\left(\frac{p+q}{2}, f\left(\frac{p+q}{2}\right)\right)$

* **Finding the Axis of Symmetry:**
  * $x = x_v$

* **Ex:** $y = f(x) = -2(x + 3)(x - 7)$
  * $x_v = \frac{-3 + 7}{2} = 2$
  * $y_v = f(2) = -2(5)(-5) = 50$
  * $V: (2, 50)$

---

# Page 10

### CONVERSION TOOLBOX

| | Standard (To) | Vertex (To) | Intercept (To) |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Standard (From)** | *[Red Shaded / N/A]* | **Complete the square**<br>• **if a = 1:**<br>$y = x^2 + 12x - 8$<br>$y = (x^2 + 12x) - 8$<br>$y = 1(x^2 + 12x) - 8$<br>$y = 1(x^2 + 12x + 36) - 8 - 1(36)$ (where $\text{blank} = (\frac{1}{2} \times \text{coeff})^2$)<br>$y = 1(x + 6)^2 - 44$<br>$y = (x + 6)^2 - 44$<br><br>• **if a ≠ 1:**<br>$y = 3x^2 + 10x - 8$<br>$y = (3x^2 + 10x) - 8$<br>$y = 3(x^2 + \frac{10}{3}x) - 8$<br>$y = 3(x^2 + \frac{10}{3}x + \frac{25}{9}) - 8 - 3(\frac{25}{9})$<br>$y = 3(x + \frac{5}{3})^2 - 8 - \frac{25}{3}$<br>$y = 3(x + \frac{5}{3})^2 - \frac{49}{3}$<br><br>$y = -x^2 + 5x - 2$<br>$y = -(x^2 - 5x) - 2$<br>$y = -1(x^2 - 5x) - 2$<br>$y = -1(x^2 - 5x + \frac{25}{4}) - 2 + 1(\frac{25}{4})$<br>$y = -(x - \frac{5}{2})^2 + \frac{17}{4}$<br><br>**Ex:** $y = 3x^2 - 6x + 1$ | **Factor, if possible**<br>(If $b^2 - 4ac$ = discriminant is either 0 or a perfect square, then the quadratic is factorable)<br>**Ex:** $6x^2 + 7x - 49$<br>$(2x + 7)(3x - 7)$<br>$= 6(x + \frac{7}{2})(x - \frac{7}{3})$ |
| **Vertex (From)** | **FOIL**<br>$y = 3(x + 4)^2 - 8$<br>$y = 3(x^2 + 8x + 16) - 8$<br>$y = 3x^2 + 24x + 48 - 8$<br>$y = 3x^2 + 24x + 40$ | *[Red Shaded / N/A]* | $\text{Vertex} \rightarrow \text{FOIL} \rightarrow \text{Standard} \rightarrow \text{Factor} \rightarrow \text{Intercept}$ |
| **Intercept (From)** | **FOIL**<br>$y = 2(x + 1)(x - \frac{3}{2})$<br>$y = 2(x^2 - \frac{1}{2}x - \frac{3}{2})$<br>$y = 2x^2 - x - 3$ | $\text{Intercept} \rightarrow \text{FOIL} \rightarrow \text{Standard} \rightarrow \text{CTS} \rightarrow \text{Vertex}$ | *[Red Shaded / N/A]* |

# Page 11

**Question 6**  
A quadratic function models the height [DV (y)], in feet, of an object above the ground in terms of the time [IV (x)], in seconds, after the object is launched off an elevated surface. The model indicates the object hits the ground 16 seconds after being launched [pointing to $(16, 0)$]; the object reaches its maximum height of 1,296 feet above the ground 7 seconds after being launched [pointing to Vertex $(7, 1296)$]. Based on the model, at what time after the object is launched, in seconds, does the object first reach a height of 972 feet? [972 feet points to $y$, "first" is circled]

**Handwritten Solution:**
* $y = a(x - 7)^2 + 1296$ (to find a)
* $0 = a(9)^2 + 1296$
* $-1296 = 81a$
* $a = -16$
* $y = -16(x - 7)^2 + 1296$ (boxed)
* $972 = -16(x - 7)^2 + 1296$
* $-324 = -16(x - 7)^2$
* $(x - 7)^2 = 20.25$
* $x - 7 = \pm 4.5$
* $x = 7 \pm 4.5$
* $x = 2.5, 11.5$ (2.5 is circled)

**Shlok Bhatt**: Hello everyone,

ATL Cyber Clash (CTF) will take place on April 18, Saturday, from 10:30 AM to 2:00 PM at Sharon Forks Library. I’m co-hosting this event in collaboration with the LHS Cybersecurity Club and Alpharetta High School.

This is a Capture the Flag cybersecurity competition where you will work in teams of two to solve challenges in areas like web exploitation, cryptography, and forensics. The competition is designed to be beginner-friendly, so no prior experience is required.

The event is open to all high school students, and there will be cash prizes for top teams. Registration is open until April 17.

You can sign up here: https://luma.com/vqckv6aj

Join us—whether you’re new to cybersecurity or already experienced, this is a great chance to learn, challenge yourself, and compete!!
